Alcott\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPreface\r\n\r\nThe Author is quite aware of the defects of this little story, many of\r\nwhich were unavoidable, as it first appeared serially. But, as Uncle\r\nAlec's experiment was intended to amuse the young folks, rather than\r\nsuggest educational improvements for the consideration of the elders,\r\nshe trusts that these shortcomings will be overlooked by the friends of\r\nthe Eight Cousins, and she will try to make amends in a second volume,\r\nwhich shall attempt to show The Rose in Bloom.\r\n\r\nL.M.A.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 1--Two Girls\r\n\r\nRose sat all alone in the big best parlor, with her little handkerchief\r\nlaid ready to catch the first tear, for she was thinking of her\r\ntroubles, and a shower was expected. She had retired to this room as a\r\ngood place in which to be miserable; for it was dark and still, full of\r\nancient furniture, sombre curtains, and hung all around with portraits\r\nof solemn old gentlemen in wigs, severe-nosed ladies in top-heavy caps,\r\nand staring children in little bob-tailed coats or short-waisted frocks.\r\nIt was an excellent place for woe; and the fitful spring rain that\r\npattered on the window-pane seemed to sob, \xe2\x80\x9cCry away: I'm with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose really did have some cause to be sad; for she had no mother, and\r\nhad lately lost her father also, which left her no home but this with\r\nher great-aunts. She had been with them only a week, and, though the\r\ndear old ladies had tried their best to make her happy, they had not\r\nsucceeded very well, for she was unlike any child they had ever seen,\r\nand they felt very much as if they had the care of a low-spirited\r\nbutterfly.\r\n\r\nThey had given her the freedom of the house, and for a day or two\r\nshe had amused herself roaming all over it, for it was a capital old\r\nmansion, and was full of all manner of odd nooks, charming rooms, and\r\nmysterious passages. Windows broke out in unexpected places, little\r\nbalconies overhung the garden most romantically, and there was a long\r\nupper hall full of curiosities from all parts of the world; for the\r\nCampbells had been sea-captains for generations.\r\n\r\nAunt Plenty had even allowed Rose to rummage in her great china closet\r\na spicy retreat, rich in all the \xe2\x80\x9cgoodies\xe2\x80\x9d that children love; but Rose\r\nseemed to care little for these toothsome temptations; and when that\r\nhope failed, Aunt Plenty gave up in despair.\r\n\r\nGentle Aunt Peace had tried all sorts of pretty needle-work, and planned\r\na doll's wardrobe that would have won the heart of even an older child.\r\nBut Rose took little interest in pink satin hats and tiny hose, though\r\nshe sewed dutifully till her aunt caught her wiping tears away with the\r\ntrain of a wedding-dress, and that discovery put an end to the sewing\r\nsociety.\r\n\r\nThen both old ladies put their heads together and picked out the model\r\nchild of the neighbourhood to come and play with their niece. But\r\nAriadne Blish was the worst failure of all, for Rose could not bear the\r\nsight of her, and said she was so like a wax doll she longed to give\r\nher a pinch and see if she would squeak. So prim little Ariadne was sent\r\nhome, and the exhausted aunties left Rose to her own devices for a day\r\nor two.\r\n\r\nBad weather and a cold kept her in-doors, and she spent most of her time\r\nin the library where her father's books were stored. Here she read a\r\ngreat deal, cried a little, and dreamed many of the innocent bright\r\ndreams in which imaginative children find such comfort and delight. This\r\nsuited her better than anything else, but it was not good for her, and\r\nshe grew pale, heavy-eyed and listless, though Aunt Plenty gave her iron\r\nenough to make a cooking-stove, and Aunt Peace petted her like a poodle.\r\n\r\nSeeing this, the poor aunties racked their brains for a new amusement\r\nand determined to venture a bold stroke, though not very hopeful of its\r\nsuccess. They said nothing to Rose about their plan for this Saturday\r\nafternoon, but let her alone till the time came for the grand surprise,\r\nlittle dreaming that the odd child would find pleasure for herself in a\r\nmost unexpected quarter.\r\n\r\nBefore she had time to squeeze out a single tear a sound broke the\r\nstillness, making her prick up her ears. It was only the soft twitter\r\nof a bird, but it seemed to be a peculiarly gifted bird, for while she\r\nlistened the soft twitter changed to a lively whistle, then a trill, a\r\ncoo, a chirp, and ended in a musical mixture of all the notes, as if the\r\nbird burst out laughing. Rose laughed also, and, forgetting her woes,\r\njumped up, saying eagerly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a mocking-bird. Where is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRunning down the long hall, she peeped out at both doors, but saw\r\nnothing feathered except a draggle-tailed chicken under a burdock leaf.\r\nShe listened again, and the sound seemed to be in the house. Away she\r\nwent, much excited by the chase, and following the changeful song, it\r\nled her to the china-closet door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn there? How funny!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. But when she entered, not a bird\r\nappeared except the everlastingly kissing swallows on the Canton china\r\nthat lined the shelves. All of a sudden Rose's face brightened, and,\r\nsoftly opening the slide, she peered into the kitchen. But the music\r\nhad stopped, and all she saw was a girl in a blue apron scrubbing the\r\nhearth. Rose stared about her for a minute, and then asked abruptly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you hear that mocking-bird?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should call it a phebe-bird,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the girl, looking up with a\r\ntwinkle in her black eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did it go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is here still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn my throat. Do you want to hear it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes! I'll come in.\xe2\x80\x9d And Rose crept through the slide to the wide\r\nshelf on the other side, being too hurried and puzzled to go round by\r\nthe door.\r\n\r\nThe girl wiped her hands, crossed her feet on the little island of\r\ncarpet where she was stranded in a sea of soap-suds, and then, sure\r\nenough, out of her slender throat came the swallow's twitter, the\r\nrobin's whistle, the blue-jay's call, the thrush's song, the wood-dove's\r\ncoo, and many another familiar note, all ending as before with the\r\nmusical ecstacy of a bobolink singing and swinging among the meadow\r\ngrass on a bright June day.\r\n\r\nRose was so astonished that she nearly fell off her perch, and when the\r\nlittle concert was over clapped her hands delightedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it was lovely! Who taught you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe birds,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the girl, with a smile, as she fell to work again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very wonderful! I can sing, but nothing half so fine as that.\r\nWhat is your name, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPhebe Moore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've heard of phebe-birds; but I don't believe the real ones could do\r\nthat,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Rose, adding, as she watched with interest the scattering\r\nof dabs of soft soap over the bricks, \xe2\x80\x9cMay I stay and see you work? It\r\nis very lonely in the parlor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed, if you want to,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Phebe, wringing out her cloth in\r\na capable sort of way that impressed Rose very much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be fun to swash the water round and dig out the soap. I'd love\r\nto do it, only aunt wouldn't like it, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, quite taken\r\nwith the new employment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou'd soon get tired, so you'd better keep tidy and look on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you help your mother a good deal?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven't got any folks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, where do you live, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm going to live here, I hope. Debby wants some one to help round, and\r\nI've come to try for a week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you will stay, for it is very dull,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, who had taken a\r\nsudden fancy to this girl, who sung like a bird and worked like a woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHope I shall; for I'm fifteen now, and old enough to earn my own\r\nliving. You have come to stay a spell, haven't you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Phebe,\r\nlooking up at her guest and wondering how life could be dull to a girl\r\nwho wore a silk frock, a daintily frilled apron, a pretty locket, and\r\nhad her hair tied up with a velvet snood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I shall stay till my uncle comes. He is my guardian now, and I\r\ndon't know what he will do with me. Have you a guardian?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy sakes, no! I was left on the poor-house steps a little mite of\r\na baby, and Miss Rogers took a liking to me, so I've been there ever\r\nsince. But she is dead now, and I take care of myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow interesting! It is like Arabella Montgomery in the 'Gypsy's Child.'\r\nDid you ever read that sweet story?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, who was fond of tales\r\nof found-lings, and had read many.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't have any books to read, and all the spare time I get I run off\r\ninto the woods; that rests me better than stories,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Phebe, as\r\nshe finished one job and began on another.\r\n\r\nRose watched her as she got out a great pan of beans to look over, and\r\nwondered how it would seem to have life all work and no play. Presently\r\nPhebe seemed to think it was her turn to ask questions, and said,\r\nwistfully,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou've had lots of schooling, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear me, yes! I've been at boarding school nearly a year, and I'm\r\nalmost dead with lessons. The more I got, the more Miss Power gave me,\r\nand I was so miserable that I 'most cried my eyes out. Papa never gave\r\nme hard things to do, and he always taught me so pleasantly I loved to\r\nstudy. Oh, we were so happy and so fond of one another! But now he is\r\ngone, and I am left all alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe tear that would not come when Rose sat waiting for it came now of\r\nits own accord two of them in fact and rolled down her cheeks, telling\r\nthe tale of love and sorrow better than any words could do it.\r\n\r\nFor a minute there was no sound in the kitchen but the little daughter's\r\nsobbing and the sympathetic patter of the rain. Phebe stopped rattling\r\nher beans from one pan to another, and her eyes were full of pity as\r\nthey rested on the curly head bent down on Rose's knee, for she saw that\r\nthe heart under the pretty locket ached with its loss, and the dainty\r\napron was used to dry sadder tears than any she had ever shed.\r\n\r\nSomehow, she felt more contented with her brown calico gown and\r\nblue-checked pinafore; envy changed to compassion; and if she had dared\r\nshe would have gone and hugged her afflicted guest.\r\n\r\nFearing that might not be considered proper, she said, in her cheery\r\nvoice,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm sure you ain't all alone with such a lot of folks belonging to\r\nyou, and all so rich and clever. You'll be petted to pieces, Debby says,\r\nbecause you are the only girl in the family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhebe's last words made Rose smile in spite of her tears, and she looked\r\nout from behind her apron with an April face, saying in a tone of comic\r\ndistress,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's one of my troubles! I've got six aunts, and they all want\r\nme, and I don't know any of them very well. Papa named this place the\r\nAunt-hill, and now I see why.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhebe laughed with her as she said encouragingly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEveryone calls it so, and it's a real good name, for all the Mrs.\r\nCampbells live handy by, and keep coming up to see the old ladies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could stand the aunts, but there are dozens of cousins, dreadful\r\nboys all of them, and I detest boys! Some of them came to see me last\r\nWednesday, but I was lying down, and when auntie came to call me I went\r\nunder the quilt and pretended to be asleep. I shall have to see them\r\nsome time, but I do dread it so.\xe2\x80\x9d And Rose gave a shudder, for, having\r\nlived alone with her invalid father, she knew nothing of boys, and\r\nconsidered them a species of wild animal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I guess you'll like 'em. I've seen 'em flying round when they\r\ncome over from the Point, sometimes in their boats and sometimes\r\non horseback. If you like boats and horses, you'll enjoy yourself\r\nfirst-rate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don't! I'm afraid of horses, and boats make me ill, and I hate\r\nboys!\xe2\x80\x9d And poor Rose wrung her hands at the awful prospect before her.\r\nOne of these horrors alone she could have borne, but all together were\r\ntoo much for her, and she began to think of a speedy return to the\r\ndetested school.\r\n\r\nPhebe laughed at her woe till the beans danced in the pan, but tried to\r\ncomfort her by suggesting a means of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps your uncle will take you away where there ain't any boys. Debby\r\nsays he is a real kind man, and always bring heaps of nice things when\r\nhe comes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but you see that is another trouble, for I don't know Uncle Alec\r\nat all. He hardly ever came to see us, though he sent me pretty things\r\nvery often. Now I belong to him, and shall have to mind him, till I am\r\neighteen. I may not like him a bit, and I fret about it all the time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I wouldn't borrow trouble, but have a real good time. I'm sure I\r\nshould think I was in clover if I had folks and money, and nothing to\r\ndo but enjoy myself,\xe2\x80\x9d began Phebe, but got no further, for a sudden rush\r\nand tumble outside made them both jump.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's thunder,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phebe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's a circus!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, who from her elevated perch had caught\r\nglimpses of a gay cart of some sort and several ponies with flying manes\r\nand tails.\r\n\r\nThe sound died away, and the girls were about to continue their\r\nconfidences when old Debby appeared, looking rather cross and sleepy\r\nafter her nap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are wanted in the parlor, Miss Rose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas anybody come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle girls shouldn't ask questions, but do as they are bid,\xe2\x80\x9d was all\r\nDebby would answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do hope it isn't Aunt Myra; she always scares me out of my wits\r\nasking how my cough is, and groaning over me as if I was going to die,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Rose, preparing to retire the way she came, for the slide, being\r\ncut for the admission of bouncing Christmas turkeys and puddings, was\r\nplenty large enough for a slender girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGuess you'll wish it was Aunt Myra when you see who has come. Don't\r\nnever let me catch you coming into my kitchen that way again, or I'll\r\nshut you up in the big b'iler,\xe2\x80\x9d growled Debby, who thought it her duty\r\nto snub children on all occasions.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 2--The Clan\r\n\r\nRose scrambled into the china-closet as rapidly as possible, and there\r\nrefreshed herself by making faces at Debby, while she settled her\r\nplumage and screwed up her courage. Then she crept softly down the hall\r\nand peeped into the parlor. No one appeared, and all was so still she\r\nfelt sure the company was upstairs. So she skipped boldly through the\r\nhalf-open folding-doors, to behold on the other side a sight that nearly\r\ntook her breath away.\r\n\r\nSeven boys stood in a row all ages, all sizes, all yellow-haired and\r\nblue-eyed, all in full Scotch costume, and all smiling, nodding, and\r\nsaying as with one voice, \xe2\x80\x9cHow are you, cousin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose gave a little gasp, and looked wildly about her as if ready to fly,\r\nfor fear magnified the seven and the room seemed full of boys. Before\r\nshe could run, however, the tallest lad stepped out of the line, saying\r\npleasantly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't be frightened. This is the Clan come to welcome you; and I'm the\r\nchief, Archie, at your service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe held out his hand as he spoke, and Rose timidly put her own into a\r\nbrown paw, which closed over the white morsel and held it as the chief\r\ncontinued his introductions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe came in full rig, for we always turn out in style on grand\r\noccasions. Hope you like it. Now I'll tell you who these chaps are, and\r\nthen we shall be all right. This big one is Prince Charlie, Aunt Clara's\r\nboy. She has but one, so he is an extra good one. This old fellow is\r\nMac, the bookworm, called Worm for short. This sweet creature is Steve\r\nthe Dandy. Look at his gloves and top-knot, if you please. They are Aunt\r\nJane's lads, and a precious pair you'd better believe. These are the\r\nBrats, my brothers, Geordie and Will, and Jamie the Baby. Now, my men,\r\nstep out and show your manners.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this command, to Rose's great dismay, six more hands were offered,\r\nand it was evident that she was expected to shake them all. It was a\r\ntrying moment to the bashful child; but, remembering that they were her\r\nkinsmen come to welcome her, she tried her best to return the greeting\r\ncordially.\r\n\r\nThis impressive ceremony being over, the Clan broke ranks, and both\r\nrooms instantly appeared to be pervaded with boys. Rose hastily retired\r\nto the shelter of a big chair and sat there watching the invaders and\r\nwondering when her aunt would come and rescue her.\r\n\r\nAs if bound to do their duty manfully, yet rather oppressed by it, each\r\nlad paused beside her chair in his wanderings, made a brief remark,\r\nreceived a still briefer answer, and then sheered off with a relieved\r\nexpression.\r\n\r\nArchie came first, and, leaning over the chair-back, observed in a\r\npaternal tone,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm glad you've come, cousin, and I hope you'll find the Aunt-hill\r\npretty jolly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I shall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMac shook his hair out of his eyes, stumbled over a stool, and asked\r\nabruptly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you bring any books with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFour boxes full. They are in the library.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMac vanished from the room, and Steve, striking an attitude which\r\ndisplayed his costume effectively, said with an affable smile,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were sorry not to see you last Wednesday. I hope your cold is\r\nbetter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d And a smile began to dimple about Rose's mouth, as she\r\nremembered her retreat under the bed-cover.\r\n\r\nFeeling that he had been received with distinguished marks of attention,\r\nSteve strolled away with his topknot higher than ever, and Prince\r\nCharlie pranced across the room, saying in a free and easy tone,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma sent her love and hopes you will be well enough to come over for\r\na day next week. It must be desperately dull here for a little thing\r\nlike you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm thirteen and a half, though I do look small,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose,\r\nforgetting her shyness in indignation at this insult to her newly\r\nacquired teens.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeg pardon, ma'am; never should have guessed it.\xe2\x80\x9d And Charlie went off\r\nwith a laugh, glad to have struck a spark out of his meek cousin.\r\n\r\nGeordie and Will came together, two sturdy eleven and twelve year\r\nolders, and, fixing their round blue eyes on Rose, fired off a question\r\napiece, as if it was a shooting match and she the target.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you bring your monkey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; he is dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going to have a boat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the two, with a right-about-face movement, abruptly marched away,\r\nand little Jamie demanded with childish frankness,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you bring me anything nice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, lots of candy,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, whereupon Jamie ascended into her\r\nlap with a sounding kiss and the announcement that he liked her very\r\nmuch.\r\n\r\nThis proceeding rather startled Rose, for the other lads looked and\r\nlaughed, and in her confusion she said hastily to the young usurper,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see the circus go by?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen? Where?\xe2\x80\x9d cried all the boys in great excitement at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust before you came. At least I thought it was a circus, for I saw a\r\nred and black sort of cart and ever so many little ponies, and--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe got no farther, for a general shout made her pause suddenly, as\r\nArchie explained the joke by saying in the middle of his laugh,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was our new dog-cart and the Shetland ponies. You'll never hear the\r\nlast of your circus, cousin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there were so many, and they went so fast, and the cart was so very\r\nred,\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose, trying to explain her mistake.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome and see them all!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Prince. And before she knew what was\r\nhappening, she was borne away to the barn and tumultuously introduced to\r\nthree shaggy ponies and the gay new dog-cart.\r\n\r\nShe had never visited these regions before, and had her doubts as to the\r\npropriety of her being there now, but when she suggested that \xe2\x80\x9cAuntie\r\nmight not like it,\xe2\x80\x9d there was a general cry of,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe told us to amuse you, and we can do it ever so much better out here\r\nthan poking round in the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm afraid I shall get cold without my sacque,\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose, who wanted\r\nto stay, but felt rather out of her element.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you won't! We'll fix you,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the lads, as one clapped his cap\r\non her head, another tied a rough jacket round her neck by the sleeves,\r\na third neatly smothered her in a carriage blanket, and a fourth threw\r\nopen the door of the old barouche that stood there, saying with a\r\nflourish,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStep in, ma'am, and make yourself comfortable while we show you some\r\nfun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Rose sat in state enjoying herself very much, for the lads proceeded\r\nto dance a Highland Fling with a spirit and skill that made her clap her\r\nhands and laugh as she had not done for weeks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is that, my lassie?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Prince, coming up all flushed and\r\nbreathless when the ballet was over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was splendid! I never went to the theatre but once, and the dancing\r\nwas not half so pretty as this. What clever boys you must be!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nRose, smiling upon her kinsmen like a little queen upon her subjects.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, we're a fine lot, and that is only the beginning of our larks. We\r\nhaven't got the pipes here or we'd,\r\n\r\n    'Sing for you, play for you\r\n     A dulcy melody,'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nanswered Charlie, looking much elated at her praise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not know we were Scotch; papa never said anything about it, or\r\nseemed to care about Scotland, except to have me sing the old ballads,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Rose, beginning to feel as if she had left America behind her\r\nsomewhere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither did we till lately. We've been reading Scott's novels, and all\r\nof a sudden we remembered that our grandfather was a Scotchman. So we\r\nhunted up the old stories, got a bagpipe, put on our plaids, and went\r\nin, heart and soul, for the glory of the Clan. We've been at it some\r\ntime now, and it's great fun. Our people like it, and I think we are a\r\npretty canny set.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArchie said this from the other coach-step, where he had perched,\r\nwhile the rest climbed up before and behind to join in the chat as they\r\nrested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm Fitzjames and he's Roderick Dhu, and we'll give you the broadsword\r\ncombat some day. It's a great thing, you'd better believe,\xe2\x80\x9d added the\r\nPrince.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and you should hear Steve play the pipes. He makes 'em skirl like\r\na good one,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Will from the box, eager to air the accomplishments\r\nof his race.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMac's the fellow to hunt up the old stories and tell us how to dress\r\nright, and pick out rousing bits for us to speak and sing,\xe2\x80\x9d put in\r\nGeordie, saying a good word for the absent Worm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what do you and Will do?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose of Jamie, who sat beside her\r\nas if bound to keep her in sight till the promised gift had been handed\r\nover.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I'm the little foot-page, and do errands, and Will and Geordie are\r\nthe troops when we march, and the stags when we hunt, and the traitors\r\nwhen we want to cut any heads off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are very obliging, I'm sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, whereat the \xe2\x80\x9cutility men\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n beamed with modest pride and resolved to enact Wallace and Montrose as\r\nsoon as possible for their cousin's special benefit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet's have a game of tag,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Prince, swinging himself up to a\r\nbeam with a sounding slap on Stevie's shoulder.\r\n\r\nRegardless of his gloves, Dandy tore after him, and the rest swarmed in\r\nevery direction as if bent on breaking their necks and dislocating their\r\njoints as rapidly as possible.\r\n\r\nIt was a new and astonishing spectacle to Rose, fresh from a prim\r\nboarding-school, and she watched the active lads with breathless\r\ninterest, thinking their antics far superior to those of Mops, the dear\r\ndeparted monkey.\r\n\r\nWill had just covered himself with glory by pitching off a high loft\r\nhead first and coming up all right, when Phebe appeared with a cloak,\r\nhood, and rubbers, also a message from Aunt Plenty that \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Rose was\r\nto come in directly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right; we'll bring her!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Archie, issuing some mysterious\r\norder, which was so promptly obeyed that, before Rose could get out of\r\nthe carriage, the boys had caught hold of the pole and rattled her out\r\nof the barn, round the oval and up to the front door with a cheer that\r\nbrought two caps to an upper window, and caused Debby to cry aloud from\r\nthe back porch,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThem harum-scarum boys will certainly be the death of that delicate\r\nlittle creter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the \xe2\x80\x9cdelicate little creter\xe2\x80\x9d seemed all the better for her trip, and\r\nran up the steps looking rosy, gay, and dishevelled, to be received with\r\nlamentation by Aunt Plenty, who begged her to go and lie down at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, please don't! We have come to tea with our cousin, and we'll be as\r\ngood as gold if you'll let us stay, auntie,\xe2\x80\x9d clamoured the boys, who\r\nnot only approved of \xe2\x80\x9cour cousin\xe2\x80\x9d but had no mind to lose their tea, for\r\nAunt Plenty's name but feebly expressed her bountiful nature.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, dears, you can; only be quiet, and let Rose go and take her iron\r\nand be made tidy, and then we will see what we can find for supper,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said the old lady as she trotted away, followed by a volley of\r\ndirections for the approaching feast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarmalade for me, auntie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlenty of plum-cake, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell Debby to trot out the baked pears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm your man for lemon-pie, ma'am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo have fritters; Rose will like 'em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe'd rather have tarts, I know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Rose came down, fifteen minutes later, with every curl smoothed and\r\nher most beruffled apron on, she found the boys loafing about the long\r\nhall, and paused on the half-way landing to take an observation, for\r\ntill now she had not really examined her new-found cousins.\r\n\r\nThere was a strong family resemblance among them, though some of the\r\nyellow heads were darker than others, some of the cheeks brown instead\r\nof rosy, and the ages varied all the way from sixteen-year-old Archie\r\nto Jamie, who was ten years younger. None of them were especially\r\ncomely but the Prince, yet all were hearty, happy-looking lads, and Rose\r\ndecided that boys were not as dreadful as she had expected to find them.\r\n\r\nThey were all so characteristically employed that she could not help\r\nsmiling as she looked. Archie and Charlie, evidently great cronies, were\r\npacing up and down, shoulder to shoulder, whistling \xe2\x80\x9cBonnie Dundee\xe2\x80\x9d; Mac\r\nwas reading in a corner, with his book close to his near-sighted eyes;\r\nDandy was arranging his hair before the oval glass in the hat-stand;\r\nGeordie and Will investigating the internal economy of the moon-faced\r\nclock; and Jamie lay kicking up his heels on the mat at the foot of the\r\nstairs, bent on demanding his sweeties the instant Rose appeared.\r\n\r\nShe guessed his intention, and forestalled his demand by dropping a\r\nhandful of sugar-plums down upon him.\r\n\r\nAt his cry of rapture the other lads looked up and smiled involuntarily,\r\nfor the little kinswoman standing there above was a winsome sight with\r\nher shy, soft eyes, bright hair, and laughing face. The black frock\r\nreminded them of her loss, and filled the boyish hearts with a kindly\r\ndesire to be good to \xe2\x80\x9cour cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d who had no longer any home but this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere she is, as fine as you please,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Steve, kissing his hand to\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on, Missy; tea is ready,\xe2\x80\x9d added the Prince encouragingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall take her in.\xe2\x80\x9d And Archie offered his arm with great dignity, an\r\nhonour that made Rose turn as red as a cherry and long to run upstairs\r\nagain.\r\n\r\nIt was a merry supper, and the two elder boys added much to the fun by\r\ntormenting the rest with dark hints of some interesting event which was\r\nabout to occur. Something uncommonly fine, they declared it was, but\r\nenveloped in the deepest mystery for the present.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I ever see it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jamie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to remember it; but Mac and Steve have, and liked it immensely,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n answered Archie, thereby causing the two mentioned to neglect Debby's\r\ndelectable fritters for several minutes, while they cudgelled their\r\nbrains.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho will have it first?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Will, with his mouth full of marmalade.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Plenty, I guess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen will she have it?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Geordie, bouncing in his seat with\r\nimpatience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometime on Monday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeart alive! what is the boy talking about?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old lady from\r\nbehind the tall urn, which left little to be seen but the topmost bow of\r\nher cap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn't auntie know?\xe2\x80\x9d asked a chorus of voices.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; and that's the best of the joke, for she is desperately fond of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat colour is it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, joining in the fun.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlue and brown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it good to eat?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jamie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome people think so, but I shouldn't like to try it,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nCharlie, laughing so he split his tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho does it belong to?\xe2\x80\x9d put in Steve.\r\n\r\nArchie and the Prince stared at one another rather blankly for a minute,\r\nthen Archie answered with a twinkle of the eye that made Charlie explode\r\nagain,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Grandfather Campbell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was a poser, and they gave up the puzzle, though Jamie confided\r\nto Rose that he did not think he could live till Monday without knowing\r\nwhat this remarkable thing was.\r\n\r\nSoon after tea the Clan departed, singing \xe2\x80\x9cAll the blue bonnets are over\r\nthe border,\xe2\x80\x9d at the tops of their voices.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, dear, how do you like your cousins?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Aunt Plenty, as the\r\nlast pony frisked round the corner and the din died away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty well, ma'am; but I like Phebe better.\xe2\x80\x9d An answer which caused\r\nAunt Plenty to hold up her hands in despair and trot away to tell sister\r\nPeace that she never should understand that child, and it was a mercy\r\nAlec was coming soon to take the responsibility off their hands.\r\n\r\nFatigued by the unusual exertions of the afternoon, Rose curled herself\r\nup in the sofa corner to rest and think about the great mystery, little\r\nguessing that she was to know it first of all.\r\n\r\nRight in the middle of her meditations she fell asleep and dreamed she\r\nwas at home again in her own little bed. She seemed to wake and see her\r\nfather bending over her; to hear him say, \xe2\x80\x9cMy little Rose\xe2\x80\x9d; to answer,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, papa\xe2\x80\x9d; and then to feel him take her in his arms and kiss her\r\ntenderly. So sweet, so real was the dream, that she started up with a\r\ncry of joy to find herself in the arms of a brown, bearded man, who held\r\nher close, and whispered in a voice so like her father's that she clung\r\nto him involuntarily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is my little girl, and I am Uncle Alec.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 3--Uncles\r\n\r\nWhen Rose woke next morning, she was not sure whether she had dreamed\r\nwhat occurred the night before, or it had actually happened. So she\r\nhopped up and dressed, although it was an hour earlier than she usually\r\nrose, for she could not sleep any more, being possessed with a strong\r\ndesire to slip down and see if the big portmanteau and packing cases\r\nwere really in the hall. She seemed to remember tumbling over them when\r\nshe went to bed, for the aunts had sent her off very punctually, because\r\nthey wanted their pet nephew all to themselves.\r\n\r\nThe sun was shining, and Rose opened her window to let in the soft May\r\nair fresh from the sea. As she leaned over her little balcony, watching\r\nan early bird get the worm, and wondering how she should like Uncle\r\nAlec, she saw a man leap the garden wall and come whistling up the path.\r\nAt first she thought it was some trespasser, but a second look showed\r\nher that it was her uncle returning from an early dip into the sea. She\r\nhad hardly dared to look at him the night before, because whenever she\r\ntried to do so she always found a pair of keen blue eyes looking at her.\r\nNow she could take a good stare at him as he lingered along, looking\r\nabout him as if glad to see the old place again.\r\n\r\nA brown, breezy man, in a blue jacket, with no hat on the curly head,\r\nwhich he shook now and then like a water dog; broad-shouldered, alert in\r\nhis motions, and with a general air of strength and stability about him\r\nwhich pleased Rose, though she could not explain the feeling of comfort\r\nit gave her. She had just said to herself, with a sense of relief, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nguess I shall like him, though he looks as if he made people mind,\xe2\x80\x9d when\r\nhe lifted his eyes to examine the budding horse-chestnut overhead,\r\nand saw the eager face peering down at him. He waved his hand to her,\r\nnodded, and called out in a bluff, cheery voice,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are on deck early, little niece.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI got up to see if you had really come, uncle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you? Well, come down here and make sure of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm not allowed to go out before breakfast, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d with a shrug. \xe2\x80\x9cThen I'll come aboard and salute,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded; and, to Rose's great amazement, Uncle Alec went up one of the\r\npillars of the back piazza hand over hand, stepped across the roof,\r\nand swung himself into her balcony, saying, as he landed on the wide\r\nbalustrade: \xe2\x80\x9cHave you any doubts about me now, ma'am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose was so taken aback, she could only answer with a smile as she went\r\nto meet him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow does my girl do this morning?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, taking the little cold\r\nhand she gave him in both his big warm ones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty well, thank you, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but it should be very well. Why isn't it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always wake up with a headache, and feel tired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't you sleep well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI lie awake a long time, and then I dream, and my sleep does not seem\r\nto rest me much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you do all day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I read, and sew a little, and take naps, and sit with auntie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo running about out of doors, or house-work, or riding, hey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Plenty says I'm not strong enough for much exercise. I drive out\r\nwith her sometimes, but I don't care for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm not surprised at that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Alec, half to himself, adding,\r\nin his quick way: \xe2\x80\x9cWho have you had to play with?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one but Ariadne Blish, and she was such a goose I couldn't bear\r\nher. The boys came yesterday, and seemed rather nice; but, of course, I\r\ncouldn't play with them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm too old to play with boys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit of it; that's just what you need, for you've been\r\nmolly-coddled too much. They are good lads, and you'll be mixed up with\r\nthem more or less for years to come, so you may as well be friends and\r\nplaymates at once. I will look you up some girls also, if I can find a\r\nsensible one who is not spoilt by her nonsensical education.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPhebe is sensible, I'm sure, and I like her, though I only saw her\r\nyesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, waking up suddenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who is Phebe, if you please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose eagerly told all she knew, and Uncle Alec listened, with an odd\r\nsmile lurking about his mouth, though his eyes were quite sober as he\r\nwatched the face before him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm glad to see that you are not aristocratic in your tastes, but I\r\ndon't quite make out why you like this young lady from the poor-house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may laugh at me, but I do. I can't tell why, only she seems so\r\nhappy and busy, and sings so beautifully, and is strong enough to scrub\r\nand sweep, and hasn't any troubles to plague her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, making a\r\nfunny jumble of reasons in her efforts to explain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I was telling her about mine, and asked if she had any, and she\r\nsaid, 'No, only I'd like to go to school, and I mean to some day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo she doesn't call desertion, poverty, and hard work, troubles? She's\r\na brave little girl, and I shall be proud to know her.\xe2\x80\x9d And Uncle Alec\r\ngave an approving nod, that made Rose wish she had been the one to earn\r\nit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what are these troubles of yours, child?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, after a minute\r\nof silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don't ask me, uncle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan't you tell them to me as well as to Phebe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething in his tone made Rose feel that it would be better to speak\r\nout and be done with it, so she answered, with sudden colour and averted\r\neyes,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe greatest one was losing dear papa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs she said that, Uncle Alec's arm came gently round her, and he drew\r\nher to him, saying, in the voice so like papa's,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a trouble which I cannot cure, my child; but I shall try to\r\nmake you feel it less. What else, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so tired and poorly all the time, I can't do anything I want to,\r\nand it makes me cross,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Rose, rubbing the aching head like a\r\nfretful child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat we can cure and we will,\xe2\x80\x9d said her uncle, with a decided nod\r\nthat made the curls bob on his head, to that Rose saw the gray ones\r\nunderneath the brown.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Myra says I have no constitution, and never shall be strong,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n observed Rose, in a pensive tone, as if it was rather a nice thing to be\r\nan invalid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Myra is a ahem! an excellent woman, but it is her hobby to believe\r\nthat everyone is tottering on the brink of the grave; and, upon my life,\r\nI believe she is offended if people don't fall into it! We will show her\r\nhow to make constitutions and turn pale-faced little ghosts into rosy,\r\nhearty girls. That's my business, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, more quietly, for\r\nhis sudden outburst had rather startled Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten you were a doctor. I'm glad of it, for I do want to be\r\nwell, only I hope you won't give me much medicine, for I've taken quarts\r\nalready, and it does me no good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs she spoke, Rose pointed to a little table just inside the window, on\r\nwhich appeared a regiment of bottles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, ha! Now we'll see what mischief these blessed women have been at.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n And, making a long arm, Dr. Alec set the bottles on the wide railing\r\nbefore him, examined each carefully, smiled over some, frowned over\r\nothers, and said, as he put down the last: \xe2\x80\x9cNow I'll show you the best\r\nway to take these messes.\xe2\x80\x9d And, as quick as a flash, he sent one after\r\nanother smashing down into the posy-beds below.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Aunt Plenty won't like it; and Aunt Myra will be angry, for she\r\nsent most of them!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, half frightened and half pleased at such\r\nenergetic measures.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are my patient now, and I'll take the responsibility. My way of\r\ngiving physic is evidently the best, for you look better already,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, laughing so infectiously that Rose followed suit, saying saucily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I don't like your medicines any better than those, I shall throw\r\nthem into the garden, and then what will you do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I prescribe such rubbish, I'll give you leave to pitch it\r\noverboard as soon as you like. Now what is the next trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hoped you would forget to ask.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how can I help you if I don't know them? Come, let us have No. 3.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very wrong, I suppose, but I do sometimes wish I had not quite so\r\nmany aunts. They are all very good to me, and I want to please them; but\r\nthey are so different, I feel sort of pulled to pieces among them,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nRose, trying to express the emotions of a stray chicken with six hens\r\nall clucking over it at once.\r\n\r\nUncle Alec threw back his head and laughed like a boy, for he could\r\nentirely understand how the good ladies had each put in her oar and\r\ntried to paddle her own way, to the great disturbance of the waters and\r\nthe entire bewilderment of poor Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI intend to try a course of uncles now, and see how that suits your\r\nconstitution. I'm going to have you all to myself, and no one is to give\r\na word of advice unless I ask it. There is no other way to keep order\r\naboard, and I am captain of this little craft, for a time at least. What\r\ncomes next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Rose stuck there, and grew so red, her uncle guessed what that\r\ntrouble was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't think I can tell this one. It wouldn't be polite, and I feel\r\npretty sure that it isn't going to be a trouble any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs she blushed and stammered over these words, Dr. Alec turned his eyes\r\naway to the distant sea, and said so seriously, so tenderly, that she\r\nfelt every word and long remembered them,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy child, I don't expect you to love and trust me all at once, but I do\r\nwant you to believe that I shall give my whole heart to this new duty;\r\nand if I make mistakes, as I probably shall, no one will grieve over\r\nthem more bitterly than I. It is my fault that I am a stranger to you,\r\nwhen I want to be your best friend. That is one of my mistakes, and I\r\nnever repented it more deeply than I do now. Your father and I had a\r\ntrouble once, and I thought I could never forgive him; so I kept away\r\nfor years. Thank God, we made it all up the last time I saw him, and he\r\ntold me then, that if he was forced to leave her he should bequeath his\r\nlittle girl to me as a token of his love. I can't fill his place, but\r\nI shall try to be a father to her; and if she learns to love me half as\r\nwell as she did the good one she has lost, I shall be a proud and happy\r\nman. Will she believe this and try?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething in Uncle Alec's face touched Rose to the heart, and when he\r\nheld out his hand with that anxious troubled look in his eyes, she was\r\nmoved to put up her innocent lips and seal the contract with a confiding\r\nkiss. The strong arm held her close a minute, and she felt the broad\r\nchest heave once as if with a great sigh of relief; but not a word was\r\nspoken till a tap at the door made both start.\r\n\r\nRose popped her head through the window to say \xe2\x80\x9ccome in,\xe2\x80\x9d while Dr. Alec\r\nhastily rubbed the sleeve of his jacket across his eyes and began to\r\nwhistle again.\r\n\r\nPhebe appeared with a cup of coffee.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDebby told me to bring this and help you get up,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, opening her\r\nblack eyes wide, as if she wondered how on earth \xe2\x80\x9cthe sailor man\xe2\x80\x9d got\r\nthere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm all dressed, so I don't need any help. I hope that is good and\r\nstrong,\xe2\x80\x9d added Rose, eyeing the steaming cup with an eager look.\r\n\r\nBut she did not get it, for a brown hand took possession of it as her\r\nuncle said quickly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold hard, my lass, and let me overhaul that dose before you take it.\r\nDo you drink all this strong coffee every morning, Rose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, and I like it. Auntie says it 'tones' me up, and I always\r\nfeel better after it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis accounts for the sleepless nights, the flutter your heart gets\r\ninto at the least start, and this is why that cheek of yours is pale\r\nyellow instead of rosy red. No more coffee for you, my dear, and by and\r\nby you'll see that I am right. Any new milk downstairs, Phebe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, plenty right in from the barn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's the drink for my patient. Go bring me a pitcherful, and another\r\ncup; I want a draught myself. This won't hurt the honeysuckles, for they\r\nhave no nerves to speak of.\xe2\x80\x9d And, to Rose's great discomfort, the coffee\r\nwent after the medicine.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec saw the injured look she put on, but took no notice, and\r\npresently banished it by saying pleasantly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've got a capital little cup among my traps, and I'll give it to you\r\nto drink your milk in, as it is made of wood that is supposed to improve\r\nwhatever is put into it something like a quassia cup. That reminds me;\r\none of the boxes Phebe wanted to lug upstairs last night is for you.\r\nKnowing that I was coming home to find a ready-made daughter, I picked\r\nup all sorts of odd and pretty trifles along the way, hoping she would\r\nbe able to find something she liked among them all. Early to-morrow\r\nwe'll have a grand rummage. Here's our milk! I propose the health of\r\nMiss Rose Campbell and drink it with all my heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was impossible for Rose to pout with the prospect of a delightful\r\nboxful of gifts dancing before her eyes; so, in spite of herself, she\r\nsmiled as she drank her own health, and found that fresh milk was not a\r\nhard dose to take.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I must be off, before I am caught again with my wig in a toss,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Dr. Alec, preparing to descend the way he came.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you always go in and out like a cat, uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, much amused\r\nat his odd ways.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI used to sneak out of my window when I was a boy, so I need not\r\ndisturb the aunts, and now I rather like it, for it's the shortest road,\r\nand it keeps me limber when I have no rigging to climb. Good-bye till\r\nbreakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d And away he went down the water-spout, over the roof, and\r\nvanished among the budding honey-suckles below.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAin't he a funny guardeen?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Phebe, as she went off with the\r\ncups.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a very kind one, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, following, to prowl\r\nround the big boxes and try to guess which was hers.\r\n\r\nWhen her uncle appeared at sound of the bell, he found her surveying\r\nwith an anxious face a new dish that smoked upon the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGot a fresh trouble, Rosy?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, stroking her smooth head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle, are you going to make me eat oatmeal?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, in a tragic\r\ntone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't you like it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI de-test it!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, with all the emphasis which a turned-up\r\nnose, a shudder, and a groan could give to the three words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not a true Scotchwoman, if you don't like the 'parritch.' It's\r\na pity, for I made it myself, and thought we'd have such a good time\r\nwith all that cream to float it in. Well, never mind.\xe2\x80\x9d And he sat down\r\nwith a disappointed air.\r\n\r\nRose had made up her mind to be obstinate about it, because she did\r\nheartily \xe2\x80\x9cdetest\xe2\x80\x9d the dish; but as Uncle Alec did not attempt to make\r\nher obey, she suddenly changed her mind and thought she would.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll try to eat it to please you, uncle; but people are always saying\r\nhow wholesome it is, and that makes me hate it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, half-ashamed\r\nat her silly excuse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do want you to like it, because I wish my girl to be as well and\r\nstrong as Jessie's boys, who are brought up on this in the good old\r\nfashion. No hot bread and fried stuff for them, and they are the biggest\r\nand bonniest lads of the lot. Bless you, auntie, and good morning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Alec turned to greet the old lady, and, with a firm resolve to eat\r\nor die in the attempt, Rose sat down.\r\n\r\nIn five minutes she forgot what she was eating, so interested was she in\r\nthe chat that went on. It amused her very much to hear Aunt Plenty call\r\nher forty-year-old nephew \xe2\x80\x9cmy dear boy\xe2\x80\x9d; and Uncle Alec was so full\r\nof lively gossip about all creation in general, and the Aunt-hill in\r\nparticular, that the detested porridge vanished without a murmur.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will go to church with us, I hope, Alec, if you are not too tired,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said the old lady, when breakfast was over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came all the way from Calcutta for that express purpose, ma'am. Only\r\nI must send the sisters word of my arrival, for they don't expect me\r\ntill to-morrow, you know, and there will be a row in church if those\r\nboys see me without warning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll send Ben up the hill, and you can step over to Myra's yourself; it\r\nwill please her, and you will have plenty of time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Alec was off at once, and they saw no more of him till the old\r\nbarouche was at the door, and Aunt Plenty just rustling downstairs in\r\nher Sunday best, with Rose like a little black shadow behind her.\r\n\r\nAway they drove in state, and all the way Uncle Alec's hat was more off\r\nhis head than on, for everyone they met smiled and bowed, and gave him\r\nas blithe a greeting as the day permitted.\r\n\r\nIt was evident that the warning had been a wise one, for, in spite of\r\ntime and place, the lads were in such a ferment that their elders sat\r\nin momentary dread of an unseemly outbreak somewhere. It was simply\r\nimpossible to keep those fourteen eyes off Uncle Alec, and the dreadful\r\nthings that were done during sermon-time will hardly be believed.\r\n\r\nRose dared not look up after a while, for these bad boys vented their\r\nemotions upon her till she was ready to laugh and cry with mingled\r\namusement and vexation. Charlie winked rapturously at her behind his\r\nmother's fan; Mac openly pointed to the tall figure beside her; Jamie\r\nstared fixedly over the back of his pew, till Rose thought his round\r\neyes would drop out of his head; George fell over a stool and dropped\r\nthree books in his excitement; Will drew sailors and Chinamen on his\r\nclean cuffs, and displayed them, to Rose's great tribulation; Steve\r\nnearly upset the whole party by burning his nose with salts, as he\r\npretended to be overcome by his joy; even dignified Archie disgraced\r\nhimself by writing in his hymn book, \xe2\x80\x9cIsn't he blue and brown?\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\npassing it politely to Rose.\r\n\r\nHer only salvation was trying to fix her attention upon Uncle Mac\r\na portly, placid gentleman, who seemed entirely unconscious of the\r\niniquities of the Clan, and dozed peacefully in his pew corner. This was\r\nthe only uncle Rose had met for years, for Uncle Jem and Uncle Steve,\r\nthe husbands of Aunt Jessie and Aunt Clara, were at sea, and Aunt Myra\r\nwas a widow. Uncle Mac was a merchant, very rich and busy, and as quiet\r\nas a mouse at home, for he was in such a minority among the women folk\r\nhe dared not open his lips, and let his wife rule undisturbed.\r\n\r\nRose liked the big, kindly, silent man who came to her when papa died,\r\nwas always sending her splendid boxes of goodies at school, and often\r\ninvited her into his great warehouse, full of teas and spices, wines and\r\nall sorts of foreign fruits, there to eat and carry away whatever she\r\nliked. She had secretly regretted that he was not to be her guardian;\r\nbut since she had seen Uncle Alec she felt better about it, for she did\r\nnot particularly admire Aunt Jane.\r\n\r\nWhen church was over, Dr. Alec got into the porch as quickly as\r\npossible, and there the young bears had a hug all round, while the\r\nsisters shook hands and welcomed him with bright faces and glad hearts.\r\nRose was nearly crushed flat behind a door in that dangerous passage\r\nfrom pew to porch; but Uncle Mac rescued her, and put her into the\r\ncarriage for safe keeping.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, girls, I want you to come and dine with Alec; Mac also, of course.\r\nBut I cannot ask the boys, for we did not expect this dear fellow till\r\ntomorrow, you know, so I made no preparations. Send the lads home, and\r\nlet them wait till Monday, for really I was shocked at their behaviour\r\nin church,\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt Plenty, as she followed Rose.\r\n\r\nIn any other place the defrauded boys would have set up a howl; as it\r\nwas, they growled and protested till Dr. Alec settled the matter by\r\nsaying,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, old chaps, I'll make it up to you to-morrow, if you sheer\r\noff quietly; if you don't, not a blessed thing shall you have out of my\r\nbig boxes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 4--Aunts\r\n\r\nAll dinner-time Rose felt that she was going to be talked about, and\r\nafterward she was sure of it, for Aunt Plenty whispered to her as they\r\nwent into the parlour,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRun up and sit awhile with Sister Peace, my dear. She likes to have you\r\nread while she rests, and we are going to be busy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose obeyed, and the quiet rooms above were so like a church that she\r\nsoon composed her ruffled feelings, and was unconsciously a little\r\nminister of happiness to the sweet old lady, who for years had sat there\r\npatiently waiting to be set free from pain.\r\n\r\nRose knew the sad romance of her life, and it gave a certain tender\r\ncharm to this great-aunt of hers, whom she already loved. When Peace was\r\ntwenty, she was about to be married; all was done, the wedding dress lay\r\nready, the flowers were waiting to be put on, the happy hour at hand,\r\nwhen word came that the lover was dead. They thought that gentle Peace\r\nwould die, too; but she bore it bravely, put away her bridal gear, took\r\nup her life afresh, and lived on a beautiful, meek woman, with hair as\r\nwhite as snow and cheeks that never bloomed again. She wore no black,\r\nbut soft, pale colours, as if always ready for the marriage that had\r\nnever come.\r\n\r\nFor thirty years she had lived on, fading slowly, but cheerful, busy,\r\nand full of interest in all that went on in the family; especially the\r\njoys and sorrows of the young girls growing up about her, and to them\r\nshe was adviser, confidante, and friend in all their tender trials and\r\ndelights. A truly beautiful old maiden, with her silvery hair, tranquil\r\nface, and an atmosphere of repose about her that soothed whoever came to\r\nher!\r\n\r\nAunt Plenty was utterly dissimilar, being a stout, brisk old lady, with\r\na sharp eye, a lively tongue, and a face like a winter-apple. Always\r\ntrotting, chatting, and bustling, she was a regular Martha, cumbered\r\nwith the cares of this world and quite happy in them.\r\n\r\nRose was right; and while she softly read psalms to Aunt Peace, the\r\nother ladies were talking about her little self in the frankest manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Alec, how do you like your ward?\xe2\x80\x9d began Aunt Jane, as they all\r\nsettled down, and Uncle Mac deposited himself in a corner to finish his\r\ndoze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like her better if I could have begun at the beginning, and\r\nso got a fair start. Poor George led such a solitary life that the child\r\nhas suffered in many ways, and since he died she has been going on worse\r\nthan ever, judging from the state I find her in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear boy, we did what we thought best while waiting for you to wind\r\nup your affairs and get home. I always told George he was wrong to bring\r\nher up as he did; but he never took my advice, and now here we are with\r\nthis poor dear child upon our hands. I, for one, freely confess that\r\nI don't know what to do with her any more than if she was one of those\r\nstrange, outlandish birds you used to bring home from foreign parts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n And Aunt Plenty gave a perplexed shake of the head which caused great\r\ncommotion among the stiff loops of purple ribbon that bristled all over\r\nthe cap like crocus buds.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf my advice had been taken, she would have remained at the excellent\r\nschool where I placed her. But our aunt thought best to remove her\r\nbecause she complained, and she has been dawdling about ever since she\r\ncame. A most ruinous state of things for a morbid, spoilt girl like\r\nRose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Jane, severely.\r\n\r\nShe had never forgiven the old ladies for yielding to Rose's pathetic\r\npetition that she might wait her guardian's arrival before beginning\r\nanother term at the school, which was a regular Blimber hot-bed, and\r\nturned out many a feminine Toots.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thought it the proper school for a child in good circumstances\r\nan heiress, in fact, as Rose is. It is all very well for girls who are\r\nto get their own living by teaching, and that sort of thing; but all\r\nshe needs is a year or two at a fashionable finishing school, so that at\r\neighteen she can come out with eclat,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Aunt Clara, who had been a\r\nbeauty and a belle, and was still a handsome woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear, dear! how short-sighted you all are to be discussing education\r\nand plans for the future, when this unhappy child is so plainly marked\r\nfor the tomb,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Aunt Myra, with a lugubrious sniff and a solemn\r\nwag of the funereal bonnet, which she refused to remove, being afflicted\r\nwith a chronic catarrh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, it is my opinion that the dear thing only wants freedom, rest, and\r\ncare. There is look in her eyes that goes to my heart, for it shows that\r\nshe feels the need of what none of us can give her a mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt\r\nJessie, with tears in her own bright eyes at the thought of her boys\r\nbeing left, as Rose was, to the care of others.\r\n\r\nUncle Alec, who had listened silently as each spoke, turned quickly\r\ntowards the last sister, and said, with a decided nod of approval,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou've got it, Jessie; and, with you to help me, I hope to make the\r\nchild feel that she is not quite fatherless and motherless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll do my best, Alec; and I think you will need me, for, wise as you\r\nare, you cannot understand a tender, timid little creature like Rose as\r\na woman can,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Jessie, smiling back at him with a heart full of\r\nmotherly goodwill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot help feeling that I, who have had a daughter of my own, can\r\nbest bring up a girl; and I am very much surprised that George did\r\nnot entrust her to me,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Aunt Myra, with an air of melancholy\r\nimportance, for she was the only one who had given a daughter to\r\nthe family, and she felt that she had distinguished herself, though\r\nill-natured people said that she had dosed her darling to death.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never blamed him in the least, when I remember the perilous\r\nexperiments you tried with poor Carrie,\xe2\x80\x9d began Mrs. Jane, in her hard\r\nvoice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJane Campbell, I will not hear a word! My sainted Caroline is a sacred\r\nobject,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Aunt Myra, rising as if to leave the room.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec detained her, feeling that he must define his position at once,\r\nand maintain it manfully if he hoped to have any success in his new\r\nundertaking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, my dear souls, don't let us quarrel and make Rose a bone of\r\ncontention though, upon my word, she is almost a bone, poor little lass!\r\nYou have had her among you for a year, and done what you liked. I cannot\r\nsay that your success is great, but that is owing to too many fingers in\r\nthe pie. Now, I intend to try my way for a year, and if at the end of it\r\nshe is not in better trim than now, I'll give up the case, and hand her\r\nover to someone else. That's fair, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will not be here a year hence, poor darling, so no one need dread\r\nfuture responsibility,\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt Myra, folding her black gloves as if\r\nall ready for the funeral.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jupiter! Myra, you are enough to damp the ardour of a saint!\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nDr. Alec, with a sudden spark in his eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cYour croaking will worry\r\nthat child out of her wits, for she is an imaginative puss, and will\r\nfret and fancy untold horrors. You have put it into her head that she\r\nhas no constitution, and she rather likes the idea. If she had not had a\r\npretty good one, she would have been 'marked for the tomb' by this\r\ntime, at the rate you have been going on with her. I will not have any\r\ninterference please understand that; so just wash your hands of her, and\r\nlet me manage till I want help, then I'll ask for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear, hear!\xe2\x80\x9d came from the corner where Uncle Mac was apparently wrapt\r\nin slumber.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were appointed guardian, so we can do nothing. But I predict that\r\nthe girl will be spoilt, utterly spoilt,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mrs. Jane, grimly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, sister. I have an idea that if a woman can bring up two boys\r\nas perfectly as you do yours, a man, if he devotes his whole mind to it,\r\nmay at least attempt as much with one girl,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Dr. Alec, with a\r\nhumorous look that tickled the others immensely, for it was a well-known\r\nfact in the family that Jane's boys were more indulged than all the\r\nother lads put together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am quite easy, for I really do think that Alec will improve the\r\nchild's health; and by the time his year is out, it will be quite soon\r\nenough for her to go to Madame Roccabella's and be finished off,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nAunt Clara, settling her rings, and thinking, with languid satisfaction,\r\nof the time when she could bring out a pretty and accomplished niece.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you will stay here in the old place, unless you think of\r\nmarrying, and it's high time you did,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Mrs. Jane, much nettled at\r\nher brother's last hit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, thank you. Come and have a cigar, Mac,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, abruptly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't marry; women enough in the family already,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Uncle Mac;\r\nand then the gentlemen hastily fled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Peace would like to see you all, she says,\xe2\x80\x9d was the message Rose\r\nbrought before the ladies could begin again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHectic, hectic! dear me, dear me!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Aunt Myra, as the shadow\r\nof her gloomy bonnet fell upon Rose, and the stiff tips of a black glove\r\ntouched the cheek where the colour deepened under so many eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad these pretty curls are natural; they will be invaluable by\r\nand by,\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt Clara, taking an observation with her head on one\r\nside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow that your uncle has come, I no longer expect you to review the\r\nstudies of the past year. I trust your time will not be entirely wasted\r\nin frivolous sports, however,\xe2\x80\x9d added Aunt Jane, sailing out of the room\r\nwith the air of a martyr.\r\n\r\nAunt Jessie said not a word, but kissed her little niece, with a look\r\nof tender sympathy that made Rose cling to her a minute, and follow her\r\nwith grateful eyes as the door closed behind her.\r\n\r\nAfter everybody had gone home, Dr. Alec paced up and down the lower\r\nhall in the twilight for an hour, thinking so intently that sometimes\r\nhe frowned, sometimes he smiled, and more than once he stood still in a\r\nbrown study. All of a sudden he said, half aloud, as if he had made up\r\nhis mind,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI might as well begin at once, and give the child something new to\r\nthink about, for Myra's dismals and Jane's lectures have made her as\r\nblue as a little indigo bag.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiving into one of the trunks that stood in a corner, he brought up,\r\nafter a brisk rummage, a silken cushion, prettily embroidered, and a\r\nquaint cup of dark carved wood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis will do for a start,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as he plumped up the cushion and\r\ndusted the cup. \xe2\x80\x9cIt won't do to begin too energetically, or Rose will be\r\nfrightened. I must beguile her gently and pleasantly along till I've won\r\nher confidence, and then she will be ready for anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJust then Phebe came out of the dining-room with a plate of brown bread,\r\nfor Rose had been allowed no hot biscuit for tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll relieve you of some of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, and, helping himself\r\nto a generous slice, he retired to the study, leaving Phebe to wonder at\r\nhis appetite.\r\n\r\nShe would have wondered still more if she had seen him making that brown\r\nbread into neat little pills, which he packed into an attractive ivory\r\nbox, out of which he emptied his own bits of lovage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere! if they insist on medicine, I'll order these, and no harm will\r\nbe done. I will have my own way, but I'll keep the peace, if possible,\r\nand confess the joke when my experiment has succeeded,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to\r\nhimself, looking very much like a mischievous boy, as he went on with\r\nhis innocent prescriptions.\r\n\r\nRose was playing softly on the small organ that stood in the upper hall,\r\nso that Aunt Peace could enjoy it; and all the while he talked with the\r\nold ladies, Uncle Alec was listening to the fitful music of the child,\r\nand thinking of another Rose who used to play for him.\r\n\r\nAs the clock struck eight, he called out,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTime for my girl to be abed, else she won't be up early, and I'm full\r\nof jolly plans for to-morrow. Come and see what I've found for you to\r\nbegin upon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose ran in and listened with bright attentive face, while Dr. Alec said\r\nimpressively,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn my wanderings over the face of the earth, I have picked up some\r\nexcellent remedies, and, as they are rather agreeable ones, I think you\r\nand I will try them. This is a herb-pillow, given to me by a wise old\r\nwoman when I was ill in India. It is filled with saffron, poppies, and\r\nother soothing plants; so lay your little head on it to-night, sleep\r\nsweetly without a dream, and wake to-morrow without a pain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I really? How nice it smells.\xe2\x80\x9d And Rose willingly received\r\nthe pretty pillow, and stood enjoying its faint, sweet odour, as she\r\nlistened to the doctor's next remedy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the cup I told you of. Its virtue depends, they say, on the\r\ndrinker filling it himself; so you must learn to milk. I'll teach you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm afraid I never can,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose; but she surveyed the cup with\r\nfavour, for a funny little imp danced on the handle, as if all ready to\r\ntake a header into the white sea below.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't you think she ought to have something more strengthening than\r\nmilk, Alec? I really shall feel anxious if she does not have a tonic of\r\nsome sort,\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt Plenty, eyeing the new remedies suspiciously, for\r\nshe had more faith in her old-fashioned doses than all the magic cups\r\nand poppy pillows of the East.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, ma'am, I'm willing to give her a pill, if you think best. It is\r\na very simple one, and very large quantities may be taken without harm.\r\nYou know hasheesh is the extract of hemp? Well, this is a preparation of\r\ncorn and rye, much used in old times, and I hope it will be again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me, how singular!\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt Plenty, bringing her spectacles to\r\nbear upon the pills, with a face so full of respectful interest that it\r\nwas almost too much for Dr. Alec's gravity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake one in the morning, and a good-night to you, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\ndismissing his patient with a hearty kiss.\r\n\r\nThen, as she vanished, he put both hands into his hair, exclaiming, with\r\na comical mixture of anxiety and amusement,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I think what I have undertaken, I declare to you, aunt, I feel\r\nlike running away and not coming back till Rose is eighteen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 5--A Belt and a Box\r\n\r\nWhen Rose came out of her chamber, cup in hand, next morning, the first\r\nperson she saw was Uncle Alec standing on the threshold of the room\r\nopposite, which he appeared to be examining with care. When he heard her\r\nstep, he turned about and began to sing,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you going, my pretty maid?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm going a-milking, sir, she said,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, waving the cup; and\r\nthen they finished the verse together in fine style.\r\n\r\nBefore either spoke, a head, in a nightcap so large and beruffled that\r\nit looked like a cabbage, popped out of a room farther down the hall,\r\nand an astonished voice exclaimed,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the world are you doing about so early?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClearing our pipes for the day, ma'am. Look here, auntie, can I have\r\nthis room?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, making her a sailor's bow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny room you like, except sister's.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks. And may I go rummaging round in the garrets and glory-holes to\r\nfurnish it as I like?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear boy, you may turn the house upside down if you will only stay\r\nin it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's a handsome offer, I'm sure. I'll stay, ma'am; here's my little\r\nanchor, so you will get more than you want of me this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's impossible! Put on your jacket, Rose. Don't tire her out\r\nwith antics, Alec. Yes, sister, I'm coming!\xe2\x80\x9d and the cabbage vanished\r\nsuddenly.\r\n\r\nThe first milking lesson was a droll one; but after several scares and\r\nmany vain attempts, Rose at last managed to fill her cup, while Ben\r\nheld Clover's tail so that it could not flap, and Dr. Alec kept her\r\nfrom turning to stare at the new milkmaid, who objected to both these\r\nproceedings very much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look chilly in spite of all this laughing. Take a smart run round\r\nthe garden and get up a glow,\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor, as they left the barn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm too old for running, uncle; Miss Power said it was not lady-like\r\nfor girls in their teens,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, primly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take the liberty of differing from Madame Prunes and Prisms, and, as\r\nyour physician, I order you to run. Off with you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Alec, with\r\na look and a gesture that made Rose scurry away as fast as she could go.\r\n\r\nAnxious to please him, she raced round the beds till she came back to\r\nthe porch where he stood, and, dropping down upon the steps, she sat\r\npanting, with cheeks as rosy as the rigolette on her shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well done, child; I see you have not lost the use of your limbs\r\nthough you are in your teens. That belt is too tight; unfasten it, then\r\nyou can take a long breath without panting so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn't tight, sir; I can breathe perfectly well,\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose, trying\r\nto compose herself.\r\n\r\nHer uncle's only answer was to lift her up and unhook the new belt of\r\nwhich she was so proud. The moment the clasp was open the belt flew\r\napart several inches, for it was impossible to restrain the involuntary\r\nsigh of relief that flatly contradicted her words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I didn't know it was tight! it didn't feel so a bit. Of course it\r\nwould open if I puff like this, but I never do, because I hardly ever\r\nrun,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Rose, rather discomfited by this discovery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see you don't half fill your lungs, and so you can wear this absurd\r\nthing without feeling it. The idea of cramping a tender little waist\r\nin a stiff band of leather and steel just when it ought to be growing,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Dr. Alec, surveying the belt with great disfavour as he put the\r\nclasp forward several holes, to Rose's secret dismay, for she was proud\r\nof her slender figure, and daily rejoiced that she wasn't as stout\r\nas Luly Miller, a former schoolmate, who vainly tried to repress her\r\nplumpness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will fall off if it is so loose,\xe2\x80\x9d she said anxiously, as she stood\r\nwatching him pull her precious belt about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if you keep taking long breaths to hold it on. That is what I want\r\nyou to do, and when you have filled this out we will go on enlarging it\r\ntill your waist is more like that of Hebe, goddess of health, and less\r\nlike that of a fashion-plate the ugliest thing imaginable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow it does look!\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose gave a glance of scorn at the loose belt\r\nhanging round her trim little waist. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will be lost, and then I shall\r\nfeel badly, for it cost ever so much, and is real steel and Russia\r\nleather. Just smell how nice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it is lost I'll give you a better one. A soft silken sash is much\r\nfitter for a pretty child like you than a plated harness like this; and\r\nI've got no end of Italian scarfs and Turkish sashes among my traps. Ah!\r\nthat makes you feel better, doesn't it?\xe2\x80\x9d and he pinched the cheek that\r\nhad suddenly dimpled with a smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very silly of me, but I can't help liking to know that\xe2\x80\x9d here she\r\nstopped and blushed and held down her head, ashamed to add, \xe2\x80\x9cyou think I\r\nam pretty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Alec's eyed twinkled, but he said very soberly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRose, are you vain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm afraid I am,\xe2\x80\x9d answered a very meek voice from behind the veil of\r\nhair that hid the red face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a sad fault.\xe2\x80\x9d And he sighed as if grieved at the confession.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it is, and I try not to be; but people praise me, and I can't\r\nhelp liking it, for I really don't think I am repulsive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe last word and the funny tone in which it was uttered were too\r\nmuch for Dr. Alec, and he laughed in spite of himself, to Rose's great\r\nrelief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI quite agree with you; and in order that you may be still less\r\nrepulsive, I want you to grow as fine a girl as Phebe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPhebe!\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose looked so amazed that her uncle nearly went off again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Phebe; for she has what you need health. If you dear little girls\r\nwould only learn what real beauty is, and not pinch and starve and\r\nbleach yourselves out so, you'd save an immense deal of time and money\r\nand pain. A happy soul in a healthy body makes the best sort of beauty\r\nfor man or woman. Do you understand that, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, much taken down by this comparison with the\r\ngirl from the poor-house. It nettled her sadly, and she showed that it\r\ndid by saying quickly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you would like to have me sweep and scrub, and wear an old\r\nbrown dress, and go round with my sleeves rolled up, as Phebe does?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should very much, if you could work as well as she does, and show as\r\nstrong a pair of arms as she can.
