I haven't seen a prettier picture for\r\nsome time than she made of herself this morning, up to the elbows in\r\nsuds, singing like a blackbird whilst she scrubbed on the back stoop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I do think you are the queerest man that ever lived!\xe2\x80\x9d was all\r\nRose could find to say after this display of bad taste.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven't begun to show you my oddities yet, so you must make up your\r\nmind to worse shocks than this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with such a whimsical look\r\nthat she was glad the sound of a bell prevented her showing more plainly\r\nwhat a blow her little vanities had already received.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will find your box all open up in auntie's parlor, and there you\r\ncan amuse her and yourself by rummaging to your heart's content; I've\r\ngot to be cruising round all the morning getting my room to rights,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Dr. Alec, as they rose from breakfast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan't I help you, uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, quite burning to be useful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, thank you, I'm going to borrow Phebe for a while, if Aunt Plenty\r\ncan spare her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnybody anything, Alec. You will want me, I know, so I'll give orders\r\nabout dinner and be all ready to lend a hand\xe2\x80\x9d; and the old lady bustled\r\naway full of interest and good-will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle will find that I can do some things that Phebe can't, so now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n thought Rose, with a toss of the head as she flew to Aunt Peace and the\r\nlong-desired box.\r\n\r\nEvery little girl can easily imagine what an extra good time she had\r\ndiving into a sea of treasures and fishing up one pretty thing after\r\nanother, till the air was full of the mingled odours of musk and\r\nsandalwood, the room gay with bright colours, and Rose in a rapture of\r\ndelight. She began to forgive Dr. Alec for the oatmeal diet when she saw\r\na lovely ivory workbox; became resigned to the state of her belt when\r\nshe found a pile of rainbow-coloured sashes; and when she came to some\r\ndistractingly pretty bottles of attar of rose, she felt that they almost\r\natoned for the great sin of thinking Phebe the finer girl of the two.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec meanwhile had apparently taken Aunt Plenty at her word, and was\r\nturning the house upside down. A general revolution was evidently going\r\non in the green-room, for the dark damask curtains were seen bundling\r\naway in Phebe's arms; the air-tight stove retiring to the cellar on\r\nBen's shoulder; and the great bedstead going up garret in a fragmentary\r\nstate, escorted by three bearers. Aunt Plenty was constantly on the trot\r\namong her store-rooms, camphor-chests, and linen-closets, looking as if\r\nthe new order of things both amazed and amused her.\r\n\r\nHalf the peculiar performances of Dr. Alec cannot be revealed; but as\r\nRose glanced up from her box now and then she caught glimpses of him\r\nstriding by, bearing a bamboo chair, a pair of ancient andirons, a queer\r\nJapanese screen, a rug or two, and finally a large bathing-pan upon his\r\nhead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a curious room it will be,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as she sat resting and\r\nrefreshing herself with \xe2\x80\x9cLumps of Delight,\xe2\x80\x9d all the way from Cairo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy you will like it, deary,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Aunt Peace, looking up with\r\na smile from some pretty trifle she was making with blue silk and white\r\nmuslin.\r\n\r\nRose did not see the smile, for just at that moment her uncle paused\r\nat the door, and she sprang up to dance before him, saying, with a face\r\nfull of childish happiness,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at me! look at me! I'm splendid I don't know myself. I haven't put\r\nthese things on right, I dare say, but I do like them so much!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look as gay as a parrot in your fez and cabaja, and it does my\r\nheart good to see the little black shadow turned into a rainbow,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Uncle Alec, surveying the bright figure before him with great\r\napprobation.\r\n\r\nHe did not say it, but he thought she made a much prettier picture than\r\nPhebe at the wash-tub, for she had stuck a purple fez on her blonde\r\nhead, tied several brilliant scarfs about her waist, and put on a truly\r\ngorgeous scarlet jacket with a golden sun embroidered on the back, a\r\nsilver moon on the front, and stars of all sizes on the sleeves. A pair\r\nof Turkish slippers adorned her feet, and necklaces of amber, coral, and\r\nfiligree hung about her neck, while one hand held a smelling-bottle, and\r\nthe other the spicy box of oriental sweetmeats.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel like a girl in the 'Arabian Nights,' and expect to find a magic\r\ncarpet or a wonderful talisman somewhere. Only I don't see how I ever\r\ncan thank you for all these lovely things,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, stopping her\r\ndance, as if suddenly oppressed with gratitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll tell you how by leaving off the black clothes, that never should\r\nhave been kept so long on such a child, and wearing the gay ones I've\r\nbrought. It will do your spirits good, and cheer up this sober old\r\nhouse. Won't it, auntie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are right, Alec, and it is fortunate that we have not\r\nbegun on her spring clothes yet, for Myra thought she ought not to wear\r\nanything brighter than violet, and she is too pale for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou just let me direct Miss Hemming how to make some of these things.\r\nYou will be surprised to see how much I know about piping hems and\r\ngathering arm-holes and shirring biases,\xe2\x80\x9d began Dr. Alec, patting a pile\r\nof muslin, cloth and silk with a knowing air.\r\n\r\nAunt Peace and Rose laughed so that he could not display his knowledge\r\nany farther, till they stopped, when he said good-naturedly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will go a great way toward filling out the belt, so laugh away,\r\nMorgiana, and I'll go back to my work, or I never shall be done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn't help it, 'shirred biases' were so very funny!\xe2\x80\x9d Rose said,\r\nas she turned to her box after the splendid laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cBut really, auntie,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n she added soberly, \xe2\x80\x9cI feel as if I ought not to have so many nice\r\nthings. I suppose it wouldn't do to give Phebe some of them? Uncle might\r\nnot like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe would not mind; but they are not suitable for Phebe. Some of the\r\ndresses you are done with would be more useful, if they can be made over\r\nto fit her,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Aunt Peace in the prudent, moderate tone which is\r\nso trying to our feelings when we indulge in little fits of charitable\r\nenthusiasm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'd rather give her new ones, for I think she is a little bit proud\r\nand might not like old things. If she was my sister it would do, because\r\nsisters don't mind, but she isn't, and that makes it bad, you see. I\r\nknow how I can manage beautifully; I'll adopt her!\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose looked\r\nquite radiant with this new idea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm afraid you could not do it legally till you are older, but you\r\nmight see if she likes the plan, and at any rate you can be very kind to\r\nher, for in one sense we are all sisters, and should help one another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sweet old face looked at her so kindly that Rose was fired with a\r\ndesire to settle the matter at once, and rushed away to the kitchen,\r\njust as she was. Phebe was there, polishing up the antique andirons so\r\nbusily that she started when a voice cried out: \xe2\x80\x9cSmell that, taste this,\r\nand look at me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhebe sniffed attar of rose, crunched the \xe2\x80\x9cLump of Delight\xe2\x80\x9d tucked into\r\nher mouth, and stared with all her eyes at little Morgiana prancing\r\nabout the room like a brilliant paroquet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy stars, ain't you splendid!\xe2\x80\x9d was all she could say, holding up two\r\ndusty hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've got heaps of lovely things upstairs, and I'll show them all to\r\nyou, and I'd go halves, only auntie thinks they wouldn't be useful, so\r\nI shall give you something else; and you won't mind, will you? because I\r\nwant to adopt you as Arabella was in the story. Won't that be nice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Miss Rose, have you lost your wits?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo wonder Phebe asked, for Rose talked very fast, and looked so odd in\r\nher new costume, and was so eager she could not stop to explain. Seeing\r\nPhebe's bewilderment, she quieted down and said, with a pretty air of\r\nearnestness,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn't fair that I should have so much and you so little, and I want\r\nto be as good to you as if you were my sister, for Aunt Peace says we\r\nare all sisters really. I thought if I adopted you as much as I can now,\r\nit would be nicer. Will you let me, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo Rose's great surprise, Phebe sat down on the floor and hid her face\r\nin her apron for a minute without answering a word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear, now she's offended, and I don't know what to do,\xe2\x80\x9d thought\r\nRose, much discouraged by this reception of her offer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, forgive me; I didn't mean to hurt your feelings, and hope you\r\nwon't think--\xe2\x80\x9d she faltered presently, feeling that she must undo the\r\nmischief, if possible.\r\n\r\nBut Phebe gave her another surprise, by dropping the apron and showing\r\na face all smiles, in spite of tears in the eyes, as she put both arms\r\nround Rose and said, with a laugh and sob,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are the dearest girl in the world, and I'll let you do\r\nanything you like with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you do like the plan? You didn't cry because I seemed to be kind\r\nof patronising? I truly didn't mean to be,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, delighted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess I do like it! and cried because no one was ever so good to me\r\nbefore, and I couldn't help it. As for patronising, you may walk on me\r\nif you want to, and I won't mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phebe, in a burst of gratitude,\r\nfor the words, \xe2\x80\x9cwe are sisters\xe2\x80\x9d went straight to her lonely heart and\r\nnestled there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, we can play I'm a good sprite out of the box, or, what is\r\nbetter, a fairy godmother come down the chimney, and you are Cinderella,\r\nand must say what you want,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, trying to put the question\r\ndelicately.\r\n\r\nPhebe understood that, for she had a good deal of natural refinement,\r\nthough she did come from the poor-house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't feel as if I wanted anything now, Miss Rose, but to find some\r\nway of thanking you for all you've done,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, rubbing off a tear\r\nthat went rolling down the bridge of her nose in the most unromantic\r\nway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I haven't done anything but given you a bit of candy! Here, have\r\nsome more, and eat 'em while you work, and think what I can do. I must\r\ngo and clear up, so good-bye, and don't forget I've adopted you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou've given me sweeter things than candy, and I'm not likely to forget\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d And carefully wiping off the brick-dust, Phebe pressed the little\r\nhand Rose offered warmly in both her hard ones, while the black eyes\r\nfollowed the departing visitor with a grateful look that made them very\r\nsoft and bright.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 6--Uncle Alec's Room\r\n\r\nSoon after dinner, and before she had got acquainted with half her\r\nnew possessions, Dr. Alec proposed a drive, to carry round the first\r\ninstalment of gifts to the aunts and cousins. Rose was quite ready to\r\ngo, being anxious to try a certain soft burnous from the box, which\r\nnot only possessed a most engaging little hood, but had funny tassels\r\nbobbing in all directions.\r\n\r\nThe big carriage was full of parcels, and even Ben's seat was loaded\r\nwith Indian war clubs, a Chinese kite of immense size, and a pair of\r\npolished ox-horns from Africa. Uncle Alec, very blue as to his clothes,\r\nand very brown as to his face, sat bolt upright, surveying well\r\nknown places with interest, while Rose, feeling unusually elegant and\r\ncomfortable, leaned back folded in her soft mantle, and played she was\r\nan Eastern princess making a royal progress among her subjects.\r\n\r\nAt three of the places their calls were brief, for Aunt Myra's catarrh\r\nwas unusually bad; Aunt Clara had a room full of company; and Aunt\r\nJane showed such a tendency to discuss the population, productions, and\r\npolitics of Europe, Asia and Africa, that even Dr. Alec was dismayed,\r\nand got away as soon as possible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we will have a good time! I do hope the boys will be at home,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nRose, with a sigh of relief, as they wound yet higher up the hill to\r\nAunt Jessie's.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI left this for the last call, so that we might find the lads just in\r\nfrom school. Yes, there is Jamie on the gate watching for us; now you'll\r\nsee the Clan gather; they are always swarming about together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe instant Jamie saw the approaching guests he gave a shrill whistle,\r\nwhich was answered by echoes from meadow, house and barn, as the cousins\r\ncame running from all directions, shouting, \xe2\x80\x9cHooray for Uncle Alec!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n They went at the carriage like highwaymen, robbed it of every parcel,\r\ntook the occupants prisoners, and marched them into the house with great\r\nexultation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle Mum! little Mum! here they are with lots of goodies! Come down\r\nand see the fun right away! Quick!\xe2\x80\x9d bawled Will and Geordie amidst a\r\ngeneral ripping off of papers and a reckless cutting of strings that\r\nsoon turned the tidy room into a chaos.\r\n\r\nDown came Aunt Jessie with her pretty cap half on, but such a beaming\r\nface below it that one rather thought the fly-away head-gear an\r\nimprovement than otherwise. She had hardly time to greet Rose and the\r\ndoctor before the boys were about her, each clamouring for her to see\r\nhis gift and rejoice over it with him, for \xe2\x80\x9clittle Mum\xe2\x80\x9d went halves in\r\neverything. The great horns skirmished about her as if to toss her to\r\nthe ceiling; the war clubs hurtled over her head as if to annihilate\r\nher; an amazing medley from the four quarters of the globe filled her\r\nlap, and seven excited boys all talked to her at once.\r\n\r\nBut she liked it; oh dear, yes! and sat smiling, admiring, and\r\nexplaining, quite untroubled by the din, which made Rose cover up her\r\nears and Dr. Alec threaten instant flight if the riot was not quelled.\r\nThat threat produced a lull, and while the uncle received thanks in one\r\ncorner, the aunt had some little confidences made to her in the other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, dear, and how are things going with you now? Better, I hope, than\r\nthey were a week ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Jessie, I think I'm going to be very happy, now uncle has come.\r\nHe does the queerest things, but he is so good to me I can't help\r\nloving him\xe2\x80\x9d; and, nestling closer to little Mum, Rose told all that had\r\nhappened, ending with a rapturous account of the splendid box.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad, dear. But, Rose, I must warn you of one thing; don't\r\nlet uncle spoil you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I like to be spoilt, auntie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't doubt it; but if you turn out badly when the year is over he\r\nwill be blamed, and his experiment prove a failure. That would be a\r\npity, wouldn't it? when he wants to do so much for you, and can do it if\r\nhis kind heart does not get in the way of his good judgment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thought of that, and I'll try not to be spoilt. But how can I\r\nhelp it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy not complaining of the wholesome things he wants you to do; by\r\ngiving him cheerful obedience as well as love; and even making some\r\nsmall sacrifices for his sake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will, I truly will! and when I get in a worry about things may I come\r\nto you? Uncle told me to, and I feel as if I shouldn't be afraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may, darling; this is the place where little troubles are best\r\ncured, and this is what mothers are for, I fancy\xe2\x80\x9d; and Aunt Jessie drew\r\nthe curly head to her shoulder with a tender look that proved how well\r\nshe knew what medicine the child most needed.\r\n\r\nIt was so sweet and comfortable that Rose sat still enjoying it till a\r\nlittle voice said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma, don't you think Pokey would like some of my shells? Rose gave\r\nPhebe some of her nice things, and it was very good of her. Can I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is Pokey?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, popping up her head, attracted by the odd\r\nname.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dolly; do you want to see her?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jamie, who had been much\r\nimpressed by the tale of adoption he had overheard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I'm fond of dollies, only don't tell the boys, or they will laugh\r\nat me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey don't laugh at me, and they play with my dolly a great deal; but\r\nshe likes me best\xe2\x80\x9d; and Jamie ran away to produce his pet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI brought my old doll, but I keep her hidden because I am too big to\r\nplay with her, and yet I can't bear to throw her away, I'm so fond of\r\nher,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, continuing her confidences in a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can come and play with Jamie's whenever you like, for we believe in\r\ndollies up here,\xe2\x80\x9d began Aunt Jessie, smiling to herself as if something\r\namused her.\r\n\r\nJust then Jamie came back, and Rose understood the smile, for his dolly\r\nproved to be a pretty four-year-old little girl, who trotted in as fast\r\nas her fat legs would carry her, and making straight for the shells,\r\nscrambled up an armful, saying, with a laugh that showed her little\r\nwhite teeth,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll for Dimmy and me, for Dimmy and me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's my dolly; isn't she a nice one?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jamie, proudly surveying\r\nhis pet with his hands behind him and his short legs rather far apart a\r\nmanly attitude copied from his brothers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is a dear dolly. But why call her Pokey?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, charmed with\r\nthe new plaything.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is such an inquisitive little body she is always poking that mite\r\nof a nose into everything; and as Paul Pry did not suit, the boys fell\r\nto calling her Pokey. Not a pretty name, but very expressive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt certainly was, for, having examined the shells, the busy tot laid\r\nhold of everything she could find, and continued her researches till\r\nArchie caught her sucking his carved ivory chessmen to see if they were\r\nnot barley sugar. Rice paper pictures were also discovered crumpled up\r\nin her tiny pocket, and she nearly smashed Will's ostrich egg by trying\r\nto sit upon it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, Jim, take her away; she's worse than the puppies, and we can't\r\nhave her round,\xe2\x80\x9d commanded the elder brother, picking her up and handing\r\nher over to the little fellow, who received her with open arms and the\r\nwarning remark,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou'd better mind what you do, for I'm going to 'dopt Pokey like\r\nRose did Phebe, and then you'll have to be very good to her, you big\r\nfellows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c'Dopt away, baby, and I'll give you a cage to keep her in, or you won't\r\nhave her long, for she is getting worse than a monkey\xe2\x80\x9d; and Archie went\r\nback to his mates, while Aunt Jessie, foreseeing a crisis, proposed that\r\nJamie should take his dolly home, as she was borrowed, and it was time\r\nher visit ended.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dolly is better than yours, isn't she? 'cause she can walk and talk\r\nand sing and dance, and yours can't do anything, can she?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jamie\r\nwith pride, as he regarded his Pokey, who just then had been moved to\r\nexecute a funny little jig and warble the well-known couplet,\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c'Puss-tat, puss-tat, where you been?'\r\n     'I been Lunnin, to saw a Tween.\xe2\x80\x9d'\r\n\r\nAfter which superb display she retired, escorted by Jamie, both making a\r\nfearful din blowing on conch shells.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must tear ourselves away, Rose, because I want to get you home\r\nbefore sunset. Will you come for a drive, Jessie?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, as the\r\nmusic died away in the distance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, thank you; but I see the boys want a scamper, so, if you don't\r\nmind, they may escort you home, but not go in. That is only allowed on\r\nholidays.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe words were hardly out of Aunt Jessie's mouth when Archie said, in a\r\ntone of command,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPass the word, lads. Boot and saddle, and be quick about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right!\xe2\x80\x9d And in a moment not a vestige of boy remained but the\r\nlitter on the floor.\r\n\r\nThe cavalcade went down the hill at a pace that made Rose cling to her\r\nuncle's arm, for the fat old horses got excited by the antics of the\r\nponies careering all about them, and went as fast as they could pelt,\r\nwith the gay dog-cart rattling in front, for Archie and Charlie scorned\r\nshelties since this magnificent equipage had been set up. Ben enjoyed\r\nthe fun, and the lads cut up capers till Rose declared that \xe2\x80\x9ccircus\xe2\x80\x9d was\r\nthe proper name for them after all.\r\n\r\nWhen they reached the house they dismounted, and stood, three on each\r\nside the steps, in martial attitudes, while her ladyship was handed out\r\nwith great elegance by Uncle Alec. Then the Clan saluted, mounted at\r\nword of command, and with a wild whoop tore down the avenue in what they\r\nconsidered the true Arab style.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was splendid, now it is safely ended,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, skipping up\r\nthe steps with her head over her shoulder to watch the dear tassels bob\r\nabout.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall get you a pony as soon as you are a little stronger,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr.\r\nAlec, watching her with a smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I couldn't ride one of those horrid, frisky little beasts! They\r\nroll their eyes and bounce about so, I should die of fright,\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nRose, clasping her hands tragically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you a coward?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout horses I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, then; come and see my new room\xe2\x80\x9d; and he led the way\r\nupstairs without another word.\r\n\r\nAs Rose followed she remembered her promise to Aunt Jessie, and was\r\nsorry she had objected so decidedly. She was a great deal more sorry\r\nfive minutes later, and well she might be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, take a good look, and tell me what you think of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr.\r\nAlec, opening the door and letting her enter before him, while Phebe was\r\nseen whisking down the backstairs with a dust-pan.\r\n\r\nRose walked to the middle of the room, stood still, and gazed about her\r\nwith eyes that brightened as they looked, for all was changed.\r\n\r\nThis chamber had been built out over the library to suit some fancy, and\r\nhad been unused for years, except at Christmas times, when the old house\r\noverflowed. It had three windows one to the east, that overlooked the\r\nbay; one to the south, where the horse-chestnuts waved their green\r\nfans; and one to the west, towards the hill and the evening sky. A ruddy\r\nsunset burned there now, filling the room with an enchanted glow; the\r\nsoft murmur of the sea was heard, and a robin chirped \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n among the budding trees.\r\n\r\nRose saw and heard these things first, and felt their beauty with a\r\nchild's quick instinct; then her eye took in the altered aspect of the\r\nroom, once so shrouded, still and solitary, now so full of light and\r\nwarmth and simple luxury.\r\n\r\nIndia matting covered the floor, with a gay rug here and there;\r\nthe antique andirons shone on the wide hearth, where a cheery blaze\r\ndispelled the dampness of the long-closed room. Bamboo lounges and\r\nchairs stood about, and quaint little tables in cosy corners; one\r\nbearing a pretty basket, one a desk, and on a third lay several\r\nfamiliar-looking books. In a recess stood a narrow white bed, with a\r\nlovely Madonna hanging over it. The Japanese screen half-folded back\r\nshowed a delicate toilet service of blue and white set forth on a marble\r\nslab, and near by was the great bath-pan, with Turkish towels and a\r\nsponge as big as Rose's head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle must love cold water like a duck,\xe2\x80\x9d she thought, with a shiver.\r\n\r\nThen her eye went on to the tall cabinet, where a half-open door\r\nrevealed a tempting array of the drawers, shelves and \xe2\x80\x9ccubby holes,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n which so delight the hearts of children.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a grand place for my new things,\xe2\x80\x9d she thought, wondering what her\r\nuncle kept in that cedar retreat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh me, what a sweet toilet table!\xe2\x80\x9d was her next mental exclamation, as\r\nshe approached this inviting spot.\r\n\r\nA round old-fashioned mirror hung over it, with a gilt eagle a-top,\r\nholding in his beak the knot of blue ribbon that tied up a curtain\r\nof muslin falling on either side of the table, where appeared little\r\nivory-handled brushes, two slender silver candle-sticks, a porcelain\r\nmatch-box, several pretty trays for small matters, and, most imposing of\r\nall, a plump blue silk cushion, coquettishly trimmed with lace, and pink\r\nrose-buds at the corners.\r\n\r\nThat cushion rather astonished Rose; in fact, the whole table did, and\r\nshe was just thinking, with a sly smile,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle is a dandy, but I never should have guessed it,\xe2\x80\x9d when he opened\r\nthe door of a large closet, saying, with a careless wave of the hand,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMen like plenty of room for their rattle-traps; don't you think that\r\nought to satisfy me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose peeped in and gave a start, though all she saw was what one usually\r\nfinds in closets clothes and boots, boxes and bags. Ah! but you see\r\nthese clothes were small black and white frocks; the row of little boots\r\nthat stood below had never been on Dr. Alec's feet; the green bandbox\r\nhad a gray veil straying out of it, and yes! the bag hanging on the door\r\nwas certainly her own piece-bag, with a hole in one corner. She gave\r\na quick look round the room and understood now why it had seemed too\r\ndainty for a man, why her Testament and Prayer Book were on the table\r\nby the bed, and what those rose-buds meant on the blue cushion. It came\r\nupon her in one delicious burst that this little paradise was all for\r\nher, and, not knowing how else to express her gratitude, she caught Dr.\r\nAlec round the neck, saying impetuously,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO uncle, you are too good to me! I'll do anything you ask me; ride wild\r\nhorses and take freezing baths and eat bad-tasting messes, and let my\r\nclothes hang on me, to show how much I thank you for this dear, sweet,\r\nlovely room!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou like it, then? But why do you think it is yours, my lass?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nDr. Alec, as he sat down looking well pleased, and drew his excited\r\nlittle niece to his knee.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't think, I know it is for me; I see it in your face, and I feel\r\nas if I didn't half deserve it. Aunt Jessie said you would spoil me, and\r\nI must not let you. I'm afraid this looks like it, and perhaps oh me!\r\nperhaps I ought not to have this beautiful room after all!\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose\r\ntried to look as if she could be heroic enough to give it up if it was\r\nbest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI owe Mrs. Jessie one for that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, trying to frown, though\r\nin his secret soul he felt that she was quite right. Then he smiled that\r\ncordial smile, which was like sunshine on his brown face, as he said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is part of the cure, Rose, and I put you here that you might take\r\nmy three great remedies in the best and easiest way. Plenty of sun,\r\nfresh air, and cold water; also cheerful surroundings, and some work;\r\nfor Phebe is to show you how to take care of this room, and be your\r\nlittle maid as well as friend and teacher. Does that sound hard and\r\ndisagreeable to you, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir; very, very pleasant, and I'll do my best to be a good patient.\r\nBut I really don't think anyone could be sick in this delightful\r\nroom,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a long sigh of happiness as her eye went from one\r\npleasant object to another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you like my sort of medicine better than Aunt Myra's, and don't\r\nwant to throw it out of the window, hey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 7--A Trip to China\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, little girl, I've got another dose for you. I fancy you won't\r\ntake it as well as you did the last, but you will like it better after a\r\nwhile,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, about a week after the grand surprise.\r\n\r\nRose was sitting in her pretty room, where she would gladly have spent\r\nall her time if it had been allowed; but she looked up with a smile, for\r\nshe had ceased to fear her uncle's remedies, and was always ready to try\r\na new one. The last had been a set of light gardening tools, with which\r\nshe had helped him put the flower-beds in order, learning all sorts of\r\nnew and pleasant things about the plants as she worked, for, though she\r\nhad studied botany at school, it seemed very dry stuff compared with\r\nUncle Alec's lively lesson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it now?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, shutting her work-box without a murmur.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSalt-water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow must I take it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPut on the new suit Miss Hemming sent home yesterday, and come down to\r\nthe beach; then I'll show you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose obediently, adding to herself, with a shiver,\r\nas he went off: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is too early for bathing, so I know it is something\r\nto do with a dreadful boat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPutting on the new suit of blue flannel, prettily trimmed with white,\r\nand the little sailor-hat with long streamers, diverted her mind from\r\nthe approaching trial, till a shrill whistle reminded her that her uncle\r\nwas waiting. Away she ran through the garden, down the sandy path, out\r\nupon the strip of beach that belonged to the house, and here she found\r\nDr. Alec busy with a slender red and white boat that lay rocking on the\r\nrising tide.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a dear little boat; and 'Bonnie Belle' is a pretty name,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, trying not to show how nervous she felt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is for you; so sit in the stern and learn to steer, till you are\r\nready to learn to row.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo all boats wiggle about in that way?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, lingering as if to\r\ntie her hat more firmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, pitch about like nutshells when the sea is a bit rough,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n answered her sailor uncle, never guessing her secret woe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it rough to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot very; it looks a trifle squally to the eastward, but we are all\r\nright till the wind changes. Come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you swim, uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, clutching at his arm as he took her\r\nhand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike a fish. Now then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, please hold me very tight till I get there! Why do you have the\r\nstern so far away?\xe2\x80\x9d and, stifling several squeaks of alarm in her\r\npassage, Rose crept to the distant seat, and sat there holding on with\r\nboth hands and looking as if she expected every wave to bring a sudden\r\nshipwreck.\r\n\r\nUncle Alec took no notice of her fear, but patiently instructed her in\r\nthe art of steering, till she was so absorbed in remembering which was\r\nstarboard and which larboard, that she forgot to say \xe2\x80\x9cOW!\xe2\x80\x9d every time a\r\nbig wave slapped against the boat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow where shall we go?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, as the wind blew freshly in her\r\nface, and a few, long swift strokes sent them half across the little\r\nbay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose we go to China?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn't that rather a long voyage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot as I go. Steer round the Point into the harbour, and I'll give you\r\na glimpse of China in twenty minutes or so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like that!\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose sat wondering what he meant, while she\r\nenjoyed the new sights all about her.\r\n\r\nBehind them the green Aunt-hill sloped gently upward to the grove at\r\nthe top, and all along the seaward side stood familiar houses, stately,\r\ncosy, or picturesque. As they rounded the Point, the great bay opened\r\nbefore them full of shipping, and the city lay beyond, its spires rising\r\nabove the tall masts with their gay streamers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we going there?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, for she had never seen this aspect of\r\nthe rich and busy old city before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Uncle Mac has a ship just in from Hong Kong, and I thought you\r\nwould like to go and see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I should. I love dearly to go poking about in the warehouses with\r\nUncle Mac; everything is so curious and new to me; and I'm specially\r\ninterested in China because you have been there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll show you two genuine Chinamen who have just arrived. You will like\r\nto welcome Whang Lo and Fun See, I'm sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't ask me to speak to them, uncle; I shall be sure to laugh at the\r\nodd names and the pig-tails and the slanting eyes. Please let me just\r\ntrot round after you; I like that best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; now steer toward the wharf where the big ship with the queer\r\nflag is. That's the 'Rajah,' and we will go aboard if we can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn among the ships they went, by the wharves where the water was green\r\nand still, and queer barnacles grew on the slippery piles. Odd smells\r\nsaluted her nose, and odd sights met her eyes, but Rose liked it all,\r\nand played she was really landing in Hong Kong when they glided up to\r\nthe steps in the shadow of the tall \xe2\x80\x9cRajah.\xe2\x80\x9d Boxes and bales were rising\r\nout of the hold and being carried into the warehouse by stout porters,\r\nwho tugged and bawled and clattered about with small trucks, or worked\r\ncranes with iron claws that came down and clutched heavy weights,\r\nwhisking them aloft to where wide doors like mouths swallowed them up.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec took her aboard the ship, and she had the satisfaction of\r\npoking her inquisitive little nose into every available corner, at the\r\nrisk of being crushed, lost, or drowned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, child, how would you like to take a voyage round the world with\r\nme in a jolly old craft like this?\xe2\x80\x9d asked her uncle, as they rested a\r\nminute in the captain's cabin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see the world, but not in such a small, untidy, smelly\r\nplace as this. We would go in a yacht all clean and comfortable;\r\nCharlie says that is the proper way,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, surveying the close\r\nquarters with little favour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not a true Campbell if you don't like the smell of tar and\r\nsalt-water, nor Charlie either, with his luxurious yacht. Now come\r\nashore and chin-chin with the Celestials.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a delightful progress through the great warehouse, peeping and\r\npicking as they went, they found Uncle Mac and the yellow gentlemen in\r\nhis private room, where samples, gifts, curiosities, and newly arrived\r\ntreasures of all sorts were piled up in pleasing pro-fusion and\r\ncon-fusion.\r\n\r\nAs soon as possible Rose retired to a corner, with a porcelain god\r\non one side, a green dragon on the other, and, what was still more\r\nembarrassing, Fun See sat on a tea-chest in front, and stared at her\r\nwith his beady black eyes till she did not know where to look.\r\n\r\nMr. Whang Lo was an elderly gentleman in American costume, with his\r\npig-tail neatly wound round his head. He spoke English, and was talking\r\nbusily with Uncle Mac in the most commonplace way so Rose considered him\r\na failure. But Fun See was delightfully Chinese from his junk-like shoes\r\nto the button on his pagoda hat; for he had got himself up in style, and\r\nwas a mass of silk jackets and slouchy trousers. He was short and fat,\r\nand waddled comically; his eyes were very \xe2\x80\x9cslanting,\xe2\x80\x9d as Rose said; his\r\nqueue was long, so were his nails; his yellow face was plump and shiny,\r\nand he was altogether a highly satisfactory Chinaman.\r\n\r\nUncle Alec told her that Fun See had come out to be educated and could\r\nonly speak a little pigeon English; so she must be kind to the poor\r\nfellow, for he was only a lad, though he looked nearly as old as Mr.\r\nWhang Lo. Rose said she would be kind; but had not the least idea how to\r\nentertain the queer guest, who looked as if he had walked out of one of\r\nthe rice-paper landscapes on the wall, and sat nodding at her so like a\r\ntoy Mandarin that she could hardly keep sober.\r\n\r\nIn the midst of her polite perplexity, Uncle Mac saw the two young\r\npeople gazing wistfully at one another, and seemed to enjoy the joke\r\nof this making acquaintance under difficulties. Taking a box from his\r\ntable, he gave it to Fun See, with an order that seemed to please him\r\nvery much.\r\n\r\nDescending from his perch, he fell to unpacking it with great neatness\r\nand despatch, while Rose watched him, wondering what was going to\r\nhappen. Presently, out from the wrappings came a teapot, which caused\r\nher to clasp her hands with delight, for it was made in the likeness of\r\na plump little Chinaman. His hat was the cover, his queue the handle,\r\nand his pipe the nose. It stood upon feet in shoes turned up at the\r\ntoes, and the smile on the fat, sleepy face was so like that on Fun's\r\nwhen he displayed the teapot, that Rose couldn't help laughing, which\r\npleased him much.\r\n\r\nTwo pretty cups with covers, and a fine scarlet tray completed the\r\nset, and made one long to have a \xe2\x80\x9cdish of tea,\xe2\x80\x9d even in Chinese style,\r\nwithout cream or sugar.\r\n\r\nWhen he had arranged them on a little table before her, Fun signified in\r\npantomime that they were hers, from her uncle. She returned her thanks\r\nin the same way, whereupon he returned to his tea-chest, and, having\r\nno other means of communication, they sat smiling and nodding at one\r\nanother in an absurd sort of way till a new idea seemed to strike\r\nFun. Tumbling off his seat, he waddled away as fast as his petticoats\r\npermitted, leaving Rose hoping that he had not gone to get a roasted\r\nrat, a stewed puppy, or any other foreign mess which civility would\r\noblige her to eat.\r\n\r\nWhile she waited for her funny new friend, she improved her mind in a\r\nway that would have charmed Aunt Jane. The gentlemen were talking over\r\nall sorts of things, and she listened attentively, storing up much\r\nof what she heard, for she had an excellent memory, and longed to\r\ndistinguish herself by being able to produce some useful information\r\nwhen reproached with her ignorance.\r\n\r\nShe was just trying to impress upon her mind that Amoy was two hundred\r\nand eighty miles from Hong Kong, when Fun came scuffling back, bearing\r\nwhat she thought was a small sword, till he unfurled an immense fan, and\r\npresented it with a string of Chinese compliments, the meaning of\r\nwhich would have amused her even more than the sound, if she could have\r\nunderstood it.\r\n\r\nShe had never seen such an astonishing fan, and at once became absorbed\r\nin examining it. Of course, there was no perspective whatever, which\r\nonly gave it a peculiar charm to Rose, for in one place a lovely lady,\r\nwith blue knitting-needles in her hair, sat directly upon the spire of a\r\nstately pagoda. In another charming view a brook appeared to flow in at\r\nthe front door of a stout gentleman's house, and out at his chimney. In\r\na third a zig-zag wall went up into the sky like a flash of lightning,\r\nand a bird with two tails was apparently brooding over a fisherman whose\r\nboat was just going aground upon the moon.\r\n\r\nIt was altogether a fascinating thing, and she would have sat wafting it\r\nto and fro all the afternoon, to Fun's great satisfaction, if Dr. Alec's\r\nattention had not suddenly been called to her by a breeze from the big\r\nfan that blew his hair into his eyes, and reminded him that they must\r\ngo. So the pretty china was repacked, Rose furled her fan, and with\r\nseveral parcels of choice teas for the old ladies stowed away in Dr.\r\nAlec's pockets, they took their leave, after Fun had saluted them with\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe three bendings and the nine knockings,\xe2\x80\x9d as they salute the Emperor,\r\nor \xe2\x80\x9cSon of Heaven,\xe2\x80\x9d at home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel as if I had really been to China, and I'm sure I look so,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nRose, as they glided out of the shadow of the \xe2\x80\x9cRajah.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe certainly did, for Mr. Whang Lo had given her a Chinese umbrella;\r\nUncle Alec had got some lanterns to light up her balcony; the great fan\r\nlay in her lap, and the tea-set reposed at her feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is not a bad way to study geography, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked her uncle, who\r\nhad observed her attention to the talk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a very pleasant way, and I really think I have learned more about\r\nChina to-day than in all the lessons I had at school, though I used to\r\nrattle off the answers as fast as I could go. No one explained anything\r\nto us, so all I remember is that tea and silk come from there, and the\r\nwomen have little bits of feet. I saw Fun looking at mine, and he must\r\nhave thought them perfectly immense,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, surveying her stout\r\nboots with sudden contempt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will have out the maps and the globe, and I'll show you some of my\r\njourneys, telling stories as we go. That will be next best to doing it\r\nactually.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are so fond of travelling, I should think it would be very dull for\r\nyou here, uncle. Do you know, Aunt Plenty says she is sure you will be\r\noff in a year or two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, me! what shall I do then?\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Rose, in a tone of despair that\r\nmade Uncle Alec's face brighten with a look of genuine pleasure as he\r\nsaid significantly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNext time I go I shall take my little anchor with me. How will that\r\nsuit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, niece.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose gave a little bounce of rapture which caused the boat to \xe2\x80\x9cwiggle\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n in a way that speedily quieted her down. But she sat beaming joyfully\r\nand trying to think which of some hundred questions she would ask first,\r\nwhen Dr. Alec said, pointing to a boat that was coming up behind them in\r\ngreat style,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow well those fellows row! Look at them, and take notes for your own\r\nuse by and by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cStormy Petrel\xe2\x80\x9d was manned by half a dozen jaunty looking sailors,\r\nwho made a fine display of blue shirts and shiny hats, with stars and\r\nanchors in every direction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow beautifully they go, and they are only boys. Why, I do believe they\r\nare our boys! Yes, I see Charlie laughing over his shoulder. Row, uncle,\r\nrow! Oh, please do, and not let them catch up with us!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, in\r\nsuch a state of excitement that the new umbrella nearly went overboard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, here we go!\xe2\x80\x9d and away they did go with a long steady sweep\r\nof the oars that carried the \xe2\x80\x9cBonnie Belle\xe2\x80\x9d through the water with a\r\nrush.\r\n\r\nThe lads pulled their prettiest, but Dr. Alec would have reached the\r\nPoint first, if Rose, in her flurry, had not retarded him by jerking the\r\nrudder ropes in a most unseamanlike way, and just as she got right again\r\nher hat blew off. That put an end to the race, and while they were still\r\nfishing for the hat the other boat came alongside, with all the oars in\r\nthe air, and the jolly young tars ready for a frolic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you catch a crab, uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, a blue-fish,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, as the dripping hat was landed on a seat\r\nto dry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you been doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeeing Fun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood for you, Rose! I know what you mean. We are going to have him up\r\nto show us how to fly the big kite, for we can't get the hang of it.\r\nIsn't he great fun, though?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, little Fun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, stop joking, and show us what you've got.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou'd better hoist that fan for a sail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLend Dandy your umbrella; he hates to burn his pretty nose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, uncle, are you going to have a Feast of Lanterns?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I'm going to have a feast of bread and butter, for it's tea-time.\r\nIf that black cloud doesn't lie, we shall have a gust before long,\r\nso you had better get home as soon as you can, or your mother will be\r\nanxious, Archie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, ay, skipper. Good-night, Rose; come out often, and we'll teach you\r\nall there is to know about rowing,\xe2\x80\x9d was Charlie's modest invitation.\r\n\r\nThen the boats parted company, and across the water from the \xe2\x80\x9cPetrel's\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n crew came a verse from one of the Nonsense songs in which the boys\r\ndelighted.\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Timballoo! how happy we are,\r\n     We live in a sieve and a crockery jar!\r\n     And all night long, in the starlight pale,\r\n     We sail away, with a pea-green sail,\r\n     And whistle and warble a moony song\r\n     To the echoing sound of a coppery gong.\r\n     Far and few, far and few\r\n     Are the lands where the Jumblies live;\r\n     Their heads are green, and their hands are blue,\r\n     And they went to sea in a sieve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 8--And what came of it\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle, could you lend me a ninepence? I'll return it as soon as I get\r\nmy pocket-money,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, coming into the library in a great hurry\r\nthat evening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I could, and I won't charge any interest for it, so you need\r\nnot be in any hurry to repay me. Come back here and help me settle these\r\nbooks if you have nothing pleasanter to do,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Alec, handing\r\nout the money with that readiness which is so delightful when we ask\r\nsmall loans.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll come in a minute; I've been longing to fix my books, but didn't\r\ndare to touch them, because you always shake your head when I read.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall shake my head when you write, if you don't do it better than\r\nyou did in making out this catalogue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it's bad, but I was in a hurry when I did it, and I am in one\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d And away went Rose, glad to escape a lecture.\r\n\r\nBut she got it when she came back, for Uncle Alec was still knitting\r\nhis brows over the list of books, and sternly demanded, pointing to a\r\ntipsy-looking title staggering down the page,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that meant for 'Pulverized Bones,' ma'am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir; it's 'Paradise Lost.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I'm glad to know it, for I began to think you were planning to\r\nstudy surgery or farming. And what is this, if you please? 'Babies'\r\nAprons' is all I can make of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose looked hard at the scrawl, and presently announced, with an air of\r\nsuperior wisdom,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that's 'Bacon's Essays.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Power did not teach anything so old-fashioned as writing, I see.\r\nNow look at this memorandum Aunt Plenty gave me, and see what a handsome\r\nplain hand that is. She went to a dame-school and learnt a few useful\r\nthings well; that is better than a smattering of half a dozen so-called\r\nhigher branches, I take the liberty of thinking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I'm sure I was considered a bright girl at school, and learned\r\neverything I was taught. Luly and me were the first in all our classes,\r\nand 'specially praised for our French and music and those sort of\r\nthings,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, rather offended at Uncle Alec's criticism.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare say; but if your French grammar was no better than your English,\r\nI think the praise was not deserved, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, uncle, we did study English grammar, and I could parse\r\nbeautifully. Miss Power used to have us up to show off when people came.\r\nI don't see but I talk as right as most girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare say you do, but we are all too careless about our English. Now,\r\nthink a minute, and tell me if these expressions are correct 'Luly and\r\nme,' 'those sort of things,' and 'as right as most girls.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose pulled her pet curl and put up her lip, but had to own that she was\r\nwrong, and said meekly, after a pause which threatened to be sulky,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I should have said 'Luly and I,' in that case, and 'that sort\r\nof things' and 'rightly,' though 'correctly' would have been a better\r\nword, I guess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you; and if you will kindly drop 'I guess,' I shall like my\r\nlittle Yankee all the better. Now, see here, Rosy, I don't pretend\r\nto set myself up for a model in anything, and you may come down on my\r\ngrammar, manners or morals as often as you think I'm wrong, and I'll\r\nthank you. I've been knocking about the world for years, and have got\r\ncareless, but I want my girl to be what I call well-educated, even if\r\nshe studies nothing but the three 'Rs' for a year to come. Let us be\r\nthorough, no matter how slowly we go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe spoke so earnestly and looked so sorry to have ruffled her that\r\nRose went and sat on the arm of his chair, saying, with a pretty air of\r\npenitence,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm sorry I was cross, uncle, when I ought to thank you for taking\r\nso much interest in me. I guess no, I think you are right about being\r\nthorough, for I used to understand a great deal better when papa taught\r\nme a few lessons than when Miss Power hurried me through so many. I\r\ndeclare my head used to be such a jumble of French and German, history\r\nand arithmetic, grammar and music, I used to feel sometimes as if it\r\nwould split. I'm sure I don't wonder it ached.\xe2\x80\x9d And she held on to it as\r\nif the mere memory of the \xe2\x80\x9cjumble\xe2\x80\x9d made it swim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet that is considered an excellent school, I find, and I dare say it\r\nwould be if the benighted lady did not think it necessary to cram her\r\npupils like Thanks-giving turkeys, instead of feeding them in a natural\r\nand wholesome way. It is the fault with most American schools, and the\r\npoor little heads will go on aching till we learn better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was one of Dr. Alec's hobbies, and Rose was afraid he was off for\r\na gallop, but he reined himself in and gave her thoughts a new turn by\r\nsaying suddenly, as he pulled out a fat pocket-book,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle Mac has put all your affairs into my hands now, and here is your\r\nmonth's pocket money. You keep your own little accounts, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. Yes, Uncle Mac gave me an account book when I went to\r\nschool, and I used to put down my expenses, but I couldn't make them go\r\nvery well, for figures are the one thing I am not at all clever about,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Rose, rummaging in her desk for a dilapidated little book, which\r\nshe was ashamed to show when she found it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, as figures are rather important things to most of us, and you may\r\nhave a good many accounts to keep some day, wouldn't it be wise to\r\nbegin at once and learn to manage your pennies before the pounds come to\r\nperplex you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you would do all that fussy part and take care of the pounds,\r\nas you call them. Need I worry about it? I do hate sums, so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall take care of things till you are of age, but I mean that you\r\nshall know how your property is managed, and do as much of it as you can\r\nby and by; then you won't be dependent on the honesty of other people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGracious me! as if I wouldn't trust you with millions of billions if I\r\nhad them,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, scandalised at the mere suggestion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but I might be tempted; guardians are sometimes; so you'd better\r\nkeep your eye on me, and in order to do that you must learn all about\r\nthese affairs,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Alec, as he made an entry in his own very\r\nneat account-book.\r\n\r\nRose peeped over his shoulder at it, and then turned to the arithmetical\r\npuzzle in her hand with a sigh of despair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle, when you add up your expenses do you ever find you have got more\r\nmoney than you had in the beginning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I usually find that I have a good deal less than I had in the\r\nbeginning. Are you troubled in the peculiar way you mention?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; it is very curious, but I never can make things come out square.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I can help you,\xe2\x80\x9d began Uncle Alec, in the most respectful tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you had better, for if I have got to keep accounts I may as\r\nwell begin in the right way. But please don't laugh! I know I'm very\r\nstupid, and my book is a disgrace, but I never could get it straight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n And with great trepidation, Rose gave up her funny little accounts.\r\n\r\nIt really was good in Dr. Alec not to laugh, and Rose felt deeply\r\ngrateful when he said in a mildly suggestive tone,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe dollars and cents seem to be rather mixed, perhaps if I just\r\nstraightened them out a bit we should find things all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease do, and then show me on a fresh leaf how to make mine look nice\r\nand ship-shape as yours do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Rose stood by him watching the ease with which he quickly brought\r\norder out of chaos, she privately resolved to hunt up her old arithmetic\r\nand perfect herself in the four first rules, with a good tug at\r\nfractions, before she read any more fairy tales.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I a rich girl, uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked suddenly, as he was copying a\r\ncolumn of figures.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather a poor one, I should say, since you had to borrow a ninepence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was your fault, because you forgot my pocket-money. But, really,\r\nshall I be rich by and by?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid you will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy afraid, uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo much money is a bad thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I can give it away, you know; that is always the pleasantest part\r\nof having it I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm glad you feel so, for you can do much good with your fortune if you\r\nknow how to use it well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall teach me, and when I am a woman we will set up a school where\r\nnothing but the three R's shall be taught, and all the children live\r\non oatmeal, and the girls have waists a yard round,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, with a\r\nsudden saucy smile dimpling her cheeks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are an impertinent little baggage, to turn on me in that way right\r\nin the midst of my first attempt at teaching. Never mind, I'll have an\r\nextra bitter dose for you next time, miss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew you wanted to laugh, so I gave you a chance. Now, I will be\r\ngood, master, and do my lesson nicely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Dr. Alec had his laugh, and then Rose sat down and took a lesson in\r\naccounts which she never forgot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow come and read aloud to me; my eyes are tired, and it is pleasant to\r\nsit here by the fire while the rain pours outside and Aunt Jane lectures\r\nupstairs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Alec, when last month's accounts had been put in\r\ngood order and a fresh page neatly begun.\r\n\r\nRose liked to read aloud, and gladly gave him the chapter in \xe2\x80\x9cNicholas\r\nNickleby\xe2\x80\x9d where the Miss Kenwigses take their French lesson. She did her\r\nvery best, feeling that she was being criticised, and hoping that she\r\nmight not be found wanting in this as in other things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I go on, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked very meekly, when the chapter ended.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you are not tired, dear. It is a pleasure to hear you, for you read\r\nremarkably well,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer that filled her heart with pride and\r\npleasure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really think so, uncle? I'm so glad! Papa taught me, and I read\r\nfor hours to him, but I thought perhaps, he liked it because he was fond\r\nof me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I; but you really do read unusually well, and I'm very glad of\r\nit, for it is a rare accomplishment, and one I value highly. Come here\r\nin this cosy, low chair; the light is better, and I can pull these curls\r\nif you go too fast. I see you are going to be a great comfort as well\r\nas a great credit to your old uncle, Rosy.\xe2\x80\x9d And Dr. Alec drew her close\r\nbeside him with such a fatherly look and tone that she felt it would\r\nbe very easy to love and obey him, since he knew how to mix praise and\r\nblame so pleasantly together.\r\n\r\nAnother chapter was just finished, when the sound of a carriage warned\r\nthem that Aunt Jane was about to depart. Before they could go to meet\r\nher, however, she appeared in the doorway looking like an unusually tall\r\nmummy in her waterproof, with her glasses shining like cat's eyes from\r\nthe depths of the hood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust as I thought! petting that child to death and letting her sit up\r\nlate reading trash. I do hope you feel the weight of the responsibility\r\nyou have taken upon yourself, Alec,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a certain grim sort\r\nof satisfaction at seeing things go wrong.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I have a very realising sense of it, sister Jane,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nDr. Alec, with a comical shrug of the shoulders and a glance at Rose's\r\nbright face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is sad to see a great girl wasting these precious hours so. Now, my\r\nboys have studied all day, and Mac is still at his books, I've no doubt,\r\nwhile you have not had a lesson since you came, I suspect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've had five to-day, ma'am,\xe2\x80\x9d was Rose's very unexpected answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm glad to hear it; and what were they, pray?\xe2\x80\x9d Rose looked very demure\r\nas she replied,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNavigation, geography, grammar, arithmetic, and keeping my temper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQueer lessons, I fancy; and what have you learned from this remarkable\r\nmixture, I should like to know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA naughty sparkle came into Rose's eyes as she answered, with a droll\r\nlook at her uncle,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can't tell you all, ma'am, but I have collected some useful\r\ninformation about China, which you may like, especially the teas. The\r\nbest are Lapsing Souchong, Assam Pekoe, rare Ankoe, Flowery Pekoe,\r\nHowqua's mixture, Scented Caper, Padral tea, black Congou, and green\r\nTwankey. Shanghai is on the Woosung River. Hong Kong means 'Island of\r\nSweet waters.' Singapore is 'Lion's Town.' 'Chops' are the boats they\r\nlive in; and they drink tea out of little saucers. Principal productions\r\nare porcelain, tea, cinnamon, shawls, tin, tamarinds and opium. They\r\nhave beautiful temples and queer gods; and in Canton is the Dwelling of\r\nthe Holy Pigs, fourteen of them, very big, and all blind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe effect of this remarkable burst was immense, especially the fact\r\nlast mentioned. It entirely took the wind out of Aunt Jane's sails; it\r\nwas so sudden, so varied and unexpected, that she had not a word to say.\r\nThe glasses remained fixed full upon Rose for a moment, and then, with\r\na hasty \xe2\x80\x9cOh, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d the excellent lady bundled into her carriage and\r\ndrove away, somewhat bewildered and very much disturbed.\r\n\r\nShe would have been more so if she had seen her reprehensible\r\nbrother-in-law dancing a triumphal polka down the hall with Rose in\r\nhonour of having silenced the enemy's battery for once.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 9--Phebe's Secret\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you keep smiling to yourself, Phebe?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, as they were\r\nworking together one morning, for Dr. Alec considered house-work the\r\nbest sort of gymnastics for girls; so Rose took lessons of Phebe in\r\nsweeping, dusting and bed-making.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was thinking about a nice little secret I know, and couldn't help\r\nsmiling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I know it, sometime?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGuess you will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I like it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, won't you, though!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill it happen soon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometime this week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what it is! The boys are going to have fireworks on the fourth,\r\nand have got some surprise for me. Haven't they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's telling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can wait; only tell me one thing is uncle in it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course he is; there's never any fun without him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it's all right, and sure to be nice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose went out on the balcony to shake the rugs, and, having given them\r\na vigorous beating, hung them on the balustrade to air, while she took a\r\nlook at her plants. Several tall vases and jars stood there, and a month\r\nof June sun and rain had worked wonders with the seeds and slips she had\r\nplanted. Morning-glories and nasturtiums ran all over the bars, making\r\nhaste to bloom. Scarlet beans and honeysuckles were climbing up from\r\nbelow to meet their pretty neighbours, and the woodbine was hanging its\r\ngreen festoons wherever it could cling.\r\n\r\nThe waters of the bay were dancing in the sunshine, a fresh wind stirred\r\nthe chestnut-trees with a pleasant sound, and the garden below was full\r\nof roses, butterflies and bees. A great chirping and twittering went on\r\namong the birds, busy with their summer house-keeping, and, far away,\r\nthe white-winged gulls were dipping and diving in the sea, where ships,\r\nlike larger birds, went sailing to and fro.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Phebe, it's such a lovely day, I do wish your fine secret was going\r\nto happen right away! I feel just like having a good time; don't you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Rose, waving her arms as if she was going to fly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI often feel that way, but I have to wait for my good times, and don't\r\nstop working to wish for 'em. There, now you can finish as soon as the\r\ndust settles; I must go do my stairs,\xe2\x80\x9d and Phebe trudged away with the\r\nbroom, singing as she went.\r\n\r\nRose leaned where she was, and fell to thinking how many good times\r\nshe had had lately, for the gardening had prospered finely, and she was\r\nlearning to swim and row, and there were drives and walks, and quiet\r\nhours of reading and talk with Uncle Alec, and, best of all, the old\r\npain and ennui seldom troubled her now. She could work and play all\r\nday, sleep sweetly all night, and enjoy life with the zest of a healthy,\r\nhappy child. She was far from being as strong and hearty as Phebe, but\r\nshe was getting on; the once pale cheeks had colour in them now, the\r\nhands were growing plump and brown, and the belt was not much too loose.\r\nNo one talked to her about her health, and she forgot that she had \xe2\x80\x9cno\r\nconstitution.\xe2\x80\x9d She took no medicine but Dr. Alec's three great remedies,\r\nand they seemed to suit her excellently. Aunt Plenty said it was the\r\npills; but, as no second batch had ever followed the first, I think the\r\nold lady was mistaken.\r\n\r\nRose looked worthy of her name as she stood smiling to herself over\r\na happier secret than any Phebe had a secret which she did not know\r\nherself till she found out, some years later, the magic of good health.\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c'Look only,' said the brownie,\r\n     'At the pretty gown of blue,\r\n     At the kerchief pinned about her head,\r\n     And at her little shoe,\xe2\x80\x9d'\r\n\r\nsaid a voice from below, as a great cabbage-rose came flying against her\r\ncheek.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the princess dreaming about up there in her hanging-garden?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n added Dr. Alec as she flung back a morning-glory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was wishing I could do something pleasant this fine day; something\r\nvery new and interesting, for the wind makes me feel frisky and gay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose we take a pull over to the Island? I intended to go this\r\nafternoon; but if you feel more like it now, we can be off at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do! I do! I'll come in fifteen minutes, uncle. I must just scrabble\r\nmy room to rights, for Phebe has got a great deal to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose caught up the rugs and vanished as she spoke, while Dr. Alec went\r\nin, saying to himself, with an indulgent smile,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may upset things a trifle, but half a child's pleasure consists in\r\nhaving their fun when they want it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNever did duster flap more briskly than the one Rose used that day, and\r\nnever was a room \xe2\x80\x9cscrabbled\xe2\x80\x9d to rights in such haste as hers. Tables and\r\nchairs flew into their places as if alive; curtains shook as if a gale\r\nwas blowing; china rattled and small articles tumbled about as if a\r\nyoung earthquake was playing with them. The boating suit went on in a\r\ntwinkling, and Rose was off with a hop and a skip, little dreaming how\r\nmany hours it would be before she saw her pretty room again.\r\n\r\nUncle Alec was putting a large basket into the boat when she arrived,\r\nand before they were off Phebe came running down with a queer, knobby\r\nbundle done up in a water-proof.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can't eat half that luncheon, and I know we shall not need so many\r\nwraps. I wouldn't lumber the boat up so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, who still had\r\nsecret scares when on the water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn't you make a smaller parcel, Phebe?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Dr. Alec, eyeing the\r\nbundle suspiciously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, not in such a hurry,\xe2\x80\x9d and Phebe laughed as she gave a\r\nparticularly large knob a good poke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it will do for ballast. Don't forget the note to Mrs. Jessie, I\r\nbeg of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir. I'll send it right off,\xe2\x80\x9d and Phebe ran up the bank as if she\r\nhad wings to her feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe'll take a look at the lighthouse first, for you have not been there\r\nyet, and it is worth seeing. By the time we have done that it will be\r\npretty warm, and we will have lunch under the trees on the Island.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose was ready for anything, and enjoyed her visit to the lighthouse on\r\nthe Point very much, especially climbing up the narrow stairs and going\r\ninside the great lantern. They made a long stay, for Dr. Alec seemed in\r\nno hurry to go, and kept looking through his spy-glass as if he expected\r\nto discover something remarkable on sea or land. It was past twelve\r\nbefore they reached the Island, and Rose was ready for her lunch long\r\nbefore she got it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow this is lovely! I do wish the boys were here. Won't it be nice to\r\nhave them with us all their vacation? Why, it begins to-day, doesn't it?\r\nOh, I wish I'd remembered it sooner, and perhaps they would have come\r\nwith us,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as they lay luxuriously eating sandwiches under the\r\nold apple-tree.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo we might. Next time we won't be in such a hurry. I expect the lads\r\nwill take our heads off when they find us out,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Alec,\r\nplacidly drinking cold tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle, I smell a frying sort of a smell,\xe2\x80\x9d Rose said, pausing suddenly\r\nas she was putting away the remains of the lunch half an hour later.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo do I; it is fish, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment they both sat with their noses in the air, sniffing like\r\nhounds; then Dr. Alec sprang up, saying with great decision,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, this won't do! No one is permitted on this island without asking\r\nleave. I must see who dares to fry fish on my private property.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTaking the basket on one arm and the bundle on the other, he strode away\r\ntowards the traitorous smell, looking as fierce as a lion, while Rose\r\nmarched behind under her umbrella.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are Robinson Crusoe and his man Friday going to see if the savages\r\nhave come,\xe2\x80\x9d she said presently, for her fancy was full of the dear old\r\nstories that all children love so well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there they are! Two tents and two boats, as I live! These rascals\r\nmean to enjoy themselves, that's evident.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere ought to be more boats and no tents. I wonder where the prisoners\r\nare?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are traces of them,\xe2\x80\x9d and Dr. Alec pointed to the heads and tails\r\nof fishes strewn on the grass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there are more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, laughing, as she pointed to a scarlet\r\nheap of what looked like lobsters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe savages are probably eating their victims now; don't you hear the\r\nknives rattle in that tent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe ought to creep up and peep; Crusoe was cautious, you know, and\r\nFriday scared out of his wits,\xe2\x80\x9d added Rose, still keeping up the joke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this Crusoe is going to pounce upon them, regardless of\r\nconsequences. If I am killed and eaten, you seize the basket and run for\r\nthe boat; there are provisions enough for your voyage home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith that Uncle Alec slipped round to the front of the tent and, casting\r\nin the big bundle like a bomb-shell, roared out, in a voice of thunder,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPirates, surrender!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA crash, a shout, a laugh, and out came the savages, brandishing knives\r\nand forks, chicken bones, and tin mugs, and all fell upon the intruder,\r\npommelling him unmercifully as they cried,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou came too soon! We are not half ready! You've spoilt it all! Where\r\nis Rose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere I am,\xe2\x80\x9d answered a half-stifled voice, and Rose was discovered\r\nsitting on the pile of red flannel bathing clothes, which she had\r\nmistaken for lobsters, and where she had fallen in a fit of merriment\r\nwhen she discovered that the cannibals were her merry cousins.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou good-for-nothing boys! You are always bursting out upon me in some\r\nridiculous way, and I always get taken in because I'm not used to such\r\npranks. Uncle is as bad as the rest, and it's great fun,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nas the lads came round her, half scolding, half welcoming, and wholly\r\nenjoying the double surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were not to come till afternoon, and mamma was to be here to\r\nreceive you. Everything is in a mess now, except your tent; we got that\r\nin order the first thing, and you can sit there and see us work,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nArchie, doing the honours as usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRose felt it in her bones, as Dolly says, that something was in the\r\nwind, and wanted to be off at once. So I let her come, and should\r\nhave kept her away an hour longer if your fish had not betrayed you,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n explained Uncle Alec, subsiding from a ferocious Crusoe into his\r\ngood-natured self again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs this seat is rather damp, I think I'll rise,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, as the\r\nexcitement lessened a little.\r\n\r\nSeveral fishy hands helped her up, and Charlie said, as he scattered the\r\nscarlet garments over the grass with an oar,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe had a jolly good swim before dinner, and I told the Brats to spread\r\nthese to dry. Hope you brought your things, Rose, for you belong to the\r\nLobsters, you know, and we can have no end of fun teaching you to dive\r\nand float and tread water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn't bring anything--\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose, but was interrupted by the Brats\r\n(otherwise Will and Geordie), who appeared bearing the big bundle, so\r\nmuch demoralised by its fall that a red flannel tunic trailed out at one\r\nend and a little blue dressing-gown at the other, while the knobs proved\r\nto be a toilet-case, rubbers, and a silver mug.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that sly Phebe! This was the secret, and she bundled up those\r\nthings after I went down to the boat,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, with sparkling eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGuess something is smashed inside, for a bit of glass fell out,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n observed Will, as they deposited the bundle at her feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatch a girl going anywhere without a looking-glass. We haven't got one\r\namong the whole lot of us,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mac, with masculine scorn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDandy has; I caught him touching up his wig behind the trees after our\r\nswim,\xe2\x80\x9d cut in Geordie, wagging a derisive finger at Steve, who promptly\r\nsilenced him by a smart rap on the head with the drum-stick he had just\r\npolished off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, come, you lazy lubbers, fall to work, or we shall not be ready\r\nfor mamma. Take Rose's things to her tent, and tell her all about it,\r\nPrince. Mac and Steve, you cut away and bring up the rest of the straw;\r\nand you small chaps, clear off the table, if you have stuffed all you\r\ncan. Please, uncle, I'd like your advice about the boundary lines and\r\nthe best place for the kitchen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEveryone obeyed the chief, and Rose was escorted to her tent by Charlie,\r\nwho devoted himself to her service. She was charmed with her quarters,\r\nand still more so with the programme which he unfolded before her as\r\nthey worked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe always camp out somewhere in vacation, and this year we thought we'd\r\ntry the Island. It is handy, and our fireworks will show off well from\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we stay over the Fourth? Three whole days! Oh, me! what a frolic\r\nit will be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless your heart, we often camp for a week, we big fellows; but this\r\nyear the small chaps wanted to come, so we let them. We have great\r\nlarks, as you'll see; for we have a cave and play Captain Kidd, and have\r\nshipwrecks, and races, and all sorts of games. Arch and I are rather\r\npast that kind of thing now, but we do it to please the children,\xe2\x80\x9d added\r\nCharlie, with a sudden recollection of his sixteen years.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had no idea boys had such good times. Their plays never seemed a bit\r\ninteresting before. But I suppose that was because I never knew any boys\r\nvery well, or perhaps you are unusually nice ones,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Rose, with\r\nan artless air of appreciation that was very flattering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are a pretty clever set, I fancy; but we have a good many\r\nadvantages, you see. There are a tribe of us, to begin with; then our\r\nfamily has been here for ages, and we have plenty of 'spondulics,' so\r\nwe can rather lord it over the other fellows, and do as we like. There,\r\nma'am, you can hang your smashed glass on that nail and do up your back\r\nhair as fine as you please. You can have a blue blanket or a red one,\r\nand a straw pillow or an air cushion for your head, whichever you like.\r\nYou can trim up to any extent, and be as free and easy as squaws in a\r\nwigwam, for this corner is set apart for you ladies and we never cross\r\nthe line uncle is drawing until we ask leave. Anything more I can do for\r\nyou, cousin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, thank you. I think I'll leave the rest till auntie comes, and go\r\nand help you somewhere else, if I may.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed, come on and see to the kitchen. Can you cook?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nCharlie, as he led the way to the rocky nook where Archie was putting up\r\na sail-cloth awning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can make tea and toast bread.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we'll shew you how to fry fish, and make chowder. Now you just\r\nset these pots and pans round tastefully, and sort of tidy up a bit,\r\nfor Aunt Jessie insists on doing some of the work, and I want it to be\r\ndecent here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy four o'clock the camp was in order, and the weary workers settled\r\ndown on Lookout Rock to watch for Mrs. Jessie and Jamie, who was never\r\nfar from mamma's apron string. They looked like a flock of blue-birds,\r\nall being in sailor rig, with blue ribbon enough flying from the seven\r\nhats to have set up a milliner. Very tuneful blue-birds they were, too,\r\nfor all the lads sang, and the echo of their happy voices reached Mrs.\r\nJessie long before she saw them.\r\n\r\nThe moment the boat hove in sight up went the Island flag, and the\r\nblue-jackets cheered lustily, as they did on every possible occasion,\r\nlike true young Americans. This welcome was answered by the flapping of\r\na handkerchief and the shrill \xe2\x80\x9cRah! Rah!
