Rah!\xe2\x80\x9d of the one small tar\r\nwho stood in the stern waving his hat manfully, while a maternal hand\r\nclutched him firmly in the rear.\r\n\r\nCleopatra landing from her golden galley never received a heartier\r\ngreeting than \xe2\x80\x9cLittle Mum\xe2\x80\x9d as she was borne to her tent by the young\r\nfolk, for love of whom she smilingly resigned herself to three days of\r\ndiscomfort; while Jamie immediately attached himself to Rose, assuring\r\nher of his protection from the manifold perils which might assail them.\r\n\r\nTaught by long experience that boys are always hungry, Aunt Jessie soon\r\nproposed supper, and proceeded to get it, enveloped in an immense apron,\r\nwith an old hat of Archie's stuck atop of her cap. Rose helped, and\r\ntried to be as handy as Phebe, though the peculiar style of table she\r\nhad to set made it no easy task. It was accomplished at last, and a\r\nvery happy party lay about under the trees, eating and drinking out of\r\nanyone's plate and cup, and quite untroubled by the frequent appearance\r\nof ants and spiders in places which these interesting insects are not\r\nexpected to adorn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thought I should like to wash dishes, but I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose,\r\nas she sat in a boat after supper lazily rinsing plates in the sea, and\r\nrocking luxuriously as she wiped them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMum is mighty particular; we just give 'em a scrub with sand, and dust\r\n'em off with a bit of paper. It's much the best way, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\r\nGeordie, who reposed in another boat alongside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow Phebe would like this! I wonder uncle did not have her come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe he tried to, but Dolly was as cross as two sticks, and said\r\nshe couldn't spare her. I'm sorry, for we all like the Phebe bird, and\r\nshe'd chirp like a good one out here, wouldn't she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe ought to have a holiday like the rest of us. It's too bad to leave\r\nher out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis thought came back to Rose several times that evening, for Phebe\r\nwould have added much to the little concert they had in the moonlight,\r\nwould have enjoyed the stories told, been quick at guessing the\r\nconundrums, and laughed with all her heart at the fun. The merry going\r\nto bed would have been the best of all, for Rose wanted someone to\r\ncuddle under the blue blanket with her, there to whisper and giggle and\r\ntell secrets, as girls delight to do.\r\n\r\nLong after the rest were asleep, Rose lay wide awake, excited by the\r\nnovelty of all about her, and a thought that had come into her mind. Far\r\naway she heard a city clock strike twelve; a large star like a mild eye\r\npeeped in at the opening of the tent, and the soft plash of the waves\r\nseemed calling her to come out. Aunt Jessie lay fast asleep, with Jamie\r\nrolled up like a kitten at her feet, and neither stirred as Rose in her\r\nwrapper crept out to see how the world looked at midnight.\r\n\r\nShe found it very lovely, and sat down on a cracker keg to enjoy it with\r\na heart full of the innocent sentiment of her years. Fortunately, Dr.\r\nAlec saw her before she had time to catch cold, for coming out to tie\r\nback the door-flap of his tent for more air, he beheld the small\r\nfigure perched in the moonlight. Having no fear of ghosts, he quietly\r\napproached, and, seeing that she was wide awake, said, with a hand on\r\nher shining hair,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is my girl doing here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaving a good time,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, not at all startled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what she was thinking about with such a sober look.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe story you told of the brave sailor who gave up his place on the\r\nraft to the woman, and the last drop of water to the poor baby. People\r\nwho make sacrifices are very much loved and admired, aren't they?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked, earnestly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the sacrifice is a true one. But many of the bravest never are\r\nknown, and get no praise. That does not lessen their beauty, though\r\nperhaps it makes them harder, for we all like sympathy,\xe2\x80\x9d and Dr. Alec\r\nsighed a patient sort of sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you have made a great many? Would you mind telling me one of\r\nthem?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, arrested by the sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy last was to give up smoking,\xe2\x80\x9d was the very unromantic answer to her\r\npensive question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBad example for the boys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was very good of you, uncle! Was it hard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm ashamed to say it was. But as a wise old fellow once said, 'It is\r\nnecessary to do right; it is not necessary to be happy.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose pondered over the saying as if it pleased her, and then said, with\r\na clear, bright look,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA real sacrifice is giving up something you want or enjoy very much,\r\nisn't it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoing it one's own self because one loves another person very much and\r\nwants her to be happy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd doing it pleasantly, and being glad about it, and not minding the\r\npraise if it doesn't come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear, that is the true spirit of self-sacrifice; you seem to\r\nunderstand it, and I dare say you will have many chances in your life to\r\ntry the real thing. I hope they won't be very hard ones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think they will,\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose, and there stopped short.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, make one now, and go to sleep, or my girl will be ill to-morrow,\r\nand then the aunts will say camping out was bad for her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll go good night!\xe2\x80\x9d and throwing him a kiss, the little ghost\r\nvanished, leaving Uncle Alec to pace the shore and think about some of\r\nthe unsuspected sacrifices that had made him what he was.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 10--Rose's Sacrifice\r\n\r\nThere certainly were \xe2\x80\x9clarks\xe2\x80\x9d on Campbell's Island next day, as Charlie\r\nhad foretold, and Rose took her part in them like one intent on enjoying\r\nevery minute to the utmost. There was a merry breakfast, a successful\r\nfishing expedition, and then the lobsters came out in full force, for\r\neven Aunt Jessie appeared in red flannel. There was nothing Uncle Alec\r\ncould not do in the water, and the boys tried their best to equal him in\r\nstrength and skill, so there was a great diving and ducking, for every\r\none was bent on distinguishing himself.\r\n\r\nRose swam out far beyond her depth, with uncle to float her back; Aunt\r\nJessie splashed placidly in the shallow pools, with Jamie paddling near\r\nby like a little whale beside its mother; while the lads careered about,\r\nlooking like a flock of distracted flamingoes, and acting like the\r\nfamous dancing party in \xe2\x80\x9cAlice's Adventures in Wonderland.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNothing but chowder would have lured them from their gambols in the\r\nbriny deep; that time-honoured dish demanded the concentrated action\r\nof several mighty minds; so the \xe2\x80\x9cWater Babies\xe2\x80\x9d came ashore and fell to\r\ncooking.\r\n\r\nIt is unnecessary to say that, when done, it was the most remarkable\r\nchowder ever cooked, and the quantity eaten would have amazed the\r\nworld if the secret had been divulged. After this exertion a siesta\r\nwas considered the thing, and people lay about in tents or out as they\r\npleased, the boys looking like warriors slumbering where they fell.\r\n\r\nThe elders had just settled to a comfortable nap when the youngsters\r\nrose, refreshed and ready for further exploits. A hint sent them all off\r\nto the cave, and there were discovered bows and arrows, battle clubs,\r\nold swords, and various relics of an interesting nature. Perched upon\r\na commanding rock, with Jamie to \xe2\x80\x9csplain\xe2\x80\x9d things to her, Rose beheld\r\na series of stirring scenes enacted with great vigour and historical\r\naccuracy by her gifted relatives.\r\n\r\nCaptain Cook was murdered by the natives of Owhyhee in the most\r\nthrilling manner. Captain Kidd buried untold wealth in the chowder\r\nkettle at the dead of night, and shot both the trusting villains who\r\nshared the secret of the hiding place. Sinbad came ashore there and had\r\nmanifold adventures, and numberless wrecks bestrewed the sands.\r\n\r\nRose considered them by far the most exciting dramas she had ever\r\nwitnessed; and when the performance closed with a grand ballet of Feejee\r\nIslanders, whose barbaric yells alarmed the gulls, she had no words in\r\nwhich to express her gratification.\r\n\r\nAnother swim at sunset, another merry evening on the rocks watching the\r\nlighted steamers pass seaward and the pleasure-boats come into port,\r\nended the second day of the camping out, and sent everyone to bed early\r\nthat they might be ready for the festivities of the morrow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArchie, didn't I hear uncle ask you to row home in the morning for\r\nfresh milk and things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, may I go too? I have something of great importance to arrange;\r\nyou know I was carried off in a hurry,\xe2\x80\x9d Rose said in a confidential\r\nwhisper as she was bidding her cousins good night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm willing, and I guess Charlie won't mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you; be sure you stand by me when I ask leave in the morning, and\r\ndon't say anything till then, except to Charlie. Promise,\xe2\x80\x9d urged Rose,\r\nso eagerly, that Archie struck an attitude and cried dramatically,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy yonder moon I swear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! it's all right, go along\xe2\x80\x9d; and Rose departed as if satisfied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe's a queer little thing, isn't she, Prince?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather a nice little thing, I think. I'm quite fond of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose's quick ears caught both remarks, and she retired to her tent,\r\nsaying to herself with sleepy dignity,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle thing, indeed! Those boys talk as if I was a baby. They will\r\ntreat me with more respect after to-morrow, I guess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArchie did stand by her in the morning, and her request was readily\r\ngranted, as the lads were coming directly back. Off they went, and Rose\r\nwaved her hand to the islanders with a somewhat pensive air, for an\r\nheroic purpose glowed within her, and the spirit of self-sacrifice was\r\nabout to be illustrated in a new and touching manner.\r\n\r\nWhile the boys got the milk Rose ran to Phebe, ordered her to leave her\r\ndishes, to put on her hat, and take a note back to Uncle Alec, which\r\nwould explain this somewhat mysterious performance. Phebe obeyed, and\r\nwhen she went to the boat Rose accompanied her, telling the boys she was\r\nnot ready to go yet, but they could, some of them, come for her when she\r\nhung a white signal on her balcony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why not come now? What are you about, miss? Uncle won't like it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n protested Charlie, in great amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust do as I tell you, little boy; uncle will understand and explain.\r\nObey, as Phebe does, and ask no questions. I can have secrets as well\r\nas other people\xe2\x80\x9d; and Rose walked off with an air of lofty independence\r\nthat impressed her friends immensely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's some plot between uncle and herself, so we won't meddle. All\r\nright, Phebe? Pull away, Prince\xe2\x80\x9d; and off they went to be received with\r\nmuch surprise by the islanders.\r\n\r\nThis was the note Phebe bore:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Uncle, I am going to take Phebe's place to-day, and let her have\r\nall the fun she can. Please don't mind what she says, but keep her, and\r\ntell the boys to be very good to her for my sake. Don't think it is easy\r\nto do this; it is very hard to give up the best day of all, but I feel\r\nso selfish to have all the pleasure and Phebe none, that I wish to make\r\nthis sacrifice. Do let me, and don't laugh at it; I truly do not wish to\r\nbe praised, and I truly want to do it. Love to all from,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless the little dear, what a generous heart she has! Shall we go after\r\nher, Jessie, or let her have her way?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, after the first\r\nmingled amusement and astonishment had subsided.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet her alone, and don't spoil her little sacrifice. She means it, I\r\nknow, and the best way in which we can show our respect for her effort\r\nis to give Phebe a pleasant day. I'm sure she has earned it\xe2\x80\x9d; and Mrs.\r\nJessie made a sign to the boys to suppress their disappointment and\r\nexert themselves to please Rose's guest.\r\n\r\nPhebe was with difficulty kept from going straight home, and declared\r\nthat she should not enjoy herself one bit without Miss Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe won't hold out all day, and we shall see her paddling back before\r\nnoon, I'll wager anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlie; and the rest so strongly\r\ninclined to his opinion that they resigned themselves to the loss of the\r\nlittle queen of the revels, sure that it would be only a temporary one.\r\n\r\nBut hour after hour passed, and no signal appeared on the balcony,\r\nthough Phebe watched it hopefully. No passing boat brought the truant\r\nback, though more than one pair of eyes looked out for the bright hair\r\nunder the round hat; and sunset came, bringing no Rose but the lovely\r\ncolour in the western sky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really did not think the child had it in her. I fancied it was a bit\r\nof sentiment, but I see she was in earnest, and means that her sacrifice\r\nshall be a true one. Dear little soul! I'll make it up to her a thousand\r\ntimes over, and beg her pardon for thinking it might be done for\r\neffect,\xe2\x80\x9d Dr. Alec said remorsefully, as he strained his eyes through the\r\ndusk, fancying he saw a small figure sitting in the garden as it had sat\r\non the keg the night before, laying the generous little plot that had\r\ncost more than he could guess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, she can't help seeing the fireworks, any way, unless she is\r\ngoose enough to think she must hide in a dark closet and not look,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nArchie, who was rather disgusted at Rose's seeming ingratitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will see ours capitally, but miss the big ones on the hill, unless\r\npapa has forgotten all about them,\xe2\x80\x9d added Steve, cutting short the\r\nharangue Mac had begun upon the festivals of the ancients.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm sure the sight of her will be better than the finest fireworks\r\nthat ever went off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Phebe, meditating an elopement with one of the\r\nboats if she could get a chance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet things work; if she resists a brilliant invitation we give her she\r\nwill be a heroine,\xe2\x80\x9d added Uncle Alec, secretly hoping that she would\r\nnot.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Rose had spent a quiet, busy day helping Dolly, waiting on\r\nAunt Peace, and steadily resisting Aunt Plenty's attempts to send her\r\nback to the happy island. It had been hard in the morning to come\r\nin from the bright world outside, with flags flying, cannon booming,\r\ncrackers popping, and everyone making ready for a holiday, and go to\r\nwashing cups, while Dolly grumbled and the aunts lamented. It was very\r\nhard to see the day go by, knowing how gay each hour must have been\r\nacross the water, and how a word from her would take her where she\r\nlonged to be with all her heart. But it was hardest of all when evening\r\ncame and Aunt Peace was asleep, Aunt Plenty seeing a gossip in the\r\nparlor, Dolly established in the porch to enjoy the show, and nothing\r\nleft for the little maid to do but sit alone in her balcony and watch\r\nthe gay rockets whizz up from island, hill, and city, while bands played\r\nand boats laden with happy people went to and fro in the fitful light.\r\n\r\nThen it must be confessed that a tear or two dimmed the blue eyes, and\r\nonce, when a very brilliant display illuminated the island for a moment,\r\nand she fancied she saw the tents, the curly head went down on the\r\nrailing, and a wide-awake nasturtium heard a little whisper,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope someone wishes I was there!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe tears were all gone, however, and she was watching the hill and\r\nisland answer each other with what Jamie called \xe2\x80\x9cwhizzers, whirligigs\r\nand busters,\xe2\x80\x9d and smiling as she thought how hard the boys must be\r\nworking to keep up such a steady fire, when Uncle Mac came walking in\r\nupon her, saying hurriedly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, child, put on your tippet, pelisse, or whatever you call it, and\r\nrun off with me. I came to get Phebe, but aunt says she is gone, so I\r\nwant you. I've got Fun down in the boat, and I want you to go with us\r\nand see my fireworks. Got them up for you, and you mustn't miss them, or\r\nI shall be disappointed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, uncle,\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose, feeling as if she ought to refuse even a\r\nglimpse of bliss, \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know, my dear, I know; aunt told me; but no one needs you now so much\r\nas I do, and I insist on your coming,\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Mac, who seemed in a\r\ngreat hurry to be off, yet was unusually kind.\r\n\r\nSo Rose went and found the little Chinaman with a funny lantern waiting\r\nto help her in and convulse her with laughter trying to express his\r\nemotions in pigeon English. The city clocks were striking nine as they\r\ngot out into the bay, and the island fireworks seemed to be over, for no\r\nrocket answered the last Roman candle that shone on the Aunt-hill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOurs are done, I see, but they are going up all round the city, and how\r\npretty they are,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, folding her mantle about her, and surveying\r\nthe scene with pensive interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHope my fellows have not got into trouble up there,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Uncle\r\nMac, adding with a satisfied chuckle, as a spark shone out, \xe2\x80\x9cNo; there\r\nit goes! Look, Rosy, and see how you like this one; it was ordered\r\nespecially in honour of your coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose looked with all her eyes, and saw the spark grow into the likeness\r\nof a golden vase, then green leaves came out, and then a crimson flower\r\nglowing on the darkness with a splendid lustre.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it a rose, uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, clasping her hands with delight as she\r\nrecognised the handsome flower.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it is! Look again, and guess what those are,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Uncle\r\nMac, chuckling and enjoying it all like a boy.\r\n\r\nA wreath of what looked at first like purple brooms appeared below the\r\nvase, but Rose guessed what they were meant for, and stood straight up,\r\nholding by his shoulder, and crying excitedly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThistles, uncle, Scotch thistles! There are seven of them one for each\r\nboy! Oh, what a joke!\xe2\x80\x9d and she laughed so that she plumped into the\r\nbottom of the boat and stayed there till the brilliant spectacle was\r\nquite gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was rather a neat thing, I flatter myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Mac, in\r\nhigh glee at the success of his illumination. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, shall I leave you\r\non the Island or take you home again, my good little girl?\xe2\x80\x9d he added,\r\nlifting her up with such a tone of approbation in his voice that Rose\r\nkissed him on the spot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHome, please uncle; and I thank you very very much for the beautiful\r\nfirework you got up for me. I'm so glad I saw it; and I know I shall\r\ndream about it,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose steadily, though a wistful glance went\r\ntoward the Island, now so near that she could smell powder and see\r\nshadowy figures flitting about.\r\n\r\nHome they went; and Rose fell asleep saying to herself, \xe2\x80\x9cIt was harder\r\nthan I thought, but I'm glad I did it, and I truly don't want any reward\r\nbut Phebe's pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 11--Poor Mac\r\n\r\nRose's sacrifice was a failure in one respect, for, though the elders\r\nloved her the better for it, and showed that they did, the boys were not\r\ninspired with the sudden respect which she had hoped for. In fact, her\r\nfeelings were much hurt by overhearing Archie say that he couldn't see\r\nany sense in it; and the Prince added another blow by pronouncing her\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe queerest chicken ever seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is apt to be so, and it is hard to bear; for, though we do not want\r\ntrumpets blown, we do like to have our little virtues appreciated, and\r\ncannot help feeling disappointed if they are not.\r\n\r\nA time soon came, however, when Rose, quite unconsciously, won not only\r\nthe respect of her cousins, but their gratitude and affection likewise.\r\n\r\nSoon after the Island episode, Mac had a sunstroke, and was very ill\r\nfor some time. It was so sudden that everyone was startled, and for some\r\ndays the boy's life was in danger. He pulled through, however; and then,\r\njust as the family were rejoicing, a new trouble appeared which cast a\r\ngloom over them all.\r\n\r\nPoor Mac's eyes gave out; and well they might, for he had abused them,\r\nand never being very strong, they suffered doubly now.\r\n\r\nNo one dared to tell him the dark predictions of the great oculist who\r\ncame to look at them, and the boy tried to be patient, thinking that a\r\nfew weeks of rest would repair the overwork of several years.\r\n\r\nHe was forbidden to look at a book, and as that was the one thing he\r\nmost delighted in, it was a terrible affliction to the Worm. Everyone\r\nwas very ready to read to him, and at first the lads contended for this\r\nhonour. But as week after week went by, and Mac was still condemned to\r\nidleness and a darkened room, their zeal abated, and one after the other\r\nfell off. It was hard for the active fellows, right in the midst of\r\ntheir vacation; and nobody blamed them when they contented themselves\r\nwith brief calls, running of errands, and warm expressions of sympathy.\r\n\r\nThe elders did their best, but Uncle Mac was a busy man, Aunt Jane's\r\nreading was of a funereal sort, impossible to listen to long, and the\r\nother aunties were all absorbed in their own cares, though they supplied\r\nthe boy with every delicacy they could invent.\r\n\r\nUncle Alec was a host in himself, but he could not give all his time to\r\nthe invalid; and if it had not been for Rose, the afflicted Worm\r\nwould have fared ill. Her pleasant voice suited him, her patience was\r\nunfailing, her time of no apparent value, and her eager good-will was\r\nvery comforting.\r\n\r\nThe womanly power of self-devotion was strong in the child, and she\r\nremained faithfully at her post when all the rest dropped away. Hour\r\nafter hour she sat in the dusky room, with one ray of light on her book,\r\nreading to the boy, who lay with shaded eyes silently enjoying the only\r\npleasure that lightened the weary days. Sometimes he was peevish and\r\nhard to please, sometimes he growled because his reader could not manage\r\nthe dry books he wished to hear, and sometimes he was so despondent that\r\nher heart ached to see him. Through all these trials Rose persevered,\r\nusing all her little arts to please him. When he fretted, she was\r\npatient; when he growled, she ploughed bravely through the hard pages\r\nnot dry to her in one sense, for quiet tears dropped on them now and\r\nthen; and when Mac fell into a despairing mood, she comforted him with\r\nevery hopeful word she dared to offer.\r\n\r\nHe said little, but she knew he was grateful, for she suited him better\r\nthan anyone else. If she was late, he was impatient; when she had to go,\r\nhe seemed forlorn; and when the tired head ached worst, she could always\r\nsoothe him to sleep, crooning the old songs her father used to love.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't know what I should do without that child,\xe2\x80\x9d Aunt Jane often\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe's worth all those racketing fellows put together,\xe2\x80\x9d Mac would add,\r\nfumbling about to discover if the little chair was ready for her coming.\r\n\r\nThat was the sort of reward Rose liked, the thanks that cheered her;\r\nand whenever she grew very tired, one look at the green shade, the curly\r\nhead so restless on the pillow, and the poor groping hands, touched her\r\ntender heart and put new spirit into the weary voice.\r\n\r\nShe did not know how much she was learning, both from the books she read\r\nand the daily sacrifices she made. Stories and poetry were her delight,\r\nbut Mac did not care for them; and since his favourite Greeks and Romans\r\nwere forbidden, he satisfied himself with travels, biographies, and the\r\nhistory of great inventions or discoveries. Rose despised this taste\r\nat first, but soon got interested in Livingstone's adventures, Hobson's\r\nstirring life in India, and the brave trials and triumphs of Watt and\r\nArkwright, Fulton, and \xe2\x80\x9cPalissy, the Potter.\xe2\x80\x9d The true, strong books\r\nhelped the dreamy girl; her faithful service and sweet patience touched\r\nand won the boy; and long afterward both learned to see how useful those\r\nseemingly hard and weary hours had been to them.\r\n\r\nOne bright morning, as Rose sat down to begin a fat volume entitled\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHistory of the French Revolution,\xe2\x80\x9d expecting to come to great grief\r\nover the long names, Mac, who was lumbering about the room like a blind\r\nbear, stopped her by asking abruptly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat day of the month is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe seventh of August, I believe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than half my vacation gone, and I've only had a week of it! I call\r\nthat hard,\xe2\x80\x9d and he groaned dismally.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it is; but there is more to come, and you may be able to enjoy\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay be able! I will be able! Does that old noodle think I'm going to\r\nstay stived up here much longer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess he does, unless your eyes get on faster than they have yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas he said anything more lately?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven't seen him, you know. Shall I begin? this looks rather nice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRead away; it's all one to me.\xe2\x80\x9d And Mac cast himself down upon the old\r\nlounge, where his heavy head felt easiest.\r\n\r\nRose began with great spirit, and kept on gallantly for a couple\r\nof chapters, getting over the unpronounceable names with unexpected\r\nsuccess, she thought, for her listener did not correct her once, and lay\r\nso still she fancied he was deeply interested. All of a sudden she was\r\narrested in the middle of a fine paragraph by Mac, who sat bolt upright,\r\nbrought both feet down with a thump, and said, in a rough, excited tone,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop! I don't hear a word, and you may as well save your breath to\r\nanswer my question.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, looking uneasy, for she had something on her\r\nmind, and feared that he suspected what it was. His next words proved\r\nthat she was right.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, look here, I want to know something, and you've got to tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, don't--\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose, beseechingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must, or I'll pull off this shade and stare at the sun as hard as\r\never I can stare. Come now!\xe2\x80\x9d and he half rose, as if ready to execute\r\nthe threat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will! oh, I will tell, if I know! But don't be reckless and do\r\nanything so crazy as that,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, in great distress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; then listen, and don't dodge, as everyone else does. Didn't\r\nthe doctor think my eyes worse the last time he came? Mother won't say,\r\nbut you shall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe he did,\xe2\x80\x9d faltered Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought so! Did he say I should be able to go to school when it\r\nbegins?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Mac,\xe2\x80\x9d very low.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was all, but Rose saw her cousin set his lips together and take\r\na long breath, as if she had hit him hard. He bore the disappointment\r\nbravely, however, and asked quite steadily in a minute,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow soon does he think I can study again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was so hard to answer that! Yet Rose knew she must, for Aunt Jane had\r\ndeclared she could not do it, and Uncle Mac had begged her to break the\r\ntruth to the poor lad.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for a good many months.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked with a pathetic sort of gruffness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA year, perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA whole year! Why, I expected to be ready for college by that time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n And, pushing up the shade, Mac stared at her with startled eyes, that\r\nsoon blinked and fell before the one ray of light.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlenty of time for that; you must be patient now, and get them\r\nthoroughly well, or they will trouble you again when it will be harder\r\nto spare them,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with tears in her own eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won't do it! I will study and get through somehow. It's all humbug\r\nabout taking care so long. These doctors like to keep hold of a fellow\r\nif they can. But I won't stand it I vow I won't!\xe2\x80\x9d and he banged his fist\r\ndown on the unoffending pillow as if he were pommelling the hard-hearted\r\ndoctor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mac, listen to me,\xe2\x80\x9d Rose said very earnestly, though her voice\r\nshook a little and her heart ached. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know you have hurt your eyes\r\nreading by fire-light and in the dusk, and sitting up late, and now\r\nyou'll have to pay for it; the doctor said so. You must be careful, and\r\ndo as he tells you, or you will be blind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is true, and he wanted us to tell you that nothing but entire\r\nrest would cure you. I know it's dreadfully hard, but we'll all help\r\nyou; I'll read all day long, and lead you, and wait upon you, and try to\r\nmake it easier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe stopped there, for it was evident that he did not hear a sound; the\r\nword \xe2\x80\x9cblind\xe2\x80\x9d seemed to have knocked him down, for he had buried his\r\nface in the pillow, and lay so still that Rose was frightened. She sat\r\nmotionless for many minutes, longing to comfort him, but not knowing\r\nhow, and wishing Uncle Alec would come, for he had promised to tell Mac.\r\n\r\nPresently, a sort of choking sound came out of the pillow, and went\r\nstraight to her heart the most pathetic sob she ever heard, for, though\r\nit was the most natural means of relief, the poor fellow must not\r\nindulge in it because of the afflicted eyes. The \xe2\x80\x9cFrench Revolution\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n tumbled out of her lap, and, running to the sofa, she knelt down by\r\nit, saying, with the motherly sort of tenderness girls feel for any\r\nsorrowing creature,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my dear, you mustn't cry! It is so bad for your poor eyes. Take\r\nyour head out of that hot pillow, and let me cool it. I don't wonder you\r\nfeel so, but please don't cry. I'll cry for you; it won't hurt me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs she spoke she pulled away the cushion with gentle force, and saw the\r\ngreen shade all crushed and stained with the few hot tears that told how\r\nbitter the disappointment had been. Mac felt her sympathy, but, being\r\na boy, did not thank her for it; only sat up with a jerk, saying, as\r\nhe tried to rub away the tell-tale drops with the sleeve of his jacket,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't bother; weak eyes always water. I'm all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Rose cried out, and caught his arm, \xe2\x80\x9cDon't touch them with that\r\nrough woollen stuff! Lie down and let me bathe them, there's a dear boy;\r\nthen there will be no harm done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey do smart confoundedly. I say, don't you tell the other fellows\r\nthat I made a baby of myself, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d he added, yielding with a sigh\r\nto the orders of his nurse, who had flown for the eye-wash and linen\r\ncambric handkerchief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I won't; but anyone would be upset at the idea of being well\r\ntroubled in this way. I'm sure you bear it splendidly, and you know it\r\nisn't half so bad when you get used to it. Besides, it is only for a\r\ntime, and you can do lots of pleasant things if you can't study. You'll\r\nhave to wear blue goggles, perhaps; won't that be funny?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd while she was pouring out all the comfortable words she could think\r\nof, Rose was softly bathing the eyes and dabbing the hot forehead with\r\nlavender-water, as her patient lay quiet with a look on his face that\r\ngrieved her sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHomer was blind, and so was Milton, and they did something to be\r\nremembered by, in spite of it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as if to himself, in a solemn\r\ntone, for even the blue goggles did not bring a smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa had a picture of Milton and his daughters writing for him. It\r\nwas a very sweet picture, I thought,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Rose in a serious voice,\r\ntrying to meet the sufferer on his own ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I could study if someone read and did the eye part. Do you\r\nsuppose I could, by and by?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, with a sudden ray of hope.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare say, if your head is strong enough. This sunstroke, you know, is\r\nwhat upset you, and your brain needs rest, the doctor says.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll have a talk with the old fellow next time he comes, and find out\r\njust what I may do; then I shall know where I am. What a fool I was that\r\nday to be stewing my brains and letting the sun glare on my book till\r\nthe letters danced before me! I see 'em now when I shut my eyes; black\r\nballs bobbing round, and stars and all sorts of queer things. Wonder if\r\nall blind people do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't think about them; I'll go on reading, shall I? We shall come\r\nto the exciting part soon, and then you'll forget all this,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested\r\nRose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I never shall forget. Hang the old 'Revolution'! I don't want to\r\nhear another word of it. My head aches, and I'm hot. Oh, wouldn't I like\r\nto go for a pull in the 'Stormy Petrel!\xe2\x80\x9d' and poor Mac tossed about as\r\nif he did not know what to do with himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me sing, and perhaps you'll drop off; then the day will seem\r\nshorter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, taking up a fan and sitting down beside him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I shall; I didn't sleep much last night, and when I did I\r\ndreamed like fun. See here, you tell the people that I know, and it's\r\nall right, and I don't want them to talk about it or howl over me.\r\nThat's all; now drone away, and I'll try to sleep. Wish I could for a\r\nyear, and wake up cured.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I wish, I wish you could!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose said it so fervently that Mac was moved to grope for her apron and\r\nhold on to a corner of it, as if it was comfortable to feel her near\r\nhim. But all he said was,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a good little soul, Rosy. Give us 'The Birks'; that is a drowsy\r\none that always sends me off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nQuite contented with this small return for all her sympathy, Rose waved\r\nher fan and sang, in a dreamy tone, the pretty Scotch air, the burden of\r\nwhich is,\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cBonny lassie, will ye gang, will ye gang\r\n     To the Birks of Aberfeldie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhether the lassie went or not I cannot say, but the laddie was off to\r\nthe land of Nod, in about ten minutes, quite worn out with hearing the\r\nbad tidings and the effort to bear them manfully.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 12--\xe2\x80\x9cThe Other Fellows\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose did tell \xe2\x80\x9cthe people\xe2\x80\x9d what had passed, and no one \xe2\x80\x9chowled\xe2\x80\x9d over\r\nMac, or said a word to trouble him. He had his talk with the doctor, and\r\ngot very little comfort out of it, for he found that \xe2\x80\x9cjust what he might\r\ndo\xe2\x80\x9d was nothing at all; though the prospect of some study by and by, if\r\nall went well, gave him courage to bear the woes of the present.\r\nHaving made up his mind to this, he behaved so well that everyone was\r\nastonished, never having suspected so much manliness in the quiet Worm.\r\n\r\nThe boys were much impressed, both by the greatness of the affliction\r\nwhich hung over him and by his way of bearing it. They were very good\r\nto him, but not always particularly wise in their attempts to cheer\r\nand amuse; and Rose often found him much downcast after a visit of\r\ncondolence from the Clan. She still kept her place as head-nurse and\r\nchief-reader, though the boys did their best in an irregular sort of\r\nway. They were rather taken aback sometimes at finding Rose's services\r\npreferred to their's, and privately confided to one another that \xe2\x80\x9cOld\r\nMac was getting fond of being molly-coddled.\xe2\x80\x9d But they could not help\r\nseeing how useful she was, and owning that she alone had remained\r\nfaithful a fact which caused some of them much secret compunction now\r\nand then.\r\n\r\nRose felt that she ruled in that room, if nowhere else, for Aunt Jane\r\nleft a great deal to her, finding that her experience with her invalid\r\nfather fitted her for a nurse, and in a case like this, her youth was\r\nan advantage rather than a drawback. Mac soon came to think that no one\r\ncould take care of him so well as Rose, and Rose soon grew fond of\r\nher patient, though at first she had considered this cousin the least\r\nattractive of the seven. He was not polite and sensible like Archie, nor\r\ngay and handsome like Prince Charlie, nor neat and obliging like Steve,\r\nnor amusing like the \xe2\x80\x9cBrats,\xe2\x80\x9d nor confiding and affectionate like\r\nlittle Jamie. He was rough, absent-minded, careless, and awkward, rather\r\npriggish, and not at all agreeable to a dainty, beauty-loving girl like\r\nRose.\r\n\r\nBut when his trouble came upon him, she discovered many good things in\r\nthis cousin of hers, and learned not only to pity but to respect and\r\nlove the poor Worm, who tried to be patient, brave, and cheerful, and\r\nfound it a harder task than anyone guessed, except the little nurse, who\r\nsaw him in his gloomiest moods. She soon came to think that his friends\r\ndid not appreciate him, and upon one occasion was moved to free her mind\r\nin a way that made a deep impression on the boys.\r\n\r\nVacation was almost over, and the time drawing near when Mac would be\r\nleft outside the happy school-world which he so much enjoyed. This made\r\nhim rather low in his mind, and his cousins exerted themselves to cheer\r\nhim up, especially one afternoon when a spasm of devotion seemed\r\nto seize them all. Jamie trudged down the hill with a basket of\r\nblackberries which he had \xe2\x80\x9cpicked all his ownself,\xe2\x80\x9d as his scratched\r\nfingers and stained lips plainly testified. Will and Geordie brought\r\ntheir puppies to beguile the weary hours, and the three elder lads\r\ncalled to discuss baseball, cricket, and kindred subjects, eminently\r\nfitted to remind the invalid of his privations.\r\n\r\nRose had gone to drive with Uncle Alec, who declared she was getting as\r\npale as a potato sprout, living so much in a dark room. But her thoughts\r\nwere with her boy all the while, and she ran up to him the moment she\r\nreturned, to find things in a fine state of confusion.\r\n\r\nWith the best intentions in life, the lads had done more harm than\r\ngood, and the spectacle that met Nurse Rose's eye was a trying one.\r\nThe puppies were yelping, the small boys romping, and the big boys all\r\ntalking at once; the curtains were up, the room close, berries scattered\r\nfreely about, Mac's shade half off, his cheeks flushed, his temper\r\nruffled, and his voice loudest of all as he disputed hotly with Steve\r\nabout lending certain treasured books which he could no longer use.\r\n\r\nNow Rose considered this her special kingdom, and came down upon the\r\ninvaders with an energy which amazed them and quelled the riot at once.\r\nThey had never seen her roused before, and the effect was tremendous;\r\nalso comical, for she drove the whole flock of boys out of the room like\r\nan indignant little hen defending her brood. They all went as meekly as\r\nsheep; the small lads fled from the house precipitately, but the three\r\nelder ones only retired to the next room, and remained there hoping for\r\na chance to explain and apologise, and so appease the irate young lady,\r\nwho had suddenly turned the tables and clattered them about their ears.\r\n\r\nAs they waited, they observed her proceedings through the half-open\r\ndoor, and commented upon them briefly but expressively, feeling quite\r\nbowed down with remorse at the harm they had innocently done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe's put the room to rights in a jiffey. What jacks we were to let\r\nthose dogs in and kick up such a row,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Steve, after a prolonged\r\npeep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe poor old Worm turns as if she was treading on him instead of\r\ncuddling him like a pussy cat. Isn't he cross, though?\xe2\x80\x9d added Charlie,\r\nas Mac was heard growling about his \xe2\x80\x9cconfounded head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will manage him; but it's mean in us to rumple him up and then\r\nleave her to smooth him down. I'd go and help, but I don't know how,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Archie, looking much depressed, for he was a conscientious fellow,\r\nand blamed himself for his want of thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, more do I. Odd, isn't it, what a knack women have for taking care\r\nof sick folks?\xe2\x80\x9d and Charlie fell a-musing over this undeniable fact.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has been ever so good to Mac,\xe2\x80\x9d began Steve, in a self-reproachful\r\ntone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter than his own brother, hey?\xe2\x80\x9d cut in Archie, finding relief for\r\nhis own regret in the delinquencies of another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you needn't preach; you didn't any of you do any more, and you\r\nmight have, for Mac likes you better than he does me. I always fret him,\r\nhe says, and it isn't my fault if I am a quiddle,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Steve, in\r\nself-defence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have all been selfish and neglected him, so we won't fight about\r\nit, but try and do better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Archie, generously taking more than\r\nhis share of blame, for he had been less inattentive than either of the\r\nothers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRose has stood by him like a good one, and it's no wonder he likes to\r\nhave her round best. I should myself if I was down on my luck as he is,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n put in Charlie, feeling that he really had not done \xe2\x80\x9cthe little thing\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n justice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll tell you what it is, boys we haven't been half good enough to\r\nRose, and we've got to make it up to her somehow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Archie, who had\r\na very manly sense of honour about paying his debts, even to a girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm awfully sorry I made fun of her doll when Jamie lugged it out; and\r\nI called her 'baby bunting' when she cried over the dead kitten. Girls\r\nare such geese sometimes, I can't help it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Steve, confessing his\r\ntransgressions handsomely, and feeling quite ready to atone for them if\r\nhe only knew how.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll go down on my knees and beg her pardon for treating her as if she\r\nwas a child. Don't it make her mad, though? Come to think of it, she's\r\nonly two years or so younger than I am. But she is so small and pretty,\r\nshe always seems like a dolly to me,\xe2\x80\x9d and the Prince looked down from\r\nhis lofty height of five feet five as if Rose was indeed a pygmy beside\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat dolly has got a real good little heart, and a bright mind of her\r\nown, you'd better believe. Mac says she understands some things quicker\r\nthan he can, and mother thinks she is an uncommonly nice girl, though\r\nshe don't know all creation. You needn't put on airs, Charlie, though\r\nyou are a tall one, for Rose likes Archie better than you; she said she\r\ndid because he treated her respectfully.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteve looks as fierce as a game-cock; but don't you get excited, my\r\nson, for it won't do a bit of good. Of course, everybody likes the Chief\r\nbest; they ought to, and I'll punch their heads if they don't. So calm\r\nyourself, Dandy, and mend your own manners before you come down on other\r\npeople's.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus the Prince with great dignity and perfect good nature, while Archie\r\nlooked modestly gratified with the flattering opinions of his kinsfolk,\r\nand Steve subsided, feeling he had done his duty as a cousin and a\r\nbrother. A pause ensued, during which Aunt Jane appeared in the other\r\nroom, accompanied by a tea-tray sumptuously spread, and prepared to feed\r\nher big nestling, as that was a task she allowed no one to share with\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you have a minute to spare before you go, child, I wish you'd just\r\nmake Mac a fresh shade; this has got a berry stain on it, and he must\r\nbe tidy, for he is to go out to-morrow if it is a cloudy day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nJane, spreading toast in a stately manner, while Mac slopped his tea\r\nabout without receiving a word of reproof.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, aunt,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, so meekly that the boys could hardly believe\r\nit could be the same voice which had issued the stern command, \xe2\x80\x9cOut of\r\nthis room, every one of you!\xe2\x80\x9d not very long ago.\r\n\r\nThey had not time to retire, without unseemly haste, before she walked\r\ninto the parlour and sat down at the work-table without a word. It was\r\nfunny to see the look the three tall lads cast at the little person\r\nsedately threading a needle with green silk. They all wanted to say\r\nsomething expressive of repentance, but no one knew how to begin, and it\r\nwas evident, from the prim expression of Rose's face, that she intended\r\nto stand upon her dignity till they had properly abased themselves. The\r\npause was becoming very awkward, when Charlie, who possessed all the\r\npersuasive arts of a born scapegrace, went slowly down upon his knees\r\nbefore her, beat his breast, and said, in a heart-broken tone,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease forgive me this time, and I'll never do so any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was very hard to keep sober, but Rose managed it and answered gravely,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is Mac's pardon you should ask, not mine, for you haven't hurt me,\r\nand I shouldn't wonder if you had him a great deal, with all that light\r\nand racket, and talk about things that only worry him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really think we've hurt him, cousin?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Archie, with a\r\ntroubled look, while Charlie settled down in a remorseful heap among the\r\ntable legs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do, for he has got a raging headache, and his eyes are as red as\r\nas this emery bag,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, solemnly plunging her needle into a\r\nfat flannel strawberry.\r\n\r\nSteve tore his hair, metaphorically speaking, for he clutched his\r\ncherished top-knot, and wildly dishevelled it, as if that was the\r\nheaviest penance he could inflict upon himself at such short notice.\r\nCharlie laid himself out flat, melodramatically begging someone to take\r\nhim away and hang him; but Archie, who felt worst of all, said nothing\r\nexcept to vow within himself that he would read to Mac till his own eyes\r\nwere as red as a dozen emery bags combined.\r\n\r\nSeeing the wholesome effects of her treatment upon these culprits, Rose\r\nfelt that she might relent and allow them a gleam of hope. She found\r\nit impossible to help trampling upon the prostrate Prince a little, in\r\nwords at least, for he had hurt her feelings oftener than he knew; so\r\nshe gave him a thimble-pie on the top of his head, and said, with an air\r\nof an infinitely superior being,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't be silly, but get up, and I'll tell you something much better to\r\ndo than sprawling on the floor and getting all over lint.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlie obediently sat himself upon a hassock at her feet; the other\r\nsinners drew near to catch the words of wisdom about to fall from her\r\nlips, and Rose, softened by this gratifying humility, addressed them in\r\nher most maternal tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, boys, if you really want to be good to Mac, you can do it in this\r\nway. Don't keep talking about things he can't do, or go and tell what\r\nfun you have had batting your ridiculous balls about. Get some nice book\r\nand read quietly; cheer him up about school, and offer to help him study\r\nby and by; you can do that better than I, because I'm only a girl, and\r\ndon't learn Greek and Latin and all sorts of headachy stuff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but you can do heaps of things better than we can; you've proved\r\nthat,\xe2\x80\x9d said Archie, with an approving look that delighted Rose, though\r\nshe could not resist giving Charlie one more rebuke, by saying, with a\r\nlittle bridling of the head, and a curl of the lip that wanted to smile\r\ninstead,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm glad you think so, though I am a 'queer chicken.\xe2\x80\x9d'\r\n\r\nThis scathing remark caused the Prince to hide his face for shame, and\r\nSteve to erect his head in the proud consciousness that this shot was\r\nnot meant for him. Archie laughed, and Rose, seeing a merry blue\r\neye winking at her from behind two brown hands, gave Charlie's ear a\r\nfriendly tweak, and extended the olive-branch of peace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we'll all be good, and plan nice things for poor Mac,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nsmiling so graciously that the boys felt as if the sun had suddenly\r\nburst out from behind a heavy cloud and was shining with great\r\nbrilliancy.\r\n\r\nThe storm had cleared the air, and quite a heavenly calm succeeded,\r\nduring which plans of a most varied and surprising sort were laid, for\r\neveryone burned to make noble sacrifices upon the shrine of \xe2\x80\x9cpoor\r\nMac,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose was the guiding star to whom the others looked with most\r\ngratifying submission. Of course, this elevated state of things could\r\nnot endure long, but it was very nice while it lasted, and left an\r\nexcellent effect upon the minds of all when the first ardour had\r\nsubsided.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, that's ready for to-morrow, and I do hope it will be cloudy,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Rose, as she finished off the new shade, the progress of which the\r\nboys had watched with interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'd bespoken an extra sunny day, but I'll tell the clerk of the weather\r\nto change it. He's an obliging fellow, and he'll attend to it, so make\r\nyourself easy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlie, who had become quite perky again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very easy for you to joke, but how would you like to wear a\r\nblinder like that for weeks and weeks, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose quenched his\r\nrising spirits by slipping the shade over his eyes, as he still sat on\r\nthe cushion at her feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's horrid! Take it off, take it off! I don't wonder the poor old boy\r\nhas the blues with a thing like that on\xe2\x80\x9d; and Charlie sat looking at\r\nwhat seemed to him an instrument of torture, with such a sober face that\r\nRose took it gently away, and went in to bid Mac good-night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall go home with her, for it is getting darkish, and she is rather\r\ntimid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Archie, forgetting that he had often laughed at this very\r\ntimidity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I might, for she's taking care of my brother,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Steve,\r\nasserting his rights.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet's all go, that will please her\xe2\x80\x9d; proposed Charlie, with a burst of\r\ngallantry which electrified his mates.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will!\xe2\x80\x9d they said with one voice, and they did, to Rose's great\r\nsurprise and secret contentment; though Archie had all the care of\r\nher, for the other two were leaping fences, running races, and having\r\nwrestling matches all the way down.\r\n\r\nThey composed themselves on reaching the door, however; shook hands\r\ncordially all round, made their best bows, and retired with great\r\nelegance and dignity, leaving Rose to say to herself, with girlish\r\nsatisfaction, as she went in,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, that is the way I like to be treated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 13--Cosey Corner\r\n\r\nVacation was over, the boys went back to school, and poor Mac was left\r\nlamenting. He was out of the darkened room now, and promoted to blue\r\ngoggles, through which he took a gloomy view of life, as might have been\r\nexpected; for there was nothing he could do but wander about, and try to\r\namuse himself without using his eyes. Anyone who has ever been condemned\r\nto that sort of idleness knows how irksome it is, and can understand the\r\nstate of mind which caused Mac to say to Rose in a desperate tone one\r\nday,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, if you don't invent some new employment or amusement for me,\r\nI shall knock myself on the head as sure as you live.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose flew to Uncle Alec for advice, and he ordered both patient and\r\nnurse to the mountains for a month, with Aunt Jessie and Jamie as\r\nescort. Pokey and her mother joined the party, and one bright September\r\nmorning six very happy-looking people were aboard the express train for\r\nPortland two smiling mammas, laden with luncheon baskets and wraps; a\r\npretty young girl with a bag of books on her arm; a tall thin lad with\r\nhis hat over his eyes; and two small children, who sat with their short\r\nlegs straight out before them, and their chubby faces beaming with the\r\nfirst speechless delight of \xe2\x80\x9ctruly travelling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn especially splendid sunset seemed to have been prepared to welcome\r\nthem when, after a long day's journey, they drove into a wide, green\r\ndoor-yard, where a white colt, a red cow, two cats, four kittens,\r\nmany hens, and a dozen people, old and young, were gaily disporting\r\nthemselves. Everyone nodded and smiled in the friendliest manner, and a\r\nlively old lady kissed the new-comers all round, as she said heartily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, I'm proper glad to see you! Come right in and rest, and\r\nwe'll have tea in less than no time, for you must be tired. Lizzie, you\r\nshow the folks upstairs; Kitty, you fly round and help father in with\r\nthe trunks; and Jenny and I will have the table all ready by the time\r\nyou come down. Bless the dears, they want to go see the pussies, and so\r\nthey shall!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe three pretty daughters did \xe2\x80\x9cfly round,\xe2\x80\x9d and everyone felt at home at\r\nonce, all were so hospitable and kind. Aunt Jessie had raptures over the\r\nhome-made carpets, quilts and quaint furniture; Rose could not keep away\r\nfrom the windows, for each framed a lovely picture; and the little folks\r\nmade friends at once with the other children, who filled their arms with\r\nchickens and kittens, and did the honours handsomely.\r\n\r\nThe toot of a horn called all to supper, and a goodly party, including\r\nsix children besides the Camp-bells, assembled in the long dining-room,\r\narmed with mountain appetites and the gayest spirits. It was impossible\r\nfor anyone to be shy or sober, for such gales of merriment arose they\r\nblew the starch out of the stiffest, and made the saddest jolly. Mother\r\nAtkinson, as all called their hostess, was the merriest there, and the\r\nbusiest; for she kept flying up to wait on the children, to bring out\r\nsome new dish, or to banish the live stock, who were of such a social\r\nturn that the colt came into the entry and demanded sugar; the cats sat\r\nabout in people's laps, winking suggestively at the food; and speckled\r\nhens cleared the kitchen floor of crumbs, as they joined in the chat\r\nwith a cheerful clucking.\r\n\r\nEverybody turned out after tea to watch the sunset till all the lovely\r\nred was gone, and mosquitoes wound their shrill horns to sound the\r\nretreat. The music of an organ surprised the new-comers, and in\r\nthe parlor they found Father Atkinson playing sweetly on the little\r\ninstrument made by himself. All the children gathered about him, and,\r\nled by the tuneful sisters, sang prettily till Pokey fell asleep behind\r\nthe door, and Jamie gaped audibly right in the middle of his favourite,\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cCoo,\xe2\x80\x9d said the little doves: \xe2\x80\x9cCoo,\xe2\x80\x9d said she,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cAll in the top of the old pine-tree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe older travellers, being tired, went to \xe2\x80\x9cbye low\xe2\x80\x9d at the same time,\r\nand slept like tops in home-spun sheets, on husk mattresses made by\r\nMother Atkinson, who seemed to have put some soothing powder among them,\r\nso deep and sweet was the slumber that came.\r\n\r\nNext day began the wholesome out-of-door life, which works such wonders\r\nwith tired minds and feeble bodies. The weather was perfect, and the\r\nmountain air made the children as frisky as young lambs; while the\r\nelders went about smiling at one another, and saying, \xe2\x80\x9cIsn't it\r\nsplendid?\xe2\x80\x9d Even Mac, the \xe2\x80\x9cslow coach,\xe2\x80\x9d was seen to leap over a fence\r\nas if he really could not help it; and when Rose ran after him with his\r\nbroad-brimmed hat, he made the spirited proposal to go into the woods\r\nand hunt for a catamount.\r\n\r\nJamie and Pokey were at once enrolled in the Cosey Corner Light Infantry\r\na truly superb company, composed entirely of officers, all wearing\r\ncocked hats, carrying flags, waving swords, or beating drums. It was a\r\nspectacle to stir the dullest soul when this gallant band marched out of\r\nthe yard in full regimentals, with Captain Dove a solemn, big-headed\r\nboy of eleven issuing his orders with the gravity of a general, and his\r\nFalstaffian regiment obeying them with more docility than skill. The\r\nlittle Snow children did very well, and Lieutenant Jack Dove was fine\r\nto see; so was Drummer Frank, the errand-boy of the house, as he\r\nrub-a-dub-dubbed with all his heart and drumsticks. Jamie had \xe2\x80\x9ctrained\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n before, and was made a colonel at once; but Pokey was the best of all,\r\nand called forth a spontaneous burst of applause from the spectators\r\nas she brought up the rear, her cocked hat all over one eye, her flag\r\ntrailing over her shoulder, and her wooden sword straight up in the air;\r\nher face beaming and every curl bobbing with delight as her fat legs\r\ntottered in the vain attempt to keep step manfully.\r\n\r\nMac and Rose were picking blackberries in the bushes beside the road\r\nwhen the soldiers passed without seeing them, and they witnessed a sight\r\nthat was both pretty and comical. A little farther on was one of the\r\nfamily burial spots so common in those parts, and just this side of it\r\nCaptain Fred Dove ordered his company to halt, explaining his reason for\r\nso doing in the following words,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's a graveyard, and it's proper to muffle the drums and lower the\r\nflags as we go by, and we'd better take off our hats, too; it's more\r\nrespectable, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn't that cunning of the dears?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Rose, as the little troop\r\nmarched slowly by to the muffled roll of the drums, every flag and sword\r\nheld low, all the little heads uncovered, and the childish faces very\r\nsober as the leafy shadows flickered over them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet's follow and see what they are after,\xe2\x80\x9d proposed Mac, who found\r\nsitting on the wall and being fed with blackberries luxurious but\r\ntiresome.\r\n\r\nSo they followed and heard the music grow lively, saw the banners wave\r\nin the breeze again when the graveyard was passed, and watched the\r\ncompany file into the dilapidated old church that stood at the corner of\r\nthree woodland roads. Presently the sound of singing made the outsiders\r\nquicken their steps, and, stealing up, they peeped in at one of the\r\nbroken windows.\r\n\r\nCaptain Dove was up in the old wooden pulpit, gazing solemnly down upon\r\nhis company, who, having stacked their arms in the porch, now sat in the\r\nbare pews singing a Sunday-school hymn with great vigour and relish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us pray,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Dove, with as much reverence as an army\r\nchaplain; and, folding his hands, he repeated a prayer which he thought\r\nall would know an excellent little prayer, but not exactly appropriate\r\nto the morning, for it was,\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cNow I lay me down to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEveryone joined in saying it, and it was a pretty sight to see the\r\nlittle creatures bowing their curly heads and lisping out the words they\r\nknew so well. Tears came into Rose's eyes as she looked; Mac took his\r\nhat off involuntarily, and then clapped it on again as if ashamed of\r\nshowing any feeling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I shall preach you a short sermon, and my text is, 'Little\r\nchildren, love one another.' I asked mamma to give me one, and she\r\nthought that would be good; so you all sit still and I'll preach it. You\r\nmustn't whisper, Marion, but hear me. It means that we should be good to\r\neach other, and play fair, and not quarrel as we did this very day about\r\nthe wagon. Jack can't always drive, and needn't be mad because I like\r\nto go with Frank. Annette ought to be horse sometimes and not always\r\ndriver; and Willie may as well make up his mind to let Marion build her\r\nhouse by his, for she will do it, and he needn't fuss about it. Jamie\r\nseems to be a good boy, but I shall preach to him if he isn't. No,\r\nPokey, people don't kiss in church or put their hats on. Now you must\r\nall remember what I tell you, because I am the captain, and you should\r\nmind me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere Lieutenant Jack spoke right out in meeting with the rebellious\r\nremark,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't care if you are; you'd better mind yourself, and tell how you\r\ntook away my strap, and kept the biggest doughnut, and didn't draw fair\r\nwhen we had the truck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and you slapped Frank; I saw you!\xe2\x80\x9d bawled Willie Snow, bobbing up\r\nin his pew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you took my book away and hid it 'cause I wouldn't go and swing\r\nwhen you wanted me to,\xe2\x80\x9d added Annette, the oldest of the Snow trio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shan't build my house by Willie's if he don't want me to, so now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n put in little Marion, joining the mutiny.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tiss Dimmy! and I tored up my hat 'tause a pin picked me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n shouted Pokey, regardless of Jamie's efforts to restrain her.\r\n\r\nCaptain Dove looked rather taken aback at this outbreak in the ranks;\r\nbut, being a dignified and calm personage, he quelled the rising\r\nrebellion with great tact and skill, by saying, briefly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe'll sing the last hymn; 'Sweet, sweet good-by' you all know that, so\r\ndo it nicely, and then we will go and have luncheon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeace was instantly restored, and a burst of melody drowned the\r\nsuppressed giggles of Rose and Mac, who found it impossible to keep\r\nsober during the latter part of this somewhat remarkable service.\r\nFifteen minutes of repose rendered it a physical impossibility for the\r\ncompany to march out as quietly as they had marched in. I grieve to\r\nstate that the entire troop raced home as hard as they could pelt, and\r\nwere soon skirmishing briskly over their lunch, utterly oblivious of\r\nwhat Jamie (who had been much impressed by the sermon) called \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\ncaptain's beautiful teck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was astonishing how much they all found to do at Cosey Corner;\r\nand Mac, instead of lying in a hammock and being read to, as he had\r\nexpected, was busiest of all. He was invited to survey and lay out\r\nSkeeterville, a town which the children were getting up in a huckleberry\r\npasture; and he found much amusement in planning little roads, staking\r\noff house-lots, attending to the water-works, and consulting with the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cselectmen\xe2\x80\x9d about the best sites for public buildings; for Mac was a boy\r\nstill, in spite of his fifteen years and his love of books.\r\n\r\nThen he went fishing with a certain jovial gentleman from the West; and\r\nthough they seldom caught anything but colds, they had great fun and\r\nexercise chasing the phantom trout they were bound to have. Mac also\r\ndeveloped a geological mania, and went tapping about at rocks and\r\nstones, discoursing wisely of \xe2\x80\x9cstrata, periods, and fossil remains\xe2\x80\x9d;\r\nwhile Rose picked up leaves and lichens, and gave him lessons in botany\r\nin return for his lectures on geology.\r\n\r\nThey led a very merry life; for the Atkinson girls kept up a sort of\r\nperpetual picnic; and did it so capitally, that one was never tired of\r\nit. So their visitors throve finely, and long before the month was out\r\nit was evident that Dr. Alec had prescribed the right medicine for his\r\npatients.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 14--A Happy Birthday\r\n\r\nThe twelfth of October was Rose's birthday, but no one seemed to\r\nremember that interesting fact, and she felt delicate about mentioning\r\nit, so fell asleep the night before wondering if she would have any\r\npresents. That question was settled early the next morning, for she was\r\nawakened by a soft tap on her face, and opening her eyes she beheld a\r\nlittle black and white figure sitting on her pillow, staring at her with\r\na pair of round eyes very like blueberries, while one downy paw patted\r\nher nose to attract her notice. It was Kitty Comet, the prettiest of all\r\nthe pussies, and Comet evidently had a mission to perform, for a pink\r\nbow adorned her neck, and a bit of paper was pinned to it bearing the\r\nwords, \xe2\x80\x9cFor Miss Rose, from Frank.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat pleased her extremely, and that was only the beginning of the\r\nfun, for surprises and presents kept popping out in the most delightful\r\nmanner all through the day, the Atkinson girls being famous jokers and\r\nRose a favourite. But the best gift of all came on the way to Mount\r\nWindy-Top, where it was decided to picnic in honour of the great\r\noccasion. Three jolly loads set off soon after breakfast, for everybody\r\nwent, and everybody seemed bound to have an extra good time, especially\r\nMother Atkinson, who wore a hat as broad-brimmed as an umbrella, and\r\ntook the dinner-horn to keep her flock from straying away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm going to drive auntie and a lot of the babies, so you must ride the\r\npony. And please stay behind us a good bit when we go to the station,\r\nfor a parcel is coming, and you are not to see it till dinner-time. You\r\nwon't mind, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mac, in a confidential aside during the wild\r\nflurry of the start.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose. \xe2\x80\x9cIt hurts my feelings very much to be told\r\nto keep out of the way at any other time, but birthdays and Christmas it\r\nis part of the fun to be blind and stupid, and poked into corners. I'll\r\nbe ready as soon as you are, Giglamps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop under the big maple till I call then you can't possibly see\r\nanything,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mac, as he mounted her on the pony his father had sent\r\nup for his use. \xe2\x80\x9cBarkis\xe2\x80\x9d was so gentle and so \xe2\x80\x9cwillin',\xe2\x80\x9d however, that\r\nRose was ashamed to be afraid to ride him; so she had learned, that she\r\nmight surprise Dr. Alec when she got home; meantime she had many a\r\nfine canter \xe2\x80\x9cover the hills and far away\xe2\x80\x9d with Mac, who preferred Mr.\r\nAtkinson's old Sorrel.\r\n\r\nAway they went, and, coming to the red maple, Rose obediently paused;\r\nbut could not help stealing a glance in the forbidden direction before\r\nthe call came. Yes, there was a hamper going under the seat, and then\r\nshe caught sight of a tall man whom Mac seemed to be hustling into\r\nthe carriage in a great hurry. One look was enough, and with a cry of\r\ndelight, Rose was off down the road as fast as Barkis could go.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I'll astonish uncle,\xe2\x80\x9d she thought. \xe2\x80\x9cI'll dash up in grand style,\r\nand show him that I am not a coward, after all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFired by this ambition, she startled Barkis by a sharp cut, and still\r\nmore bewildered him by leaving him to his own guidance down the steep,\r\nstony road. The approach would have been a fine success if, just as Rose\r\nwas about to pull up and salute, two or three distracted hens had not\r\nscuttled across the road with a great squawking, which caused Barkis\r\nto shy and stop so suddenly that his careless rider landed in an\r\nignominious heap just under old Sorrel's astonished nose.\r\n\r\nRose was up again before Dr. Alec was out of the carryall, and threw two\r\ndusty arms about his neck crying with a breathless voice,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO uncle, I'm so glad to see you! It is better than a cart-load of\r\ngoodies, and so dear of you to come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut aren't you hurt, child! That was a rough tumble, and I'm afraid you\r\nmust be damaged somewhere,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the Doctor, full of fond anxiety,\r\nas he surveyed his girl with pride.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy feelings are hurt, but my bones are all safe. It's too bad! I was\r\ngoing to do it so nicely, and those stupid hens spoilt it all,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nRose, quite crestfallen, as well as much shaken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn't believe my eyes when I asked 'Where is Rose?' and Mac\r\npointed to the little Amazon pelting down the hill at such a rate. You\r\ncouldn't have done anything that would please me more, and I'm delighted\r\nto see how well you ride. Now, will you mount again, or shall we turn\r\nMac out and take you in?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Dr. Alec, as Aunt Jessie proposed a\r\nstart, for the others were beckoning them to follow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPride goeth before a fall better not try to show off again, ma'am,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Mac, who would have been more than mortal if he had refrained from\r\nteasing when so good a chance offered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPride does go before a fall, but I wonder if a sprained ankle always\r\ncomes after it?\xe2\x80\x9d thought Rose, bravely concealing her pain, as she\r\nanswered, with great dignity,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI prefer to ride. Come on, and see who will catch up first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was up and away as she spoke, doing her best to efface the memory\r\nof her downfall by sitting very erect, elbows down, head well up, and\r\ntaking the motion of the pony as Barkis cantered along as easily as a\r\nrocking-chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to see her go over a fence and race when we ride together.\r\nShe can scud, too, like a deer when we play 'Follow the leader,' and\r\nskip stones and bat balls almost as well as I can,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mac, in reply\r\nto his uncle's praise of his pupil.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm afraid you will think her a sad tomboy, Alec; but really she seems\r\nso well and happy, I have not the heart to check her. She has broken\r\nout in the most unexpected way, and frisks like a colt; for she says she\r\nfeels so full of spirits she must run and shout whether it is proper or\r\nnot,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mrs. Jessie, who had been a pretty hoyden years ago herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood good! that's the best news you could tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d and Dr. Alec rubbed\r\nhis hands heartily. \xe2\x80\x9cLet the girl run and shout as much as she will it\r\nis a sure sign of health, and as natural to a happy child as frisking is\r\nto any young animal full of life. Tomboys make strong women usually, and\r\nI had far rather find Rose playing football with Mac than puttering over\r\nbead-work like that affected midget, Ariadne Blish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she cannot go on playing football very long, and we must not forget\r\nthat she has a woman's work to do by and by,\xe2\x80\x9d began Mrs. Jessie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither will Mac play football much longer, but he will be all the\r\nbetter fitted for business, because of the health it gives him. Polish\r\nis easily added, if the foundations are strong; but no amount of gilding\r\nwill be of use if your timber is not sound. I'm sure I'm right, Jessie;\r\nand if I can do as well by my girl during the next six months as I have\r\nthe last, my experiment will succeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt certainly will; for when I contrast that bright, blooming face\r\nwith the pale, listless one that made my heart ache a while ago, I can\r\nbelieve in almost any miracle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Jessie, as Rose looked round\r\nto point out a lovely view, with cheeks like the ruddy apples in the\r\norchard near by, eyes clear as the autumn sky overhead, and vigour in\r\nevery line of her girlish figure.\r\n\r\nA general scramble among the rocks was followed by a regular gypsy\r\nlunch, which the young folks had the rapture of helping to prepare.\r\nMother Atkinson put on her apron, turned up her sleeves, and fell to\r\nwork as gaily as if in her own kitchen, boiling the kettle slung on\r\nthree sticks, over a fire of cones and fir boughs; while the girls\r\nspread the mossy table with a feast of country goodies, and the children\r\ntumbled about in everyone's way till the toot of the horn made them\r\nsettle down like a flock of hungry birds.\r\n\r\nAs soon as the merry meal and a brief interval of repose were over,\r\nit was unanimously voted to have some charades. A smooth, green spot\r\nbetween two stately pines was chosen for the stage; shawls hung up,\r\nproperties collected, audience and actors separated, and a word quickly\r\nchosen.\r\n\r\nThe first scene discovered Mac in a despondent attitude and shabby\r\ndress, evidently much troubled in mind. To him entered a remarkable\r\ncreature with a brown paper bag over its head. A little pink nose peeped\r\nthrough one hole in the middle, white teeth through another, and above\r\ntwo eyes glared fiercely. Spires of grass stuck in each side of the\r\nmouth seemed meant to represent whiskers; the upper corners of the bag\r\nwere twisted like ears, and no one could doubt for a moment that the\r\nblack scarf pinned on behind was a tail.\r\n\r\nThis singular animal seemed in pantomime to be comforting his master and\r\noffering advice, which was finally acted upon, for Mac pulled off his\r\nboots, helped the little beast into them, and gave him a bag; then,\r\nkissing his paw, with a hopeful gesture, the creature retired, purring\r\nso successfully that there was a general cry of \xe2\x80\x9cCat, puss, boots!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCat is the word,\xe2\x80\x9d replied a voice, and the curtain fell.\r\n\r\nThe next scene was a puzzler, for in came another animal, on all-fours\r\nthis time, with a new sort of tail and long ears. A gray shawl concealed\r\nits face, but an inquisitive sunbeam betrayed the glitter as of goggles\r\nunder the fringe. On its back rode a small gentleman in Eastern costume,\r\nwho appeared to find some difficulty in keeping his seat as his steed\r\njogged along. Suddenly a spirit appeared, all in white, with long\r\nnewspaper wings upon its back and golden locks about its face.\r\nSingularly enough, the beast beheld this apparition and backed\r\ninstantly, but the rider evidently saw nothing and whipped up\r\nunmercifully, also unsuccessfully, for the spirit stood directly in the\r\npath, and the amiable beast would not budge a foot. A lively skirmish\r\nfollowed, which ended in the Eastern gentleman being upset into a\r\nsweet-fern bush, while the better bred animal abased itself before the\r\nshining one.\r\n\r\nThe children were all in the dark till Mother Atkinson said, in an\r\ninquiring tone,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf that isn't Balaam and the ass, I'd like to know what it is. Rose\r\nmakes a sweet angel, doesn't she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAss\xe2\x80\x9d was evidently the word, and the angel retired, smiling with\r\nmundane satisfaction over the compliment that reached her ears.\r\n\r\nThe next was a pretty little scene from the immortal story of \xe2\x80\x9cBabes in\r\nthe Wood.\xe2\x80\x9d Jamie and Pokey came trotting in, hand in hand, and, having\r\nbeen through the parts many times before, acted with great ease and much\r\nfluency, audibly directing each other from time to time as they\r\nwent along. The berries were picked, the way lost, tears shed, baby\r\nconsolation administered, and then the little pair lay down among the\r\nbrakes and died with their eyes wide open and the toes of their four\r\nlittle boots turned up to the daisies in the most pathetic manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow the wobins tum. You be twite dead, Dimmy, and I'll peep in and see\r\n'em,\xe2\x80\x9d one defunct innocent was heard to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope they'll be quick, for I'm lying on a stone, and ants are walking\r\nup my leg like fury,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the other.\r\n\r\nHere the robins came flapping in with red scarves over their breasts\r\nand leaves in their mouths, which they carefully laid upon the babes\r\nwherever they would show best. A prickly blackberry leaf placed directly\r\nover Pokey's nose caused her to sneeze so violently that her little legs\r\nflew into the air; Jamie gave a startled \xe2\x80\x9cOw!\xe2\x80\x9d and the pitying fowls\r\nfled giggling.\r\n\r\nAfter some discussion it was decided that the syllable must be \xe2\x80\x9cstrew or\r\nstrow\xe2\x80\x9d and then they waited to see if it was a good guess.\r\n\r\nThis scene discovered Annette Snow in bed, evidently very ill; Miss\r\nJenny was her anxious mamma, and her merry conversation amused the\r\naudience till Mac came in as a physician, and made great fun with his\r\nbig watch, pompous manner, and absurd questions. He prescribed one\r\npellet with an unpronounceable name, and left after demanding twenty\r\ndollars for his brief visit.\r\n\r\nThe pellet was administered, and such awful agonies immediately set in\r\nthat the distracted mamma bade a sympathetic neighbour run for Mother\r\nKnow-all. The neighbour ran, and in came a brisk little old lady in\r\ncap and specs, with a bundle of herbs under her arm, which she at once\r\napplied in all sorts of funny ways, explaining their virtues as she\r\nclapped a plantain poultice here, put a pounded catnip plaster there,\r\nor tied a couple of mullein leaves round the sufferer's throat.
