Instant\r\nrelief ensued, the dying child sat up and demanded baked beans. The\r\ngrateful parent offered fifty dollars; but Mother Know-all indignantly\r\nrefused it and went smiling away, declaring that a neighbourly turn\r\nneeded no reward, and a doctor's fee was all a humbug.\r\n\r\nThe audience were in fits of laughter over this scene, for Rose imitated\r\nMrs. Atkinson capitally, and the herb cure was a good hit at the\r\nexcellent lady's belief that \xe2\x80\x9cyarbs\xe2\x80\x9d would save mankind if properly\r\napplied. No one enjoyed it more than herself, and the saucy children\r\nprepared for the grand finale in high feather.\r\n\r\nThis closing scene was brief but striking, for two trains of cars\r\nwhizzed in from opposite sides, met with a terrible collision in\r\nthe middle of the stage, and a general smash-up completed the word\r\ncatastrophe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow let us act a proverb. I've got one all ready,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, who was\r\ndying to distinguish herself in some way before Uncle Alec.\r\n\r\nSo everyone but Mac, the gay Westerner, and Rose, took their places on\r\nthe rocky seats and discussed the late beautiful and varied charade, in\r\nwhich Pokey frankly pronounced her own scene the \xe2\x80\x9cbestest of all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn five minutes the curtain was lifted; nothing appeared but a very\r\nlarge sheet of brown paper pinned to a tree, and on it was drawn a\r\nclock-face, the hands pointing to four. A small note below informed the\r\npublic that 4 A.M. was the time. Hardly had the audience grasped this\r\nimportant fact when a long waterproof serpent was seen uncoiling\r\nitself from behind a stump. An inch-worm, perhaps, would be a better\r\ndescription, for it travelled in the same humpy way as that pleasing\r\nreptile. Suddenly a very wide-awake and active fowl advanced, pecking,\r\nchirping, and scratching vigorously. A tuft of green leaves waved upon\r\nhis crest, a larger tuft of brakes made an umbrageous tail, and a shawl\r\nof many colours formed his flapping wings. A truly noble bird, whose\r\nlegs had the genuine strut, whose eyes shone watchfully, and whose voice\r\nhad a ring that evidently struck terror into the catterpillar's soul, if\r\nit was a catterpillar. He squirmed, he wriggled, he humped as fast as\r\nhe could, trying to escape; but all in vain. The tufted bird espied\r\nhim, gave one warbling sort of crow, pounced upon him, and flapped\r\ntriumphantly away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat early bird got such a big worm he could hardly carry him off,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n laughed Aunt Jessie, as the children shouted over the joke suggested by\r\nMac's nickname.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is one of uncle's favourite proverbs, so I got it up for his\r\nespecial benefit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, coming up with the two-legged worm beside\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery clever; what next?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Dr. Alec as she sat down beside him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Dove boys are going to give us an 'Incident in the Life of\r\nNapoleon,' as they call it; the children think it very splendid, and the\r\nlittle fellows do it rather nicely,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mac with condescension.\r\n\r\nA tent appeared, and pacing to and fro before it was a little sentinel,\r\nwho, in a brief soliloquy, informed the observers that the elements were\r\nin a great state of confusion, that he had marched some hundred miles\r\nor so that day, and that he was dying for want of sleep. Then he\r\npaused, leaned upon his gun, and seemed to doze; dropped slowly down,\r\noverpowered with slumber, and finally lay flat, with his gun beside him,\r\na faithless little sentinel. Enter Napoleon, cocked hat, gray coat, high\r\nboots, folded arms, grim mouth, and a melodramatic stride. Freddy Dove\r\nalways covered himself with glory in this part, and \xe2\x80\x9ctook the stage\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n with a Napoleonic attitude that brought down the house; for the\r\nbig-headed boy, with solemn, dark eyes and square brow, was \xe2\x80\x9cthe very\r\nmoral of that rascal, Boneyparty,\xe2\x80\x9d Mother Atkinson said.\r\n\r\nSome great scheme was evidently brewing in his mighty mind a trip across\r\nthe Alps, a bonfire at Moscow, or a little skirmish at Waterloo perhaps,\r\nfor he marched in silent majesty till suddenly a gentle snore disturbed\r\nthe imperial reverie. He saw the sleeping soldier and glared upon him,\r\nsaying in an awful tone,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa! asleep at his post! Death is the penalty he must die!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPicking up the musket, he is about to execute summary justice, as\r\nemperors are in the habit of doing, when something in the face of the\r\nweary sentinel appears to touch him. And well it might, for a most\r\nengaging little warrior was Jack as he lay with his shako half off, his\r\nchildish face trying to keep sober, and a great black moustache over his\r\nrosy mouth. It would have softened the heart of any Napoleon, and the\r\nLittle Corporal proved himself a man by relenting, and saying, with a\r\nlofty gesture of forgiveness,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrave fellow, he is worn out; I will let him sleep, and mount guard in\r\nhis place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, shouldering the gun, this noble being strode to and fro with a\r\ndignity which thrilled the younger spectators. The sentinel awakes,\r\nsees what has happened, and gives himself up for lost. But the Emperor\r\nrestores his weapon, and, with that smile which won all hearts, says,\r\npointing to a high rock whereon a crow happens to be sitting, \xe2\x80\x9cBe brave,\r\nbe vigilant, and remember that from yonder Pyramid generations are\r\nbeholding you,\xe2\x80\x9d and with these memorable words he vanishes, leaving the\r\ngrateful soldier bolt upright, with his hand at his temple and deathless\r\ndevotion stamped upon his youthful countenance.\r\n\r\nThe applause which followed this superb piece had hardly subsided,\r\nwhen a sudden splash and a shrill cry caused a general rush toward the\r\nwaterfall that went gambolling down the rocks, singing sweetly as it\r\nran. Pokey had tried to gambol also, and had tumbled into a shallow\r\npool, whither Jamie had gallantly followed, in a vain attempt to fish\r\nher out, and both were paddling about half frightened, half pleased with\r\nthe unexpected bath.\r\n\r\nThis mishap made it necessary to get the dripping infants home as soon\r\nas possible; so the wagons were loaded up, and away they went, as merry\r\nas if the mountain air had really been \xe2\x80\x9cOxygenated Sweets not Bitters,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n as Dr. Alec suggested when Mac said he felt as jolly as if he had been\r\ndrinking champagne instead of the current wine that came with a great\r\nfrosted cake wreathed with sugar roses in Aunt Plenty's hamper of\r\ngoodies.\r\n\r\nRose took part in all the fun, and never betrayed by look or word the\r\ntwinges of pain she suffered in her ankle. She excused herself from the\r\ngames in the evening, however, and sat talking to Uncle Alec in a lively\r\nway, that both amazed and delighted him; for she confided to him that\r\nshe played horse with the children, drilled with the light infantry,\r\nclimbed trees, and did other dreadful things that would have caused the\r\naunts to cry aloud if they knew of them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't care a pin what they say if you don't mind, uncle,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nanswered, when he pictured the dismay of the good ladies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, it's all very well to defy them, but you are getting so rampant,\r\nI'm afraid you will defy me next, and then where are we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I won't! I shouldn't dare; because you are my guardian, and can put\r\nme in a strait-jacket if you like;\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose laughed in his face, even\r\nwhile she nestled closer with a confiding gesture pleasant to see.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word, Rosy, I begin to feel like the man who bought an\r\nelephant, and then didn't know what to do with him. I thought I had got\r\na pet and plaything for years to come; but here you are growing up like\r\na bean-stalk, and I shall find I've got a strong-minded little woman on\r\nmy hands before I can turn round. There's predicament for a man and an\r\nuncle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Alec's comic distress was mercifully relieved for the time being\r\nby a dance of goblins on the lawn, where the children, with pumpkin\r\nlanterns on their heads, frisked about like will-o'-the-wisps, as a\r\nparting surprise.\r\n\r\nWhen Rose went to bed, she found that Uncle Alec had not forgotten her;\r\nfor on the table stood a delicate little easel, holding two miniatures\r\nset in velvet. She knew them both, and stood looking at them till her\r\neyes brimmed over with tears that were both sweet and sad; for they were\r\nthe faces of her father and mother, beautifully copied from portraits\r\nfast fading away.\r\n\r\nPresently, she knelt down, and, putting her arms round the little\r\nshrine, kissed one after the other, saying with an earnest voice, \xe2\x80\x9cI'll\r\ntruly try to make them glad to see me by and by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd that was Rose's little prayer on the night of her fourteenth\r\nbirthday.\r\n\r\nTwo days later the Campbells went home, a larger party than when they\r\ncame; for Dr. Alec was escort and Kitty Comet was borne in state in a\r\nbasket, with a bottle of milk, some tiny sandwiches, and a doll's dish\r\nto drink out of, as well as a bit of carpet to lie on in her palace car,\r\nout of which she kept popping her head in the most fascinating manner.\r\n\r\nThere was a great kissing and cuddling, waving of handkerchiefs, and\r\nlast good-byes, as they went; and when they had started, Mother Atkinson\r\ncame running after them, to tuck in some little pies, hot from the oven,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cfor the dears, who might get tired of bread and butter during that long\r\nday's travel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother start, and another halt; for the Snow children came shrieking\r\nup to demand the three kittens that Pokey was cooly carrying off in\r\na travelling bag. The unhappy kits were rescued, half smothered, and\r\nrestored to their lawful owners, amid dire lamentation from the little\r\nkidnapper, who declared that she only \xe2\x80\x9ctooked um 'cause they'd want to\r\ngo wid their sister Tomit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStart number three and stoppage number three, as Frank hailed them\r\nwith the luncheon basket, which had been forgotten, after everyone had\r\nprotested that it was safely in.\r\n\r\nAll went well after that, and the long journey was pleasantly beguiled\r\nby Pokey and Pussy, who played together so prettily that they were\r\nconsidered public benefactors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRose doesn't want to go home, for she knows the aunts won't let her\r\nrampage as she did up at Cosey Corner,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mac, as they approached the\r\nold house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can't rampage if I want to for a time, at least; and I'll tell you\r\nwhy. I sprained my ankle when I tumbled off of Barkis, and it gets worse\r\nand worse; though I've done all I know to cure it and hide it, so it\r\nshouldn't trouble anyone,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Rose, knitting her brows with pain,\r\nas she prepared to descend, wishing her uncle would take her instead of\r\nher bundles.\r\n\r\nHow he did it, she never knew; but Mac had her up the steps and on the\r\nparlour sofa before she could put her foot to the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you are right side up with care; and mind, now, if your ankle\r\nbothers you, and you are laid up with it, I am to be your footman. It's\r\nonly fair, you know; for I don't forget how good you have been to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n And Mac went to call Phebe, so full of gratitude and good-will that his\r\nvery goggles shone.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 15--Ear-Rings\r\n\r\nRose's sprain proved to be a serious one, owing to neglect, and Dr. Alec\r\nordered her to lie on the sofa for a fortnight at least; whereat she\r\ngroaned dismally, but dared not openly complain, lest the boys turn\r\nupon her with some of the wise little sermons on patience which she had\r\ndelivered for their benefit.\r\n\r\nIt was Mac's turn now, and honourably did he repay his debt; for, as\r\nschool was still forbidden, he had plenty of leisure, and devoted most\r\nof it to Rose. He took many steps for her, and even allowed her to teach\r\nhim to knit, after assuring himself that many a brave Scotchman knew how\r\nto \xe2\x80\x9cclick the pricks.\xe2\x80\x9d She was obliged to take a solemn vow of secrecy,\r\nhowever, before he would consent; for, though he did not mind being\r\ncalled \xe2\x80\x9cGiglamps,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cGranny\xe2\x80\x9d was more than his boyish soul could bear,\r\nand at the approach of any of the Clan his knitting vanished as if by\r\nmagic, which frequent \xe2\x80\x9cchucking\xe2\x80\x9d out of sight did not improve the stripe\r\nhe was doing for Rose's new afghan.\r\n\r\nShe was busy with this pretty work one bright October afternoon, all\r\nnicely established on her sofa in the upper hall, while Jamie and Pokey\r\n(lent for her amusement) were keeping house in a corner, with Comet and\r\nRose's old doll for their \xe2\x80\x9cchilderns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPresently, Phebe appeared with a card. Rose read it, made a grimace,\r\nthen laughed and said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll see Miss Blish,\xe2\x80\x9d and immediately put on her company face, pulled\r\nout her locket, and settled her curls.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou dear thing, how do you do? I've been trying to call every day since\r\nyou got back, but I have so many engagements, I really couldn't manage\r\nit till to-day. So glad you are alone, for mamma said I could sit\r\nawhile, and I brought my lace-work to show you, for it's perfectly\r\nlovely.\xe2\x80\x9d cried Miss Blish, greeting Rose with a kiss, which was not very\r\nwarmly returned, though Rose politely thanked her for coming, and bid\r\nPhebe roll up the easy chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow nice to have a maid!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ariadne, as she settled herself with\r\nmuch commotion. \xe2\x80\x9cStill, dear, you must be very lonely, and feel the need\r\nof a bosom friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have my cousins,\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose, with dignity, for her visitor's\r\npatronising manner ruffled her temper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGracious, child! you don't make friends of those great boys, do you?\r\nMamma says she really doesn't think it's proper for you to be with them\r\nso much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are like brothers, and my aunts do think it's proper,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\r\nRose, rather sharply, for it struck her that this was none of Miss\r\nBlish's business.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was merely going to say I should be glad to have you for my bosom\r\nfriend, for Hatty Mason and I have had an awful quarrel, and don't\r\nspeak. She is too mean to live, so I gave her up. Just think, she never\r\npaid back one of the caramels I've given her, and never invited me to\r\nher party. I could have forgiven the caramels, but to be left out in\r\nthat rude way was more than I could bear, and I told her never to look\r\nat me again as long as she lived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very kind, but I don't think I want a bosom friend, thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Rose, as Ariadne stopped to bridle and shake her flaxen head over\r\nthe delinquent Hatty Mason.\r\n\r\nNow, in her heart Miss Blish thought Rose \xe2\x80\x9ca stuck-up puss,\xe2\x80\x9d but\r\nthe other girls wanted to know her and couldn't, the old house was a\r\ncharming place to visit, the lads were considered fine fellows, and\r\nthe Campbells \xe2\x80\x9care one of our first families,\xe2\x80\x9d mamma said. So Ariadne\r\nconcealed her vexation at Rose's coolness, and changed the subject as\r\nfast as possible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStudying French, I see; who is your teacher?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, flitting over\r\nthe leaves of \xe2\x80\x9cPaul and Virginia,\xe2\x80\x9d that lay on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't study it, for I read French as well as English, and uncle and\r\nI often speak it for hours. He talks like a native, and says I have a\r\nremarkably good accent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose really could not help this small display of superiority, for French\r\nwas one of her strong points, and she was vain of it, though she usually\r\nmanaged to hide this weakness. She felt that Ariadne would be the better\r\nfor a little crushing, and could not resist the temptation to patronise\r\nin her turn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Blish, rather blankly, for French was not her\r\nstrong point by any means.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am to go abroad with uncle in a year or two, and he knows how\r\nimportant it is to understand the languages. Half the girls who leave\r\nschool can't speak decent French, and when they go abroad they are\r\nso mortified. I shall be very glad to help you, if you like, for, of\r\ncourse, you have no one to talk with at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow Ariadne, though she looked like a wax doll, had feelings within her\r\ninstead of sawdust, and these feelings were hurt by Rose's lofty tone.\r\nShe thought her more \xe2\x80\x9cstuck up\xe2\x80\x9d than ever, but did not know how to bring\r\nher down, yet longed to do it, for she felt as if she had received a\r\nbox on the ear, and involuntarily put her hand up to it. The touch of an\r\near-ring consoled her, and suggested a way of returning tit for tat in a\r\ntelling manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, dear; I don't need any help, for our teacher is from Paris,\r\nand of course he speaks better French than your uncle.\xe2\x80\x9d Then she added,\r\nwith a gesture of her head that set the little bells on her ears to\r\ntingling: \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you like my new ear-rings? Papa gave them to me last\r\nweek, and everyone says they are lovely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose came down from her high horse with a rapidity that was comical,\r\nfor Ariadne had the upper hand now. Rose adored pretty things, longed\r\nto wear them, and the desire of her girlish soul was to have her ears\r\nbored, only Dr. Alec thought it foolish, so she never had done it. She\r\nwould gladly have given all the French she could jabber for a pair of\r\ngolden bells with pearl-tipped tongues, like those Ariadne wore; and,\r\nclasping her hands, she answered, in a tone that went to the hearer's\r\nheart,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are too sweet for anything! If uncle would only let me wear some,\r\nI should be perfectly happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn't mind what he says. Papa laughed at me at first, but he likes\r\nthem now, and says I shall have diamond solitaires when I am eighteen,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Ariadne, quite satisfied with her shot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've got a pair now that were mamma's, and a beautiful little pair of\r\npearl and turquoise ones, that I am dying to wear,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen do it. I'll pierce your ears, and you must wear a bit of silk in\r\nthem till they are well; your curls will hide them nicely; then, some\r\nday, slip in your smallest ear-rings, and see if your uncle don't like\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI asked him if it wouldn't do my eyes good once when they were red, and\r\nhe only laughed. People do cure weak eyes that way, don't they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed, and yours are sort of red. Let me see. Yes, I really think\r\nyou ought to do it before they get worse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ariadne, peering into\r\nthe large clear eye offered for inspection.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes it hurt much?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, wavering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear, no; just a prick and a pull, and it's all over. I've done\r\nlots of ears, and know just how. Come, push up your hair and get a big\r\nneedle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't quite like to do it without asking uncle's leave,\xe2\x80\x9d faltered\r\nRose, when all was ready for the operation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he ever forbid it?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Ariadne, hovering over her prey like a\r\nvampire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, never!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen do it, unless you are afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Miss Blish, bent on\r\naccomplishing the deed.\r\n\r\nThat last word settled the matter, and, closing her eyes, Rose said\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPunch!\xe2\x80\x9d in the tone of one giving the fatal order \xe2\x80\x9cFire!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAriadne punched, and the victim bore it in heroic silence, though she\r\nturned pale and her eyes were full of tears of anguish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere! Now pull the bits of silk often, and cold-cream your ears every\r\nnight, and you'll soon be ready for the rings,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ariadne, well\r\npleased with her job, for the girl who spoke French with \xe2\x80\x9ca fine accent\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n lay flat upon the sofa, looking as exhausted as if she had had both ears\r\ncut off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does hurt dreadfully, and I know uncle won't like it,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Rose,\r\nas remorse began to gnaw. \xe2\x80\x9cPromise not to tell, or I shall be teased\r\nto death,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, anxiously, entirely forgetting the two little\r\npitchers gifted with eyes as well as ears, who had been watching the\r\nwhole performance from afar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever. Mercy me, what's that?\xe2\x80\x9d and Ariadne started as a sudden sound of\r\nsteps and voices came up from below.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's the boys! Hide the needle. Do my ears show? Don't breathe a word!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n whispered Rose, scrambling about to conceal all traces of their iniquity\r\nfrom the sharp eyes of the Clan.\r\n\r\nUp they came, all in good order, laden with the proceeds of a nutting\r\nexpedition, for they always reported to Rose and paid tribute to their\r\nqueen in the handsomest manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many, and how big! We'll have a grand roasting frolic after tea,\r\nwon't we?\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, plunging both hands into a bag of glossy brown\r\nnuts, while the Clan \xe2\x80\x9cstood at ease\xe2\x80\x9d and nodded to Ariadne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat lot was picked especially for you, Rosy. I got every one myself,\r\nand they are extra whackers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mac, presenting a bushel or so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have seen Giglamps when he was after them. He pitched out\r\nof the tree, and would have broken his blessed old neck if Arch had\r\nnot caught him,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Steve, as he lounged gracefully in the window\r\nseat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou needn't talk, Dandy, when you didn't know a chestnut from a beech,\r\nand kept on thrashing till I told you of it,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Mac, festooning\r\nhimself over the back of the sofa, being a privileged boy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't make mistakes when I thrash you, old Worm, so you'd better mind\r\nwhat you are about,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Steve, without a ray of proper respect for\r\nhis elder brother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is getting dark, and I must go, or mamma will be alarmed,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nAriadne, rising in sudden haste, though she hoped to be asked to remain\r\nto the nut-party.\r\n\r\nNo one invited her; and all the while she was putting on her things and\r\nchatting to Rose the boys were telegraphing to one another the sad fact\r\nthat someone ought to escort the young lady home. Not a boy felt heroic\r\nenough to cast himself into the breach, however; even polite Archie\r\nshirked the duty, saying to Charlie, as they quietly slipped into an\r\nadjoining room,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm not going to do all the gallivanting. Let Steve take that chit home\r\nand show his manners.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll be hanged if I do!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Prince, who disliked Miss Blish\r\nbecause she tried to be coquettish with him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I will,\xe2\x80\x9d and, to the dismay of both recreant lads, Dr. Alec walked\r\nout of the room to offer his services to the \xe2\x80\x9cchit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was too late, however, for Mac, obeying a look from Rose, had already\r\nmade a victim of himself, and trudged meekly away, wishing the gentle\r\nAriadne at the bottom of the Red Sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I will take this lady down to tea, as the other one has found a\r\ngentleman to go home with her. I see the lamps are lighted below, and I\r\nsmell a smell which tells me that auntie has something extra nice for us\r\nto-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he spoke, Dr. Alec was preparing to carry Rose downstairs as usual;\r\nbut Archie and Prince rushed forward, begging with penitent eagerness\r\nfor the honour of carrying her in an arm-chair. Rose consented, fearing\r\nthat her uncle's keen eye would discover the fatal bits of silk; so the\r\nboys crossed hands, and, taking a good grip of each curly pate, she was\r\nborne down in state, while the others followed by way of the banisters.\r\n\r\nTea was ordered earlier than usual, so that Jamie and his dolly could\r\nhave a taste, at least, of the holiday fun, for they were to stay till\r\nseven, and be allowed twelve roasted chestnuts apiece, which they were\r\nunder bonds not to eat till next day.\r\n\r\nTea was despatched rapidly, therefore, and the party gathered round the\r\nwide hearth in the dining-room, where the nuts were soon dancing gaily\r\non hot shovels or bouncing out among the company, thereby causing\r\ndelightful panics among the little ones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Rosy, tell us a story while we work, for you can't help much,\r\nand must amuse us as your share,\xe2\x80\x9d proposed Mac, who sat in the shade\r\npricking nuts, and who knew by experience what a capital little\r\nScheherazade his cousin was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we poor monkeys can't burn our paws for nothing, so tell away,\r\nPussy,\xe2\x80\x9d added Charlie, as he threw several hot nuts into her lap and\r\nshook his fingers afterwards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I happen to have a little story with a moral to it in my mind,\r\nand I will tell it, though it is intended for younger children than\r\nyou,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, who was rather fond of telling instructive tales.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFire away,\xe2\x80\x9d said Geordie, and she obeyed, little thinking what a\r\ndisastrous story it would prove to herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, once upon a time, a little girl went to see a young lady who was\r\nvery fond of her. Now, the young lady happened to be lame, and had\r\nto have her foot bandaged up every day; so she kept a basketful of\r\nbandages, all nicely rolled and ready. The little girl liked to play\r\nwith this basket, and one day, when she thought no one saw her, she took\r\none of the rolls without asking leave, and put it in her pocket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere Pokey, who had been peering lovingly down at the five warm nuts\r\nthat lay at the bottom of her tiny pocket, suddenly looked up and said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d in a startled tone, as if the moral tale had become intensely\r\ninteresting all at once.\r\n\r\nRose heard and saw the innocent betrayal of the small sinner, and went\r\non in a most impressive manner, while the boys nudged one another and\r\nwinked as they caught the joke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut an eye did see this naughty little girl, and whose eye do you think\r\nit was?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEye of Dod,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured conscience-stricken Pokey, spreading two chubby\r\nlittle hands before the round face, which they were not half big enough\r\nto hide.\r\n\r\nRose was rather taken aback by this reply, but, feeling that she was\r\nproducing a good effect, she added seriously,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, God saw her, and so did the young lady, but she did not say\r\nanything; she waited to see what the little girl would do about it. She\r\nhad been very happy before she took the bandage, but when it was in her\r\npocket she seemed troubled, and pretty soon stopped playing, and sat\r\ndown in a corner looking very sober. She thought a few minutes, and then\r\nwent and put back the roll very softly, and her face cleared up, and\r\nshe was a happy child again. The young lady was glad to see that, and\r\nwondered what made the little girl put it back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTonscience p'icked her,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured a contrite voice from behind the\r\nsmall hands pressed tightly over Pokey's red face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why did she take it, do you suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, in a\r\nschool-marmish tone, feeling that all the listeners were interested in\r\nher tale and its unexpected application.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was so nice and wound, and she wanted it deffly,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the\r\nlittle voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I'm glad she had such a good conscience. The moral is that people\r\nwho steal don't enjoy what they take, and are not happy till they put\r\nit back. What makes that little girl hide her face?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, as she\r\nconcluded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMe's so 'shamed of Pokey,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed the small culprit, quite overcome by\r\nremorse and confusion at this awful disclosure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Rose, it's too bad to tell her little tricks before everyone,\r\nand preach at her in that way; you wouldn't like it yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d began Dr.\r\nAlec, taking the weeper on his knee and administering consolation in the\r\nshape of kisses and nuts.\r\n\r\nBefore Rose could express her regret, Jamie, who had been reddening\r\nand ruffling like a little turkey-cock for several minutes, burst out\r\nindignantly, bent on avenging the wound given to his beloved dolly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know something bad that you did, and I'm going to tell right out. You\r\nthought we didn't see you, but we did, and you said uncle wouldn't like\r\nit, and the boys would tease, and you made Ariadne promise not to tell,\r\nand she punched holes in your ears to put ear-rings in. So now! and\r\nthat's much badder than to take an old piece of rag; and I hate you for\r\nmaking my Pokey cry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJamie's somewhat incoherent explosion produced such an effect that\r\nPokey's small sin was instantly forgotten, and Rose felt that her hour\r\nhad come.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! what! what!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the boys in a chorus, dropping their shovels\r\nand knives to gather round Rose, for a guilty clutching at her ears\r\nbetrayed her, and with a feeble cry of \xe2\x80\x9cAriadne made me!\xe2\x80\x9d she hid her\r\nhead among the pillows like an absurd little ostrich.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow she'll go prancing round with bird cages and baskets and carts and\r\npigs, for all I know, in her ears, as the other girls do, and won't she\r\nlook like a goose?\xe2\x80\x9d asked one tormentor, tweaking a curl that strayed\r\nout from the cushions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn't think she'd be so silly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mac, in a tone of\r\ndisappointment that told Rose she had sunk in the esteem of her wise\r\ncousin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat Blish girl is a nuisance, and ought not to be allowed to come here\r\nwith her nonsensical notions,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Prince, feeling a strong desire\r\nto shake that young person as an angry dog might shake a mischievous\r\nkitten.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you like it, uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Archie, who, being the head of a\r\nfamily himself, believed in preserving discipline at all costs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very much surprised; but I see she is a girl, after all, and must\r\nhave her vanities like all the rest of them,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Alec, with\r\na sigh, as if he had expected to find Rose a sort of angel, above all\r\nearthly temptations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall you do about it, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Geordie, wondering what\r\npunishment would be inflicted on a feminine culprit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs she is fond of ornaments, perhaps we had better give her a nose-ring\r\nalso. I have one somewhere that a Fiji belle once wore; I'll look it\r\nup,\xe2\x80\x9d and, leaving Pokey to Jamie's care, Dr. Alec rose as if to carry\r\nout his suggestion in earnest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood! good! We'll do it right away! Here's a gimlet, so you hold\r\nher, boys, while I get her dear little nose all ready,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Charlie,\r\nwhisking away the pillow as the other boys danced about the sofa in true\r\nFiji style.\r\n\r\nIt was a dreadful moment, for Rose could not run away she could only\r\ngrasp her precious nose with one hand and extend the other, crying\r\ndistractedly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO uncle, save me, save me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course he saved her; and when she was securely barricaded by his\r\nstrong arm, she confessed her folly in such humiliation of spirit, that\r\nthe lads, after a good laugh at her, decided to forgive her and lay all\r\nthe blame on the tempter, Ariadne. Even Dr. Alec relented so far as to\r\npropose two gold rings for the ears instead of one copper one for the\r\nnose; a proceeding which proved that if Rose had all the weakness of\r\nher sex for jewellery, he had all the inconsistency of his in giving a\r\npretty penitent exactly what she wanted, spite of his better judgment.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 16--Bread and Button-Holes\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the world is my girl thinking about all alone here, with such\r\na solemn face?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Dr. Alec, coming into the study, one November\r\nday, to find Rose sitting there with folded hands and a very thoughtful\r\naspect.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle, I want to have some serious conversation with you, if you have\r\ntime,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, coming out of a brown study, as if she had not heard\r\nhis question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm entirely at your service, and most happy to listen,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered,\r\nin his politest manner, for when Rose put on her womanly little airs he\r\nalways treated her with a playful sort of respect that pleased her very\r\nmuch.\r\n\r\nNow, as he sat down beside her, she said, very soberly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've been trying to decide what trade I would learn, and I want you to\r\nadvise me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrade, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d and Dr. Alec looked so astonished that she hastened to\r\nexplain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgot that you didn't hear the talk about it up at Cosey Corner. You\r\nsee we used to sit under the pines and sew, and talk a great deal all\r\nthe ladies, I mean and I liked it very much. Mother Atkinson thought\r\nthat everyone should have a trade, or something to make a living out of,\r\nfor rich people may grow poor, you know, and poor people have to work.\r\nHer girls were very clever, and could do ever so many things, and Aunt\r\nJessie thought the old lady was right; so when I saw how happy and\r\nindependent those young ladies were, I wanted to have a trade, and then\r\nit wouldn't matter about money, though I like to have it well enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Alec listened to this explanation with a curious mixture of\r\nsurprise, pleasure, and amusement in his face, and looked at his little\r\nniece as if she had suddenly changed into a young woman. She had grown a\r\ngood deal in the last six months, and an amount of thinking had gone on\r\nin that young head which would have astonished him greatly could he have\r\nknown it all, for Rose was one of the children who observe and meditate\r\nmuch, and now and then nonplus their friends by a wise or curious\r\nremark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI quite agree with the ladies, and shall be glad to help you decide on\r\nsomething if I can,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you incline to?\r\nA natural taste or talent is a great help in choosing, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven't any talent, or any especial taste that I can see, and that is\r\nwhy I can't decide, uncle. So, I think it would be a good plan to pick\r\nout some very useful business and learn it, because I don't do it for\r\npleasure, you see, but as a part of my education, and to be ready in\r\ncase I'm ever poor,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, looking as if she rather longed for\r\na little poverty so that her useful gift might be exercised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, there is one very excellent, necessary, and womanly\r\naccomplishment that no girl should be without, for it is a help to rich\r\nand poor, and the comfort of families depends upon it. This fine talent\r\nis neglected nowadays, and considered old-fashioned, which is a sad\r\nmistake, and one that I don't mean to make in bringing up my girl.\r\nIt should be a part of every girl's education, and I know of a most\r\naccomplished lady who will teach you in the best and pleasantest\r\nmanner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose eagerly, charmed to be met in this helpful\r\nand cordial way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHousekeeping!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Alec.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that an accomplishment?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, while her face fell, for she\r\nhad indulged in all sorts of vague, delightful dreams.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; it is one of the most beautiful as well as useful of all the\r\narts a woman can learn. Not so romantic, perhaps, as singing,\r\npainting, writing, or teaching, even; but one that makes many happy and\r\ncomfortable, and home the sweetest place in the world. Yes, you may\r\nopen your big eyes; but it is a fact that I had rather see you a good\r\nhousekeeper than the greatest belle in the city. It need not interfere\r\nwith any talent you may possess, but it is a necessary part of your\r\ntraining, and I hope that you will set about it at once, now that you\r\nare well and strong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is the lady?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, rather impressed by her uncle's earnest\r\nspeech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Plenty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she accomplished?\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose in a wondering tone, for this\r\ngreat-aunt of hers had seemed the least cultivated of them all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the good old-fashioned way she is very accomplished, and has made\r\nthis house a happy home to us all, ever since we can remember. She is\r\nnot elegant, but genuinely good, and so beloved and respected that there\r\nwill be universal mourning for her when her place is empty. No one can\r\nfill it, for the solid, homely virtues of the dear soul have gone out of\r\nfashion, as I say, and nothing new can be half so satisfactory, to me at\r\nleast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to have people feel so about me. Can she teach me to do\r\nwhat she does, and to grow as good?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, with a little prick of\r\nremorse for even thinking that Aunt Plenty was a commonplace old lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if you don't despise such simple lessons as she can give. I know\r\nit would fill her dear old heart with pride and pleasure to feel that\r\nanyone cared to learn of her, for she fancies her day gone by. Let\r\nher teach you how to be what she has been a skilful, frugal, cheerful\r\nhousewife; the maker and the keeper of a happy home, and by and by you\r\nwill see what a valuable lesson it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will, uncle. But how shall I begin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll speak to her about it, and she will make it all right with Dolly,\r\nfor cooking is one of the main things, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it is! I don't mind that a bit, for I like to mess, and used to try\r\nat home; but I had no one to tell me, so I never did much but spoil my\r\naprons. Pies are great fun, only Dolly is so cross, I don't believe she\r\nwill ever let me do a thing in the kitchen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we'll cook in the parlour. I fancy Aunt Plenty will manage her,\r\nso don't be troubled. Only mind this, I'd rather you learned how to make\r\ngood bread than the best pies ever baked. When you bring me a handsome,\r\nwholesome loaf, entirely made by yourself, I shall be more pleased than\r\nif you offered me a pair of slippers embroidered in the very latest\r\nstyle. I don't wish to bribe you, but I'll give you my heartiest kiss,\r\nand promise to eat every crumb of the loaf myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's a bargain! it's a bargain! Come and tell aunty all about it, for\r\nI'm in a hurry to begin,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, dancing before him toward the\r\nparlor, where Miss Plenty sat alone knitting contentedly, yet ready to\r\nrun at the first call for help of any sort, from any quarter.\r\n\r\nNo need to tell how surprised and gratified she was at the invitation\r\nshe received to teach the child the domestic arts which were her only\r\naccomplishments, nor to relate how energetically she set about her\r\npleasant task. Dolly dared not grumble, for Miss Plenty was the one\r\nperson whom she obeyed, and Phebe openly rejoiced, for these new lessons\r\nbrought Rose nearer to her, and glorified the kitchen in the good girl's\r\neyes.\r\n\r\nTo tell the truth, the elder aunts had sometimes felt that they did not\r\nhave quite their share of the little niece who had won their hearts long\r\nago, and was the sunshine of the house. They talked it over together\r\nsometimes, but always ended by saying that as Alec had all the\r\nresponsibility, he should have the larger share of the dear girl's love\r\nand time, and they would be contented with such crumbs of comfort as\r\nthey could get.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec had found out this little secret, and, after reproaching\r\nhimself for being blind and selfish, was trying to devise some way of\r\nmending matters without troubling anyone, when Rose's new whim suggested\r\nan excellent method of weaning her a little from himself. He did not\r\nknow how fond he was of her till he gave her up to the new teacher, and\r\noften could not resist peeping in at the door to see how she got on,\r\nor stealing sly looks through the slide when she was deep in dough, or\r\nlistening intently to some impressive lecture from Aunt Plenty. They\r\ncaught him at it now and then, and ordered him off the premises at the\r\npoint of the rolling-pin; or, if unusually successful, and, therefore,\r\nin a milder mood, they lured him away with bribes of ginger-bread, a\r\nstray pickle, or a tart that was not quite symmetrical enough to suit\r\ntheir critical eyes.\r\n\r\nOf course he made a point of partaking copiously of all the delectable\r\nmesses that now appeared at table, for both the cooks were on their\r\nmettle, and he fared sumptuously every day. But an especial relish\r\nwas given to any dish when, in reply to his honest praise of it, Rose\r\ncoloured up with innocent pride, and said modestly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI made that, uncle, and I'm glad you like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was some time before the perfect loaf appeared, for bread-making\r\nis an art not easily learned, and Aunt Plenty was very thorough in her\r\nteaching; so Rose studied yeast first, and through various stages of\r\ncake and biscuit came at last to the crowning glory of the \xe2\x80\x9chandsome,\r\nwholesome loaf.\xe2\x80\x9d It appeared at tea-time, on a silver salver, proudly\r\nborne in by Phebe, who could not refrain from whispering, with a beaming\r\nface, as she set it down before Dr. Alec,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAin't it just lovely, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a regularly splendid loaf! Did my girl make it all herself?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nasked, surveying the shapely, sweet-smelling object with real interest\r\nand pleasure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvery particle herself, and never asked a bit of help or advice\r\nfrom anyone,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Aunt Plenty, folding her hands with an air of\r\nunmitigated satisfaction, for her pupil certainly did her great credit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've had so many failures and troubles that I really thought I never\r\nshould be able to do it alone. Dolly let one splendid batch burn up\r\nbecause I forgot it. She was there and smelt it, but never did a thing,\r\nfor she said, when I undertook to bake bread I must give my whole mind\r\nto it. Wasn't it hard? She might have called me at least,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose,\r\nrecollecting, with a sigh, the anguish of that moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe meant you should learn by experience, as Rosamond did in that\r\nlittle affair of the purple jar, you remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always thought it very unfair in her mother not to warn the poor\r\nthing a little bit; and she was regularly mean when Rosamond asked for a\r\nbowl to put the purple stuff in, and she said, in such a provoking way,\r\n'I did not agree to lend you a bowl, but I will, my dear.' Ugh! I always\r\nwant to shake that hateful woman, though she was a moral mamma.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind her now, but tell me all about my loaf,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, much\r\namused at Rose's burst of indignation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's nothing to tell, uncle, except that I did my best, gave my mind\r\nto it, and sat watching over it all the while it was in the oven till I\r\nwas quite baked myself. Everything went right this time, and it came out\r\na nice, round, crusty loaf, as you see. Now taste it, and tell me if it\r\nis good as well as handsome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust I cut it? Can't I put it under a glass cover and keep it in the\r\nparlor as they do wax flowers and fine works of that sort?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat an idea, uncle! It would mould and be spoilt. Besides, people\r\nwould laugh at us, and make fun of my old-fashioned accomplishment. You\r\npromised to eat it, and you must; not all at once, but as soon as you\r\ncan, so I can make you some more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Alec solemnly cut off his favourite crusty slice, and solemnly ate\r\nit; then wiped his lips, and brushing back Rose's hair, solemnly kissed\r\nher on the forehead, saying, heartily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, it is perfect bread, and you are an honour to your teacher.\r\nWhen we have our model school I shall offer a prize for the best bread,\r\nand you will get it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've got it already, and I'm quite satisfied,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, slipping into\r\nher seat, and trying to hide her right hand which had a burn on it.\r\n\r\nBut Dr. Alec saw it, guessed how it came there, and after tea insisted\r\non easing the pain which she would hardly confess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Clara says I am spoiling my hands, but I don't care, for I've had\r\nsuch good times with Aunt Plenty, and I think she has enjoyed it as\r\nmuch as I have. Only one thing troubles me, uncle, and I want to ask\r\nyou about it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, as they paced up and down the hall in the\r\ntwilight, the bandaged hand very carefully laid on Dr. Alec's arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore little confidences? I like them immensely, so tell away, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see I feel as if Aunt Peace would like to do something for\r\nme, and I've found out what it can be. You know she can't go about like\r\nAunty Plen, and we are so busy nowadays that she is rather lonely,\r\nI'm afraid. So I want to take lessons in sewing of her. She works so\r\nbeautifully, and it is a useful thing, you know, and I ought to be a\r\ngood needlewoman as well as housekeeper, oughtn't I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless your kind little heart, that is what I was thinking of the other\r\nday when Aunt Peace said she saw you very seldom now, you were so busy I\r\nwanted to speak of it, but fancied you had as much on your hands as\r\nyou could manage. It would delight the dear woman to teach you all her\r\ndelicate handicraft, especially button-holes, for I believe that is\r\nwhere young ladies fail; at least, I've heard them say so. So, do you\r\ndevote your mind to button-holes; make 'em all over my clothes if you\r\nwant something to practice on. I'll wear any quantity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose laughed at this reckless offer, but promised to attend to that\r\nimportant branch, though she confessed that darning was her weak point.\r\nWhereupon Uncle Alec engaged to supply her with socks in all stages of\r\ndilapidation, and to have a new set at once, so that she could run the\r\nheels for him as a pleasant beginning.\r\n\r\nThen they went up to make their request in due form, to the great\r\ndelight of gentle Aunt Peace, who got quite excited with the fun that\r\nwent on while they would yarn, looked up darning needles, and fitted out\r\na nice little mending basket for her pupil.\r\n\r\nVery busy and very happy were Rose's days now, for in the morning she\r\nwent about the house with Aunt Plenty attending to linen-closets and\r\nstore-rooms, pickling and preserving, exploring garret and cellar to see\r\nthat all was right, and learning, in the good old-fashioned manner, to\r\nlook well after the ways of the household.\r\n\r\nIn the afternoon, after her walk or drive, she sat with Aunt Peace\r\nplying her needle, while Aunt Plenty, whose eyes were failing, knitted\r\nand chatted briskly, telling many a pleasant story of old times, till\r\nthe three were moved to laugh and cry together, for the busy needles\r\nwere embroidering all sorts of bright patterns on the lives of the\r\nworkers, though they seemed to be only stitching cotton and darning\r\nhose.\r\n\r\nIt was a pretty sight to see the rosy-faced little maid sitting between\r\nthe two old ladies, listening dutifully to their instructions, and\r\ncheering the lessons with her lively chatter and blithe laugh. If the\r\nkitchen had proved attractive to Dr. Alec when Rose was there at work,\r\nthe sewing-room was quite irresistible, and he made himself so agreeable\r\nthat no one had the heart to drive him away, especially when he read\r\naloud or spun yarns.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere! I've made you a new set of warm night-gowns with four\r\nbutton-holes in each. See if they are not neatly done,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, one\r\nday, some weeks after the new lessons began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven to a thread, and nice little bars across the end so I can't tear\r\nthem when I twitch the buttons out. Most superior work, ma'am, and I'm\r\ndeeply grateful; so much so, that I'll sew on these buttons myself, and\r\nsave those tired fingers from another prick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou sew them on?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, with her eyes wide open in amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a bit till I get my sewing tackle, and then you shall see what I\r\ncan do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan he, really?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose of Aunt Peace, as Uncle Alec marched off\r\nwith a comical air of importance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, I taught him years ago, before he went to sea; and I suppose\r\nhe has had to do things for himself, more or less, ever since; so he has\r\nkept his hand in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe evidently had, for he was soon back with a funny little work-bag, out\r\nof which he produced a thimble without a top; and, having threaded his\r\nneedle, he proceeded to sew on the buttons so handily that Rose was much\r\nimpressed and amused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if there is anything in the world that you cannot do,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, in a tone of respectful admiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are one or two things that I am not up to yet,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered,\r\nwith a laugh in the corner of his eye, as he waxed his thread with a\r\nflourish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to know what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBread and button-holes, ma'am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 17--Good Bargains\r\n\r\nIt was a rainy Sunday afternoon, and four boys were trying to spend it\r\nquietly in the \xe2\x80\x9cliberry,\xe2\x80\x9d as Jamie called the room devoted to books and\r\nboys, at Aunt Jessie's. Will and Geordie were sprawling on the sofa,\r\ndeep in the adventures of the scapegraces and ragamuffins whose\r\nhistories are now the fashion. Archie lounged in the easy chair,\r\nsurrounded by newspapers; Charlie stood upon the rug, in an Englishman's\r\nfavourite attitude, and, I regret to say, both were smoking cigars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my opinion that this day will never come to an end,\xe2\x80\x9d said Prince,\r\nwith a yawn that nearly rent him asunder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRead and improve your mind, my son,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Archie, peering solemnly\r\nover the paper behind which he had been dozing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't you preach, parson, but put on your boots and come out for a\r\ntramp, instead of mulling over the fire like a granny.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, thank you, tramps in an easterly storm don't strike me as amusing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n There Archie stopped and held up his hand, for a pleasant voice was\r\nheard saying outside,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre the boys in the library, auntie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear, and longing for sunshine; so run in and make it for them,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n answered Mrs. Jessie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's Rose,\xe2\x80\x9d and Archie threw his cigar into the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's that for?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Charlie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen don't smoke before ladies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue; but I'm not going to waste my weed,\xe2\x80\x9d and Prince poked his into\r\nthe empty inkstand that served them for an ash tray.\r\n\r\nA gentle tap at the door was answered by a chorus of \xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose\r\nappeared, looking blooming and breezy with the chilly air.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I disturb you, say so, and I'll go away,\xe2\x80\x9d she began, pausing on the\r\nthreshold with modest hesitation, for something in the elder boys' faces\r\nexcited her curiosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou never disturb us, cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d said the smokers, while the readers tore\r\nthemselves from the heroes of the bar-room and gutter long enough to nod\r\naffably to their guest.\r\n\r\nAs Rose bent to warm her hands, one end of Archie's cigar stuck out of\r\nthe ashes, smoking furiously and smelling strongly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you bad boys, how could you do it, to-day of all days?\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nreproachfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere's the harm?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Archie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know as well as I do; your mother doesn't like it, and it's a bad\r\nhabit, for it wastes money and does you no good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFiddlesticks! every man smokes, even Uncle Alec, whom you think so\r\nperfect,\xe2\x80\x9d began Charlie, in his teasing way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he doesn't! He has given it up, and I know why,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose\r\neagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I think of it, I haven't seen the old meerschaum since he came\r\nhome. Did he stop it on our account?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Archie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose told the little scene on the seashore in the camping-out\r\ntime.\r\n\r\nArchie seemed much impressed, and said manfully, \xe2\x80\x9cHe won't have done\r\nthat in vain so far as I'm concerned. I don't care a pin about smoking,\r\nso can give it up as easy as not, and I promise you I will. I only do it\r\nnow and then for fun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou too?\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose looked up at the bonny Prince, who never looked less\r\nbonny than at that moment, for he had resumed his cigar just to torment\r\nher.\r\n\r\nNow Charlie cared as little as Archie about smoking, but it would not\r\ndo to yield too soon: so he shook his head, gave a great puff, and said\r\nloftily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou women are always asking us to give up harmless little things just\r\nbecause you don't approve of them. How would you like it if we did the\r\nsame by you, miss?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I did harmful or silly things, I'd thank you for telling me of them,\r\nand I'd try to mend my ways,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, we'll see if you mean what you say. I'll give up smoking to\r\nplease you, if you will give up something to please me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Prince,\r\nseeing a good chance to lord it over the weaker vessel at small cost to\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll agree if it is as foolish as cigars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it's ever so much sillier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I promise; what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose quite trembled with anxiety to\r\nknow which of her pet habits or possessions she must lose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive up your ear-rings,\xe2\x80\x9d and Charlie laughed wickedly, sure that she\r\nwould never hold to that bargain.\r\n\r\nRose uttered a cry and clapped both hands to her ears where the gold\r\nrings hung.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Charlie, wouldn't anything else do as well? I've been through so\r\nmuch teasing and trouble, I do want to enjoy my pretty ear-rings, for I\r\ncan wear them now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWear as many as you like, and I'll smoke in peace,\xe2\x80\x9d returned this bad\r\nboy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill nothing else satisfy you?\xe2\x80\x9d imploringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing,\xe2\x80\x9d sternly.\r\n\r\nRose stood silent for a minute, thinking of something Aunt Jessie once\r\nsaid \xe2\x80\x9cYou have more influence over the boys than you know; use it for\r\ntheir good, and I shall thank you all my life.\xe2\x80\x9d Here was a chance to\r\ndo some good by sacrificing a little vanity of her own. She felt it was\r\nright to do it, yet found it very hard, and asked wistfully,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean never wear them, Charlie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever, unless you want me to smoke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen clinch the bargain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had no idea she would do it, and was much surprised when she took\r\nthe dear rings from her ears, with a quick gesture, and held them out to\r\nhim, saying, in a tone that made the colour come up to his brown cheek,\r\nit was so full of sweet good will,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI care more for my cousins than for my ear-rings, so I promise, and\r\nI'll keep my word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor shame, Prince! let her wear her little danglers if she likes, and\r\ndon't bargain about doing what you know is right,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Archie, coming\r\nout of his grove of newspapers with an indignant bounce.\r\n\r\nBut Rose was bent on showing her aunt that she could use her influence\r\nfor the boys' good, and said steadily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is fair, and I want it to be so, then you will believe I'm in\r\nearnest. Here, each of you wear one of these on your watch-guard\r\nto remind you. I shall not forget, because very soon I cannot wear\r\near-rings if I want to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs she spoke, Rose offered a little ring to each cousin, and the boys,\r\nseeing how sincere she was, obeyed her. When the pledges were safe, Rose\r\nstretched a hand to each, and the lads gave hers a hearty grip, half\r\npleased and half ashamed of their part in the compact.\r\n\r\nJust at that moment Dr. Alec and Mrs. Jessie came in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's this? Dancing Ladies' Triumph on Sunday?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Uncle Alec,\r\nsurveying the trio with surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, it is the Anti-Tobacco League. Will you join?\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlie,\r\nwhile Rose slipped away to her aunt, and Archie buried both cigars\r\nbehind the back log.\r\n\r\nWhen the mystery was explained, the elders were well pleased, and Rose\r\nreceived a vote of thanks, which made her feel as if she had done a\r\nservice to her country, as she had, for every boy who grows up free from\r\nbad habits bids fair to make a good citizen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish Rose would drive a bargain with Will and Geordie also, for I\r\nthink these books are as bad for the small boys as cigars for the large\r\nones,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Jessie, sitting down on the sofa between the readers,\r\nwho politely curled up their legs to make room for her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought they were all the fashion,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Alec, settling in\r\nthe big chair with Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo is smoking, but it is harmful. The writers of these popular stories\r\nintend to do good, I have no doubt, but it seems to me they fail because\r\ntheir motto is, 'Be smart, and you will be rich,' instead of 'Be honest,\r\nand you will be happy.' I do not judge hastily, Alec, for I have read a\r\ndozen, at least, of these stories, and, with much that is attractive to\r\nboys, I find a great deal to condemn in them, and other parents say the\r\nsame when I ask them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mum, that's too bad! I like 'em tip-top. This one is a regular\r\nscreamer,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey're bully books, and I'd like to know where's the harm,\xe2\x80\x9d added\r\nGeordie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have just shown us one of the chief evils, and that is slang,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n answered their mother quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust have it, ma'am. If these chaps talked all right, there'd be no fun\r\nin 'em,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA boot-black mustn't use good grammar, and a newsboy must swear a\r\nlittle, or he wouldn't be natural,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Geordie, both boys ready\r\nto fight gallantly for their favourites.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my sons are neither boot-blacks nor newsboys, and I object to\r\nhearing them use such words as 'screamer,' 'bully,' and 'buster.' In\r\nfact, I fail to see the advantage of writing books about such people\r\nunless it is done in a very different way. I cannot think they will help\r\nto refine the ragamuffins if they read them, and I'm sure they can do no\r\ngood to the better class of boys, who through these books are introduced\r\nto police courts, counterfeiters' dens, gambling houses, drinking\r\nsaloons, and all sorts of low life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome of them are about first-rate boys, mother; and they go to sea and\r\nstudy, and sail round the world, having great larks all the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have read about them, Geordie, and though they are better than the\r\nothers, I am not satisfied with these optical delusions, as I call\r\nthem. Now, I put it to you, boys, is it natural for lads from fifteen\r\nto eighteen to command ships, defeat pirates, outwit smugglers, and\r\nso cover themselves with glory, that Admiral Farragut invites them to\r\ndinner, saying, 'Noble boy, you are an honour to your country!' Or,\r\nif the hero is in the army, he has hair-breadth escapes and adventures\r\nenough in one small volume to turn his hair white, and in the end\r\nhe goes to Washington at the express desire of the President or\r\nCommander-in-chief to be promoted to no end of stars and bars. Even if\r\nthe hero is merely an honest boy trying to get his living, he is not\r\npermitted to do so in a natural way, by hard work and years of patient\r\neffort, but is suddenly adopted by a millionaire whose pocket-book he\r\nhas returned; or a rich uncle appears from sea just in the nick of time;\r\nor the remarkable boy earns a few dollars, speculates in pea-nuts or\r\nneckties, and grows rich so rapidly that Sinbad in the diamond valley is\r\na pauper compared to him. Isn't it so, boys?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, the fellows in these books are mighty lucky, and very smart, I\r\nmust say,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Will, surveying an illustration on the open page\r\nbefore him, where a small but virtuous youth is upsetting a tipsy giant\r\nin a bar-room, and under it the elegant inscription, \xe2\x80\x9cDick Dauntless\r\npunches the head of Sam Soaker.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt gives boys such wrong ideas of life and business; shows them so\r\nmuch evil and vulgarity that they need not know about, and makes the\r\none success worth having a fortune, a lord's daughter, or some worldly\r\nhonour, often not worth the time it takes to win. It does seem to me\r\nthat some one might write stories that should be lively, natural and\r\nhelpful tales in which the English should be good, the morals pure, and\r\nthe characters such as we can love in spite of the faults that all may\r\nhave. I can't bear to see such crowds of eager little fellows at the\r\nlibraries reading such trash; weak, when it is not wicked, and totally\r\nunfit to feed the hungry minds that feast on it for want of something\r\nbetter. There! my lecture is done; now I should like to hear what you\r\ngentlemen have to say,\xe2\x80\x9d and Aunt Jessie subsided with a pretty flush on\r\nthe face that was full of motherly anxiety for her boys.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom Brown just suits mother, and me too, so I wish Mr. Hughes would\r\nwrite another story as good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Archie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don't find things of this sort in Tom Brown; yet these books are\r\nall in the Sunday-school libraries\xe2\x80\x9d and Mrs. Jessie read the following\r\nparagraph from the book she had taken from Will's hand,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c'In this place we saw a tooth of John the Baptist. Ben said he could\r\nsee locust and wild honey sticking to it. I couldn't. Perhaps John used\r\na piece of the true cross for a tooth-pick.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA larky sort of a boy says that, Mum, and we skip the parts where they\r\ndescribe what they saw in the different countries,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd those descriptions, taken mostly from guidebooks, I fancy, are the\r\nonly parts of any real worth. The scrapes of the bad boys make up the\r\nrest of the story, and it is for those you read these books, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n answered his mother, stroking back the hair off the honest little face\r\nthat looked rather abashed at this true statement of the case.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyway, mother, the ship part is useful, for we learn how to sail\r\nher, and by and by that will all come handy when we go to sea,\xe2\x80\x9d put in\r\nGeordie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, then you can explain this manoeuvre to me, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d and Mrs.\r\nJessie read from another page the following nautical paragraph,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe wind is south-south-west, and we can have her up four points closer\r\nto the wind, and still be six points off the wind. As she luffs up we\r\nshall man the fore and main sheets, slack on the weather, and haul on\r\nthe lee braces.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess I could, if I wasn't afraid of uncle. He knows so much\r\nmore than I do, he'd laugh,\xe2\x80\x9d began Geordie, evidently puzzled by the\r\nquestion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHo, you know you can't, so why make believe? We don't understand half\r\nof the sea lingo, Mum, and I dare say it's all wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Will,\r\nsuddenly going over to the enemy, to Geordie's great disgust.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do wish the boys wouldn't talk to me as if I was a ship,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose,\r\nbringing forward a private grievance. \xe2\x80\x9cComing home from church this\r\nmorning, the wind blew me about, and Will called out, right in the\r\nstreet, 'Brail up the foresail, and take in the flying-jib, that will\r\nease her.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys shouted at the plaintive tone in which Rose repeated the words\r\nthat offended her, and Will vainly endeavoured to explain that he only\r\nmeant to tell her to wrap her cloak closer, and tie a veil over the\r\ntempest-tossed feathers in her hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo tell the truth, if the boys must have slang, I can bear the 'sea\r\nlingo,' as Will calls it, better than the other. It afflicts me less to\r\nhear my sons talk about 'brailing up the foresail' than doing as they\r\n'darn please,' and 'cut your cable' is decidedly preferable to 'let her\r\nrip.' I once made a rule that I would have no slang in the house. I give\r\nit up now, for I cannot keep it; but I will not have rubbishy books; so,\r\nArchie, please send these two after your cigars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Jessie held both the small boys fast with an arm round each neck,\r\nand when she took this base advantage of them they could only\r\nsquirm with dismay. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, right behind the back log,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued,\r\nenergetically. \xe2\x80\x9cThere, my hearties (you like sea slang, so I'll give\r\nyou a bit) now, I want you to promise not to read any more stuff for a\r\nmonth, and I'll agree to supply you with wholesome fare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, mother, not a single one?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn't we just finish those?\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Geordie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe boys threw away half-smoked cigars; and your books must go after\r\nthem. Surely you would not be outdone by the 'old fellows,' as you call\r\nthem, or be less obedient to little Mum than they were to Rose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourse not! Come on, Geordie,\xe2\x80\x9d and Will took the vow like a hero. His\r\nbrother sighed and obeyed, but privately resolved to finish his story\r\nthe minute the month was over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have laid out a hard task for yourself, Jessie, in trying to\r\nprovide good reading for boys who have been living on sensation stories.\r\nIt will be like going from raspberry tarts to plain bread and butter;\r\nbut you will probably save them from a bilious fever,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec,\r\nmuch amused at the proceedings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember hearing grandpa say that a love for good books was one\r\nof the best safeguards a man could have,\xe2\x80\x9d began Archie, staring\r\nthoughtfully at the fine library before him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but there's no time to read nowadays; a fellow has to keep\r\nscratching round to make money or he's nobody,\xe2\x80\x9d cut in Charlie, trying\r\nto look worldly-wise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis love of money is the curse of America, and for the sake of it men\r\nwill sell honour and honesty, till we don't know whom to trust, and it\r\nis only a genius like Agassiz who dares to say, 'I cannot waste my time\r\nin getting rich,'\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Jessie sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want us to be poor, mother?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Archie, wondering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, dear, and you never need be, while you can use your hands; but I am\r\nafraid of this thirst for wealth, and the temptations it brings. O, my\r\nboys! I tremble for the time when I must let you go, because I think it\r\nwould break my heart to have you fail as so many fail. It would be far\r\neasier to see you dead if it could be said of you as of Sumner 'No man\r\ndared offer him a bribe.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Jessie was so earnest in her motherly anxiety that her voice\r\nfaltered over the last words, and she hugged the yellow heads closer in\r\nher arms, as if she feared to let them leave that safe harbour for the\r\ngreat sea where so many little boats go down. The younger lads nestled\r\ncloser to her, and Archie said, in his quiet, resolute way,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot promise to be an Agassiz or a Sumner, mother; but I do promise\r\nto be an honest man, please God.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I'm satisfied!\xe2\x80\x9d and holding fast the hand he gave her, she sealed\r\nhis promise with a kiss that had all a mother's hope and faith in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't see how they ever can be bad, she is so fond and proud of\r\nthem,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Rose, quite touched by the little scene.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must help her make them what they should be. You have begun\r\nalready, and when I see those rings where they are, my girl is prettier\r\nin my sight than if the biggest diamonds that ever twinkled shone in her\r\nears,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Alec, looking at her with approving eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm so glad you think I can do anything, for I perfectly ache to be\r\nuseful; everyone is so good to me, especially Aunt Jessie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are in a fair way to pay your debts, Rosy, for when girls\r\ngive up their little vanities, and boys their small vices, and try to\r\nstrengthen each other in well-doing, matters are going as they ought.\r\nWork away, my dear, and help their mother keep these sons fit friends\r\nfor an innocent creature like yourself; they will be the manlier men for\r\nit, I can assure you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 18--Fashion and Physiology\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, sir, I guess you'd better step up right away, or it will be too\r\nlate, for I heard Miss Rose say she knew you wouldn't like it, and she'd\r\nnever dare to let you see her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhebe said this as she popped her head into the study, where Dr. Alec\r\nsat reading a new book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are at it, are they?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, looking up quickly, and giving\r\nhimself a shake, as if ready for a battle of some sort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, as hard as they can talk, and Miss Rose don't seem to know\r\nwhat to do, for the things are ever so stylish, and she looks elegant in\r\n'em; though I like her best in the old ones,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Phebe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a girl of sense. I'll settle matters for Rosy, and you'll lend\r\na hand. Is everything ready in her room, and are you sure you understand\r\nhow they go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, sir; but they are so funny! I know Miss Rose will think it's a\r\njoke,\xe2\x80\x9d and Phebe laughed as if something tickled her immensely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind what she thinks so long as she obeys. Tell her to do it for\r\nmy sake, and she will find it the best joke she ever saw. I expect to\r\nhave a tough time of it, but we'll win yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, as he\r\nmarched upstairs with the book in his hand, and an odd smile on his\r\nface.\r\n\r\nThere was such a clatter of tongues in the sewing-room that no one heard\r\nhis tap at the door, so he pushed it open and took an observation. Aunt\r\nPlenty, Aunt Clara, and Aunt Jessie were all absorbed in gazing at Rose,\r\nwho slowly revolved between them and the great mirror, in a full winter\r\ncostume of the latest fashion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless my heart! worse even than I expected,\xe2\x80\x9d thought the Doctor, with\r\nan inward groan, for, to his benighted eyes, the girl looked like a\r\ntrussed fowl, and the fine new dress had neither grace, beauty, nor\r\nfitness to recommend it.\r\n\r\nThe suit was of two peculiar shades of blue, so arranged that patches of\r\nlight and dark distracted the eye. The upper skirt was tied so lightly\r\nback that it was impossible to take a long step, and the under one\r\nwas so loaded with plaited frills that it \xe2\x80\x9cwobbled\xe2\x80\x9d no other word\r\nwill express it ungracefully, both fore and aft. A bunch of folds was\r\ngathered up just below the waist behind, and a great bow rode a-top. A\r\nsmall jacket of the same material was adorned with a high ruff at the\r\nback, and laid well open over the breast, to display some lace and a\r\nlocket. Heavy fringes, bows, puffs, ruffles, and revers finished off the\r\ndress, making one's head ache to think of the amount of work wasted,\r\nfor not a single graceful line struck the eye, and the beauty of the\r\nmaterial was quite lost in the profusion of ornament.\r\n\r\nA high velvet hat, audaciously turned up in front, with a bunch of pink\r\nroses and a sweeping plume, was cocked over one ear, and, with her curls\r\nbraided into a club at the back of her neck, Rose's head looked more\r\nlike that of a dashing young cavalier than a modest little girl's.\r\nHigh-heeled boots tilted her well forward, a tiny muff pinioned her\r\narms, and a spotted veil, tied so closely over her face that her\r\neyelashes were rumpled by it, gave the last touch of absurdity to her\r\nappearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow she looks like other girls, and as I like to see her,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Clara\r\nwas saying, with an air of great satisfaction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe does look like a fashionable young lady, but somehow I miss my\r\nlittle Rose, for children dressed like children in my day,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nAunt Plenty, peering through her glasses with a troubled look, for\r\nshe could not imagine the creature before her ever sitting in her lap,\r\nrunning to wait upon her, or making the house gay with a child's blithe\r\npresence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThings have changed since your day, Aunt, and it takes time to get used\r\nto new ways. But you, Jessie, surely like this costume better than the\r\ndowdy things Rose has been wearing all summer. Now, be honest, and own\r\nyou do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Clara, bent on being praised for her work.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, dear to be quite honest, then, I think it is frightful,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nMrs. Jessie, with a candour that caused revolving Rose to stop in\r\ndismay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear, hear,\xe2\x80\x9d cried a deep voice, and with a general start the ladies\r\nbecame aware that the enemy was among them.\r\n\r\nRose blushed up to her hat brim, and stood, looking, as she felt, like a\r\nfool, while Mrs. Clara hastened to explain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, I don't expect you to like it, Alec, but I don't consider\r\nyou a judge of what is proper and becoming for a young lady. Therefore,\r\nI have taken the liberty of providing a pretty street suit for Rose.\r\nShe need not wear it if you object, for I know we promised to let you do\r\nwhat you liked with the poor dear for a year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a street costume, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Doctor, mildly. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know,\r\nI never should have guessed that it was meant for winter weather and\r\nbrisk locomotion. Take a turn, Rosy, and let me see all its beauties and\r\nadvantages.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose tried to walk off with her usual free tread, but the under-skirt\r\ngot in her way, the over-skirt was so tight she could not take a long\r\nstep, and her boots made it impossible to carry herself perfectly erect.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven't got used to it yet,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, petulantly, kicking at her\r\ntrain, as she turned to toddle back again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose a mad dog or a runaway horse was after you, could you get out\r\nof the way without upsetting, Colonel,\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Doctor, with a twinkle\r\nin the eyes that were fixed on the rakish hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't think I could, but I'll try,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose made a rush across the\r\nroom. Her boot-heels caught on a rug, several strings broke, her hat\r\ntipped over her eyes, and she plunged promiscuously into a chair, where\r\nshe sat laughing so infectiously that all but Mrs. Clara joined in her\r\nmirth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say that a walking suit in which one could not walk, and a\r\nwinter suit which exposes the throat, head, and feet to cold and damp,\r\nwas rather a failure, Clara, especially as it has no beauty to reconcile\r\none to its utter unfitness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, as he helped Rose undo her\r\nveil, adding, in a low tone, \xe2\x80\x9cNice thing for the eyes; you'll soon see\r\nspots when it's off as well as when it's on, and, by and by, be a case\r\nfor an oculist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo beauty!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Clara, warmly, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, that is just a man's\r\nblindness. This is the best of silk and camel's hair, real ostrich\r\nfeathers, and an expensive ermine muff. What could be in better taste,\r\nor more proper for a young girl?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll shew you, if Rose will go to her room and oblige me by putting on\r\nwhat she finds there,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the Doctor, with unexpected readiness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlec, if it is a Bloomer, I shall protest. I've been expecting it, but\r\nI know I cannot bear to see that pretty child sacrificed to your wild\r\nideas of health.
