Tell me it isn't a Bloomer!\xe2\x80\x9d and Mrs. Clara clasped her\r\nhands imploringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank Heaven!\xe2\x80\x9d and she resigned herself with a sigh of relief, adding\r\nplaintively, \xe2\x80\x9cI did hope you'd accept my suit, for poor Rose has been\r\nafflicted with frightful clothes long enough to spoil the taste of any\r\ngirl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou talk of my afflicting the child, and then make a helpless guy like\r\nthat of her!\xe2\x80\x9d answered the Doctor, pointing to the little fashion plate\r\nthat was scuttling out of sight as fast as it could go.\r\n\r\nHe closed the door with a shrug, but before anyone could speak, his\r\nquick eye fell upon an object which caused him to frown, and demand in\r\nan indignant tone,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter all I have said, were you really going to tempt my girl with\r\nthose abominable things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought we put them away when she wouldn't wear them,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mrs.\r\nClara, whisking a little pair of corsets out of sight with guilty haste.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only brought them to try, for Rose is growing stout, and will have\r\nno figure if it is not attended to soon,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, with an air of calm\r\nconviction that roused the Doctor still more, for this was one of his\r\nespecial abominations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrowing stout! Yes, thank Heaven, she is, and shall continue to do it,\r\nfor Nature knows how to mould a woman better than any corset-maker,\r\nand I won't have her interfered with. My dear Clara, have you lost your\r\nsenses that you can for a moment dream of putting a growing girl into an\r\ninstrument of torture like this?\xe2\x80\x9d and with a sudden gesture he plucked\r\nforth the offending corsets from under the sofa cushion, and held them\r\nout with the expression one would wear on beholding the thumbscrews or\r\nthe rack of ancient times.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't be absurd, Alec. There is no torture about it, for tight lacing\r\nis out of fashion, and we have nice, sensible things nowadays. Everyone\r\nwears them; even babies have stiffened waists to support their weak\r\nlittle backs,\xe2\x80\x9d began Mrs. Clara, rushing to the defence of the pet\r\ndelusion of most women.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it, and so the poor little souls have weak backs all their days,\r\nas their mothers had before them. It is vain to argue the matter, and I\r\nwon't try, but I wish to state, once for all, that if I ever see a pair\r\nof corsets near Rose, I'll put them in the fire, and you may send the\r\nbill to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he spoke the corsets were on their way to destruction, but Mrs.\r\nJessie caught his arm, exclaiming merrily, \xe2\x80\x9cDon't burn them, for mercy\r\nsake, Alec; they are full of whalebones, and will make a dreadful odour.\r\nGive them to me. I'll see that they do no harm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhalebones, indeed! A regular fence of them, and metal gate-posts in\r\nfront. As if our own bones were not enough, if we'd give them a\r\nchance to do their duty,\xe2\x80\x9d growled the Doctor, yielding up the bone\r\nof contention with a last shake of contempt. Then his face cleared\r\nsuddenly, and he held up his finger, saying, with a smile, \xe2\x80\x9cHear those\r\ngirls laugh; cramped lungs could not make hearty music like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeals of laughter issued from Rose's room, and smiles involuntarily\r\ntouched the lips of those who listened to the happy sound.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome new prank of yours, Alec?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Aunt Plenty, indulgently, for she\r\nhad come to believe in most of her nephew's odd notions, because they\r\nseemed to work so well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, ma'am, my last, and I hope you will like it. I discovered what\r\nClara was at, and got my rival suit ready for to-day. I'm not going to\r\n'afflict' Rose, but let her choose, and if I'm not entirely mistaken,\r\nshe will like my rig best. While we wait I'll explain, and then you will\r\nappreciate the general effect better. I got hold of this little book,\r\nand was struck with its good sense and good taste, for it suggests a\r\nway to clothe women both healthfully and handsomely, and that is a great\r\npoint. It begins at the foundations, as you will see if you will look\r\nat these pictures, and I should think women would rejoice at this\r\nlightening of their burdens.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he spoke, the Doctor laid the book before Aunt Plenty, who obediently\r\nbrought her spectacles to bear upon the illustrations, and after a long\r\nlook exclaimed, with a scandalised face,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercy on us, these things are like the night-drawers Jamie wears! You\r\ndon't mean to say you want Rose to come out in this costume? It's not\r\nproper, and I won't consent to it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do mean it, and I'm sure my sensible aunt will consent when she\r\nunderstands that these well I'll call them by an Indian name, and say\r\npajamas are for underwear, and Rose can have as pretty frocks as she\r\nlikes outside. These two suits of flannel, each in one piece from head\r\nto foot, with a skirt or so hung on this easily-fitting waist, will\r\nkeep the child warm without burdening her with belts, and gathers, and\r\nbuckles, and bunches round the waist, and leave free the muscles that\r\nneed plenty of room to work in. She shall never have the back-ache if I\r\ncan help it, nor the long list of ills you dear women think you cannot\r\nescape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't consider it modest, and I'm sure Rose will be shocked at it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n began Mrs. Clara, but stopped suddenly, as Rose appeared in the doorway,\r\nnot looking shocked a bit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on, my hygienic model, and let us see you,\xe2\x80\x9d said her uncle, with\r\nan approving glance, as she walked in, looking so mischievously merry,\r\nthat it was evident she enjoyed the joke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don't see anything remarkable. That is a neat, plain suit; the\r\nmaterials are good, and it's not unbecoming, if you want her to look\r\nlike a little school-girl; but it has not a particle of style, and no\r\none would ever give it a second glance,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Clara, feeling that\r\nher last remark condemned the whole thing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly what I want,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the provoking Doctor, rubbing his hands\r\nwith a satisfied air. \xe2\x80\x9cRosy looks now like what she is, a modest little\r\ngirl, who does not want to be stared at. I think she would get a glance\r\nof approval, though, from people who like sense and simplicity rather\r\nthan fuss and feathers. Revolve, my Hebe, and let me refresh my eyes by\r\nthe sight of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was very little to see, however, only a pretty Gabrielle dress, of\r\na soft warm shade of brown, coming to the tops of a trim pair of boots\r\nwith low heels. A seal-skin sack, cap, and mittens, with a glimpse of\r\nscarlet at the throat, and the pretty curls tied up with a bright velvet\r\nof the same colour, completed the external adornment, making her look\r\nlike a robin redbreast wintry, yet warm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you like it, Rosy?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Doctor, feeling that her opinion\r\nwas more important to the success of his new idea than that of all the\r\naunts on the hill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel very odd and light, but I'm warm as a toast, and nothing seems\r\nto be in my way,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, with a skip which displayed shapely\r\ngaiters on legs that now might be as free and active as a boy's under\r\nthe modest skirts of the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can run away from the mad dogs, and walk off at a smart pace\r\nwithout tumbling on your nose, now, I fancy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, uncle! suppose the dog coming, I just hop over a wall so and when\r\nI walk of a cold day, I go like this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEntering fully into the spirit of the thing, Rose swung herself over\r\nthe high back of the sofa as easily as one of her cousins, and then\r\nwent down the long hall as if her stout boots were related to the famous\r\nseven-leaguers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere! you see how it will be; dress her in that boyish way and she\r\nwill act like a boy. I do hate all these inventions of strong-minded\r\nwomen!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mrs. Clara, as Rose came back at a run.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but you see some of these sensible inventions come from the brain\r\nof a fashionable modiste, who will make you more lovely, or what you\r\nvalue more 'stylish' outside and comfortable within. Mrs. Van Tassel\r\nhas been to Madame Stone, and is wearing a full suit of this sort. Van\r\nhimself told me, when I asked how she was, that she had given up lying\r\non the sofa, and was going about in a most astonishing way, considering\r\nher feeble health.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don't say so! Let me see that book a moment,\xe2\x80\x9d and Aunt Clara\r\nexamined the new patterns with a more respectful air, for if the elegant\r\nMrs. Van Tassel wore these \xe2\x80\x9cdreadful things\xe2\x80\x9d it would never do to be\r\nleft behind, in spite of her prejudices.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec looked at Mrs. Jessie, and both smiled, for \xe2\x80\x9clittle Mum\xe2\x80\x9d had\r\nbeen in the secret, and enjoyed it mightily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought that would settle it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with a nod.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn't wait for Mrs. Van to lead the way, and for once in my life I\r\nhave adopted a new fashion before Clara. My freedom suit is ordered, and\r\nyou may see me playing tag with Rose and the boys before long,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nMrs. Jessie, nodding back at him.\r\n\r\nMeantime Aunt Plenty was examining Rose's costume, for the hat and sack\r\nwere off, and the girl was eagerly explaining the new under-garments.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee, auntie, all nice scarlet flannel, and a gay little petticoat, and\r\nlong stockings, oh, so warm! Phebe and I nearly died laughing when I put\r\nthis rig on, but I like it ever so much. The dress is so comfortable,\r\nand doesn't need any belt or sash, and I can sit without rumpling any\r\ntrimming, that's such a comfort! I like to be tidy, and so, when I wear\r\nfussed-up things, I'm thinking of my clothes all the time, and that's\r\ntiresome. Do say you like it. I resolved I would, just to please uncle,\r\nfor he does know more about health than anyone else, I'm sure, and I'd\r\nwear a bag if he asked me to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't ask that, Rose, but I wish you'd weigh and compare the\r\ntwo suits, and then choose which seems best. I leave it to your own\r\ncommonsense,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Alec, feeling pretty sure he had won.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I take this one, of course, uncle. The other is fashionable, and\r\nyes I must say I think it's pretty but it's very heavy, and I should\r\nhave to go round like a walking doll if I wore it. I'm much obliged to\r\nauntie, but I'll keep this, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose spoke gently but decidedly, though there was a look of regret when\r\nher eye fell on the other suit which Phebe had brought in; and it was\r\nvery natural to like to look as other girls did. Aunt Clara sighed;\r\nUncle Alec smiled, and said heartily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, dear; now read this book and you will understand why I ask\r\nit of you. Then, if you like, I'll give you a new lesson; you asked for\r\none yesterday, and this is more necessary than French or housekeeping.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what?\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose caught up the book which Mrs. Clara had thrown down\r\nwith a disgusted look.\r\n\r\nThough Dr. Alec was forty, the boyish love of teasing was not yet dead\r\nin him, and, being much elated at his victory, he could not resist the\r\ntemptation of shocking Mrs. Clara by suggesting dreadful possibilities,\r\nso he answered, half in earnest, half in jest, \xe2\x80\x9cPhysiology, Rose.\r\nWouldn't you like to be a little medical student, with Uncle Doctor for\r\nteacher, and be ready to take up his practice when he has to stop? If\r\nyou agree, I'll hunt up my old skeleton to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was too much for Aunt Clara, and she hastily departed, with her\r\nmind in a sad state of perturbation about Mrs. Van Tassel's new costume\r\nand Rose's new study.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 19--Brother Bones\r\n\r\nRose accepted her uncle's offer, as Aunt Myra discovered two or three\r\ndays later. Coming in for an early call, and hearing voices in the\r\nstudy, she opened the door, gave a cry and shut it quickly, looking a\r\ngood deal startled. The Doctor appeared in a moment, and begged to know\r\nwhat the matter was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you ask when that long box looks so like a coffin I thought it\r\nwas one, and that dreadful thing stared me in the face as I opened the\r\ndoor,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mrs. Myra, pointing to the skeleton that hung from the\r\nchandelier cheerfully grinning at all beholders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a medical college where women are freely admitted, so walk in,\r\nmadam, and join the class if you'll do me the honour,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor,\r\nwaving her forward with his politest bow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo, auntie, it's perfectly splendid,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose's voice, and Rose's\r\nblooming face was seen behind the ribs of the skeleton, smiling and\r\nnodding in the gayest possible manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing, child?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Aunt Myra, dropping into a chair\r\nand staring about her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I'm learning bones to-day, and I like it so much. There are twelve\r\nribs, you know, and the two lower ones are called floating ribs, because\r\nthey are not fastened to the breastbone. That's why they go in so easily\r\nif you lace tight and squeeze the lungs and heart in the let me see,\r\nwhat was that big word oh, I know thoracic cavity,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose beamed with\r\npride as she aired her little bit of knowledge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think that is a good sort of thing for her to be poking over?\r\nShe is a nervous child, and I'm afraid it will be bad for her,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nAunt Myra, watching Rose as she counted vertebrae, and waggled a\r\nhip-joint in its socket with an inquiring expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn excellent study, for she enjoys it, and I mean to teach her how\r\nto manage her nerves so that they won't be a curse to her, as many a\r\nwoman's become through ignorance or want of thought. To make a mystery\r\nor terror of these things is a mistake, and I mean Rose shall understand\r\nand respect her body so well that she won't dare to trifle with it as\r\nmost women do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she really likes it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much, auntie! It's all so wonderful, and so nicely planned, you\r\ncan hardly believe what you see. Just think, there are 600,000,000 air\r\ncells in one pair of lungs, and 2,000 pores to a square inch of surface;\r\nso you see what quantities of air we must have, and what care we should\r\ntake of our skin so all the little doors will open and shut right. And\r\nbrains, auntie, you've no idea how curious they are; I haven't got to\r\nthem yet, but I long to, and uncle is going to show me a manikin that\r\nyou can take to pieces. Just think how nice it will be to see all the\r\norgans in their places; I only wish they could be made to work as ours\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was funny to see Aunt Myra's face as Rose stood before her talking\r\nrapidly with one hand laid in the friendliest manner on the skeleton's\r\nshoulder. Every word both the Doctor and Rose uttered hit the good lady\r\nin her weakest spot, and as she looked and listened a long array of\r\nbottles and pill-boxes rose up before her, reproaching her with the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cignorance and want of thought\xe2\x80\x9d that made her what she was, a nervous,\r\ndyspeptic, unhappy old woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don't know but you may be right, Alec, only I wouldn't carry\r\nit too far. Women don't need much of this sort of knowledge, and are not\r\nfit for it. I couldn't bear to touch that ugly thing, and it gives me\r\nthe creeps to hear about 'organs,'\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt Myra, with a sigh and her\r\nhand on her side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWouldn't it be a comfort to know that your liver was on the right side,\r\nauntie, and not on the left!\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose with a naughty laugh in her\r\neyes, for she had lately learnt that Aunt Myra's liver complaint was not\r\nin the proper place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's a dying world, child, and it don't much matter where the pain\r\nis, for sooner or later we all drop off and are seen no more,\xe2\x80\x9d was Aunt\r\nMyra's cheerful reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I intend to know what kills me if I can, and meantime, I'm going\r\nto enjoy myself in spite of a dying world. I wish you'd do so too, and\r\ncome and study with uncle, it would do you good, I'm sure,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose\r\nwent back to counting vertebrae with such a happy face, that Aunt Myra\r\nhad not the heart to say a word to dampen her ardour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it's as well to let her do what she likes the little while\r\nshe is with us. But pray be careful of her, Alec, and not allow her to\r\noverwork,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered as she went out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's exactly what I'm trying to do, ma'am, and rather a hard job\r\nI find it,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as he shut the door, for the dear aunts were\r\ndreadfully in his way sometimes.\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later came another interruption in the shape of Mac, who\r\nannounced his arrival by the brief but elegant remark,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo! what new game is this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose explained, Mac gave a long whistle of surprise, and then took a\r\npromenade round the skeleton, observing gravely,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrother Bones looks very jolly, but I can't say much for his beauty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn't make fun of him, for he's a good old fellow, and you'd be\r\njust as ugly if your flesh was off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, defending her new friend\r\nwith warmth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare say, so I'll keep my flesh on, thank you. You are so busy you\r\ncan't read to a fellow, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mac, whose eyes were better,\r\nbut still too weak for books.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't you want to come and join my class? Uncle explains it all to us,\r\nand you can take a look at the plates as they come along. We'll give\r\nup bones today and have eyes instead; that will be more interesting to\r\nyou,\xe2\x80\x9d added Rose, seeing no ardent thirst for physiological information\r\nin his face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRose, we must not fly about from one thing to another in this way,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n began Dr. Alec, but she whispered quickly, with a nod towards Mac, whose\r\ngoggles were turned wistfully in the direction of the forbidden books,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe's blue to-day, and we must amuse him; give a little lecture on eyes,\r\nand it will do him good. No matter about me, uncle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; the class will please be seated,\xe2\x80\x9d and the Doctor gave a\r\nsounding rap on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, sit by me, dear, then we can both see the pictures; and if your\r\nhead gets tired you can lie down,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, generously opening her\r\nlittle college to a brother, and kindly providing for the weaknesses\r\nthat all humanity is subject to.\r\n\r\nSide by side they sat and listened to a very simple explanation of the\r\nmechanism of the eye, finding it as wonderful as a fairy tale, for fine\r\nplates illustrated it, and a very willing teacher did his best to make\r\nthe lesson pleasant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJove! if I'd known what mischief I was doing to that mighty delicate\r\nmachine of mine, you wouldn't have caught me reading by firelight,\r\nor studying with a glare of sunshine on my book,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mac, peering\r\nsolemnly at a magnified eye-ball; then, pushing it away, he added\r\nindignantly, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy isn't a fellow taught all about his works, and how\r\nto manage 'em, and not left to go blundering into all sorts of worries?\r\nTelling him after he's down isn't much use, for then he's found it out\r\nhimself and won't thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Mac, that's just what I keep lecturing about, and people won't\r\nlisten. You lads need that sort of knowledge so much, and fathers and\r\nmothers ought to be able to give it to you. Few of them are able, and\r\nso we all go blundering, as you say. Less Greek and Latin and more\r\nknowledge of the laws of health for my boys, if I had them. Mathematics\r\nare all very well, but morals are better, and I wish, how I wish that I\r\ncould help teachers and parents to feel it as they ought.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome do; Aunt Jessie and her boys have capital talks, and I wish we\r\ncould; but mother's so busy with her housekeeping, and father with his\r\nbusiness, there never seems to be any time for that sort of thing; even\r\nif there was, it don't seem as if it would be easy to talk to them,\r\nbecause we've never got into the way of it, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor Mac was right there, and expressed a want that many a boy and girl\r\nfeels. Fathers and mothers are too absorbed in business and housekeeping\r\nto study their children, and cherish that sweet and natural confidence\r\nwhich is a child's surest safeguard, and a parent's subtlest power. So\r\nthe young hearts hide trouble or temptation till the harm is done, and\r\nmutual regret comes too late. Happy the boys and girls who tell all\r\nthings freely to father or mother, sure of pity, help, and pardon; and\r\nthrice happy the parents who, out of their own experience, and by\r\ntheir own virtues, can teach and uplift the souls for which they are\r\nresponsible.\r\n\r\nThis longing stirred in the hearts of Rose and Mac, and by a natural\r\nimpulse both turned to Dr. Alec, for in this queer world of ours,\r\nfatherly and motherly hearts often beat warm and wise in the breasts\r\nof bachelor uncles and maiden aunts; and it is my private opinion that\r\nthese worthy creatures are a beautiful provision of nature for the\r\ncherishing of other people's children. They certainly get great comfort\r\nout of it, and receive much innocent affection that otherwise would be\r\nlost.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec was one of these, and his big heart had room for every one of\r\nthe eight cousins, especially orphaned Rose and afflicted Mac; so, when\r\nthe boy uttered that unconscious reproach to his parents, and Rose added\r\nwith a sigh, \xe2\x80\x9cIt must be beautiful to have a mother!\xe2\x80\x9d the good Doctor\r\nyearned over them, and, shutting his book with a decided slam, said in\r\nthat cordial voice of his,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, look here, children, you just come and tell me all your worries,\r\nand with God's help, I'll settle them for you. That is what I'm here\r\nfor, I believe, and it will be a great happiness to me if you can trust\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can, uncle, and we will!\xe2\x80\x9d both answered, with a heartiness that\r\ngratified him much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood! now school is dismissed, and I advise you to go and refresh your\r\n600,000,000 air cells by a brisk run in the garden. Come again whenever\r\nyou like, Mac, and we'll teach you all we can about your 'works,' as you\r\ncall them, so you can keep them running smoothly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe'll come, sir, much obliged,\xe2\x80\x9d and the class in physiology went out to\r\nwalk.\r\n\r\nMac did come again, glad to find something he could study in spite of\r\nhis weak eyes, and learned much that was of more value than anything his\r\nschool had ever taught him.\r\n\r\nOf course, the other lads made great fun of the whole thing, and\r\nplagued Dr. Alec's students half out of their lives. But they kept\r\non persistently, and one day something happened which made the other\r\nfellows behave themselves for ever after.\r\n\r\nIt was a holiday, and Rose up in her room thought she heard the voices\r\nof her cousins, so she ran down to welcome them, but found no one there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, they will be here soon, and then we'll have a frolic,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid to herself, and thinking she had been mistaken she went into the\r\nstudy to wait. She was lounging over the table looking at a map when an\r\nodd noise caught her ear. A gentle tapping somewhere, and following the\r\nsound it seemed to come from the inside of the long case in which the\r\nskeleton lived when not professionally engaged. This case stood upright\r\nin a niche between two book-cases at the back of the room, a darkish\r\ncorner, where Brother Bones, as the boys would call him, was out of the\r\nway.\r\n\r\nAs Rose stood looking in that direction, and wondering if a rat had got\r\nshut in, the door of the case swung slowly open, and with a great start\r\nshe saw a bony arm lifted, and a bony finger beckon to her. For a minute\r\nshe was frightened, and ran to the study door with a fluttering heart,\r\nbut just as she touched the handle a queer, stifled sort of giggle made\r\nher stop short and turn red with anger. She paused an instant to collect\r\nherself, and then went softly toward the bony beckoner. A nearer look\r\nrevealed black threads tied to the arm and fingers, the ends of threads\r\ndisappearing through holes bored in the back of the case. Peeping into\r\nthe dark recess, she also caught sight of the tip of an elbow covered\r\nwith a rough gray cloth which she knew very well.\r\n\r\nQuick as a flash she understood the joke, her fear vanished, and with\r\na wicked smile, she whipped out her scissors, cut the threads, and\r\nthe bony arm dropped with a rattle. Before she could say, \xe2\x80\x9cCome out,\r\nCharlie, and let my skeleton alone,\xe2\x80\x9d a sudden irruption of boys, all in\r\na high state of tickle, proclaimed to the hidden rogue that his joke was\r\na failure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told him not to do it, because it might give you a start,\xe2\x80\x9d explained\r\nArchie, emerging from the closet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had a smelling bottle all ready if she fainted away,\xe2\x80\x9d added Steve,\r\npopping up from behind the great chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's too bad of you not to squawk and run; we depended on it, it's such\r\nfun to howl after you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Will and Geordie, rolling out from under\r\nthe sofa in a promiscuous heap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are getting altogether too strong-minded, Rose; most girls would\r\nhave been in a jolly twitter to see this old fellow waggling his finger\r\nat them,\xe2\x80\x9d complained Charlie, squeezing out from his tight quarters,\r\ndusty and disgusted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm used to your pranks now, so I'm always on the watch and prepared.\r\nBut I won't have Brother Bones made fun of. I know uncle wouldn't like\r\nit, so please don't,\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose just as Dr. Alec came in, and, seeing\r\nthe state of the case at a glance, he said quietly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear how I got that skeleton, and then I'm sure you will treat it with\r\nrespect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys settled down at once on any article of furniture that was\r\nnearest and listened dutifully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYears ago, when I was in the hospital, a poor fellow was brought there\r\nwith a rare and very painful disease. There was no hope for him, but we\r\ndid our best, and he was so grateful that when he died he left us his\r\nbody that we might discover the mysteries of his complaint, and so be\r\nable to help others afflicted in the same way. It did do good, and his\r\nbrave patience made us remember him long after he was gone. He thought\r\nI had been kind to him, and said to a fellow-student of mine, 'Tell the\r\nDoctor I lave him me bones, for I've nothing else in the wide world, and\r\nI'll nos be wanting 'em at all, at all, when the great pain hat kilt me\r\nentirely.' So that is how they came to be mine, and why I've kept them\r\ncarefully, for, though only a poor, ignorant fellow, Mike Nolan did what\r\nhe could to help others, and prove his gratitude to those who tried to\r\nhelp him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Dr. Alec paused, Archie closed the door of the case as respectfully\r\nas if the mummy of an Egyptian king was inside; Will and Geordie looked\r\nsolemnly at one another, evidently much impressed, and Charlie pensively\r\nremarked from the coal-hod where he sat,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've often heard of a skeleton in the house, but I think few people\r\nhave one as useful and as interesting as ours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 20--Under The Mistletoe\r\n\r\nRose made Phebe promise that she would bring her stocking into the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBower,\xe2\x80\x9d as she called her pretty room, on Christmas morning, because\r\nthat first delicious rummage loses half its charm if two little\r\nnight-caps at least do not meet over the treasures, and two happy voices\r\nOh and Ah together.\r\n\r\nSo when Rose opened her eyes that day they fell upon faithful Phebe,\r\nrolled up in a shawl, sitting on the rug before a blazing fire, with her\r\nuntouched stocking laid beside her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMerry Christmas!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the little mistress smiling gaily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMerry Christmas!\xe2\x80\x9d answered the little maid, so heartily that it did one\r\ngood to hear her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring the stockings right away, Phebe, and let's see what we've got,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Rose, sitting up among the pillows, and looking as eager as a\r\nchild.\r\n\r\nA pair of long knobby hose were laid out upon the coverlet, and their\r\ncontents examined with delight, though each knew every blessed thing\r\nthat had been put into the other's stocking.\r\n\r\nNever mind what they were; it is evident that they were quite\r\nsatisfactory, for as Rose leaned back, she said, with a luxurious sigh\r\nof satisfaction, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, I believe I've got everything in the world that I\r\nwant,\xe2\x80\x9d and Phebe answered, smiling over a lapful of treasures, \xe2\x80\x9cThis is\r\nthe most splendid Christmas I ever had since I was born.\xe2\x80\x9d Then she added\r\nwith an important air,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo wish for something else, because I happen to know of two more\r\npresents outside the door this minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, me, what richness!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, much excited. \xe2\x80\x9cI used to wish for\r\na pair of glass slippers like Cinderella's, but as I can't have them, I\r\nreally don't know what to ask for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhebe clapped her hands as she skipped off the bed and ran to the door,\r\nsaying merrily, \xe2\x80\x9cOne of them is for your feet, anyway. I don't know what\r\nyou'll say to the other, but I think it's elegant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo did Rose, when a shining pair of skates and a fine sled appeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle sent those; I know he did; and, now I see them, I remember that\r\nI did want to skate and coast. Isn't it a beauty? See! they fit nicely,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n and, sitting on the new sled, Rose tried a skate on her little bare\r\nfoot, while Phebe stood by admiring the pretty tableau.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we must hurry and get dressed, for there is a deal to do to-day,\r\nand I want to get through in time to try my sled before dinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGracious me, and I ought to be dusting my parlors this blessed minute!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n and mistress and maid separated with such happy faces that anyone would\r\nhave known what day it was without being told.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBirnam Wood has come to Dunsinane, Rosy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, as he left the\r\nbreakfast table to open the door for a procession of holly, hemlock, and\r\ncedar boughs that came marching up the steps.\r\n\r\nSnowballs and \xe2\x80\x9cMerry Christmases!\xe2\x80\x9d flew about pretty briskly for several\r\nminutes; then all fell to work trimming the old house, for the family\r\nalways dined together there on that day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI rode miles and mileses, as Ben says, to get this fine bit, and I'm\r\ngoing to hang it there as the last touch to the rig-a-madooning,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nCharlie, as he fastened a dull green branch to the chandelier in the\r\nfront parlor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn't very pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, who was trimming the chimney-piece\r\nwith glossy holly sprays.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind that, it's mistletoe, and anyone who stands under it will\r\nget kissed whether they like it or not. Now's your time, ladies,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n answered the saucy Prince, keeping his place and looking sentimentally\r\nat the girls, who retired precipitately from the dangerous spot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won't catch me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, with great dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee if I don't!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've got my eye on Phebe,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Will, in a patronising tone that\r\nmade them all laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless the dear; I shan't mind it a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Phebe, with such a\r\nmaternal air that Will's budding gallantry was chilled to death.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the mistletoe bough,\xe2\x80\x9d sang Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the mistletoe bough!\xe2\x80\x9d echoed all the boys, and the teasing ended in\r\nthe plaintive ballad they all liked so well.\r\n\r\nThere was plenty of time to try the new skates before dinner, and then\r\nRose took her first lesson on the little bay, which seemed to have\r\nfrozen over for that express purpose. She found tumbling down and\r\ngetting up again warm work for a time, but with six boys to teach her,\r\nshe managed at last to stand alone; and, satisfied with that success,\r\nshe refreshed herself with a dozen grand coasts on the Amazon, as her\r\nsled was called.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that fatal colour! it breaks my heart to see it,\xe2\x80\x9d croaked Aunt\r\nMyra, as Rose came down a little late, with cheeks almost as ruddy\r\nas the holly berries on the wall, and every curl as smooth as Phebe's\r\ncareful hands could make it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm glad to see that Alec allows the poor child to make herself pretty\r\nin spite of his absurd notions,\xe2\x80\x9d added Aunt Clara, taking infinite\r\nsatisfaction in the fact that Rose's blue silk dress had three frills on\r\nit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe's a very intelligent child, and has a nice little manner of her\r\nown,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Aunt Jane, with unusual affability; for Rose had just\r\nhanded Mac a screen to guard his eyes from the brilliant fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I had a daughter like that to show my Jem when he gets home, I\r\nshould be a very proud and happy woman,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Aunt Jessie, and then\r\nreproached herself for not being perfectly satisfied with her four brave\r\nlads.\r\n\r\nAunt Plenty was too absorbed in the dinner to have an eye for anything\r\nelse; if she had not been, she would have seen what an effect her new\r\ncap produced upon the boys. The good lady owned that she did \xe2\x80\x9clove a\r\ndressy cap,\xe2\x80\x9d and on this occasion her head gear was magnificent; for\r\nthe towering structure of lace was adorned with buff ribbons to such an\r\nextent that it looked as if a flock of yellow butterflies had settled on\r\nher dear old head. When she trotted about the rooms the ruches quivered,\r\nthe little bows all stood erect, and the streamers waved in the breeze\r\nso comically that it was absolutely necessary for Archie to smother the\r\nBrats in the curtains till they had had their first laugh out.\r\n\r\nUncle Mac had brought Fun See to dinner, and it was a mercy he did,\r\nfor the elder lads found a vent for their merriment in joking the young\r\nChinaman on his improved appearance. He was in American costume now,\r\nwith a cropped head, and spoke remarkably good English after six months\r\nat school; but, for all that, his yellow face and beady eyes made a\r\ncurious contrast to the blonde Campbells all about him. Will called him\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cTyphoon,\xe2\x80\x9d meaning Tycoon, and the name stuck to him to his great\r\ndisgust.\r\n\r\nAunt Peace was brought down and set in the chair of state at table, for\r\nshe never failed to join the family on this day, and sat smiling at them\r\nall, \xe2\x80\x9clike an embodiment of Peace on earth,\xe2\x80\x9d Uncle Alec said, as he took\r\nhis place beside her, while Uncle Mac supported Aunt Plenty at the other\r\nend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ate hardly any breakfast, and I've done everything I know to make\r\nmyself extra hungry, but I really don't think I can eat straight\r\nthrough, unless I burst my buttons off,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Geordie to Will, as\r\nhe surveyed the bounteous stores before him with a hopeless sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fellow never knows what he can do till he tries,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Will,\r\nattacking his heaped-up plate with an evident intention of doing his\r\nduty like a man.\r\n\r\nEverybody knows what a Christmas dinner is, so we need waste no words in\r\ndescribing this one, but hasten at once to tell what happened at the\r\nend of it. The end, by the way, was so long in coming that the gas was\r\nlighted before dessert was over, for a snow flurry had come on and the\r\nwintry daylight faded fast. But that only made it all the jollier in the\r\nwarm, bright rooms, full of happy souls. Everyone was very merry, but\r\nArchie seemed particularly uplifted so much so, that Charlie confided to\r\nRose that he was afraid the Chief had been at the decanters.\r\n\r\nRose indignantly denied the insinuation, for when healths were drunk\r\nin the good old-fashioned way to suit the elders, she had observed that\r\nAunt Jessie's boys filled their glasses with water, and had done the\r\nsame herself in spite of the Prince's jokes about \xe2\x80\x9cthe rosy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Archie certainly was unusually excited, and when someone remembered\r\nthat it was the anniversary of Uncle Jem's wedding, and wished he was\r\nthere to make a speech, his son electrified the family by trying to do\r\nit for him. It was rather incoherent and flowery, as maiden speeches are\r\napt to be, but the end was considered superb; for, turning to his mother\r\nwith a queer little choke in his voice, he said that she \xe2\x80\x9cdeserved to be\r\nblessed with peace and plenty, to be crowned with roses and lads'-love,\r\nand to receive the cargo of happiness sailing home to her in spite of\r\nwind or tide to add another Jem to the family jewels.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat allusion to the Captain, now on his return trip, made Mrs. Jessie\r\nsob in her napkin, and set the boys cheering. Then, as if that was not\r\nsensation enough, Archie suddenly dashed out of the room, as if he had\r\nlost his wits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo bashful to stay and be praised,\xe2\x80\x9d began Charlie, excusing the\r\npeculiarities of his chief as in duty bound.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPhebe beckoned to him; I saw her,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, staring hard at the\r\ndoor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it more presents coming?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jamie, just as his brother\r\nre-appeared, looking more excited than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; a present for mother, and here it is!\xe2\x80\x9d roared Archie, flinging\r\nwide the door to let in a tall man, who cried out,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere's my little woman? The first kiss for her, then the rest may come\r\non as fast as they like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBefore the words were out of his mouth, Mrs. Jessie was half-hidden\r\nunder his rough great-coat, and four boys were prancing about him\r\nclamouring for their turn.\r\n\r\nOf course, there was a joyful tumult for a time, during which Rose\r\nslipped into the window recess and watched what went on, as if it were\r\na chapter in a Christmas story. It was good to see bluff Uncle Jem look\r\nproudly at his tall son, and fondly hug the little ones. It was better\r\nstill to see him shake his brothers' hands as if he would never leave\r\noff, and kiss all the sisters in a way that made even solemn Aunt Myra\r\nbrighten up for a minute. But it was best of all to see him finally\r\nestablished in grandfather's chair, with his \xe2\x80\x9clittle woman\xe2\x80\x9d beside him,\r\nhis three youngest boys in his lap, and Archie hovering over him like a\r\nlarge-sized cherub. That really was, as Charlie said, \xe2\x80\x9cA landscape to do\r\none's heart good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll hearty and all here, thank God!\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Jem in the first\r\npause that came, as he looked about him with a grateful face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll but Rose,\xe2\x80\x9d answered loyal little Jamie, remembering the absent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, I forgot the child! Where is George's little girl?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the\r\nCaptain, who had not seen her since she was a baby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou'd better say Alec's great girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Mac, who professed to\r\nbe madly jealous of his brother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere I am, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose appeared from behind the curtains, looking as\r\nif she had rather have stayed there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSaint George Germain, how the mite has grown!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Captain Jem, as he\r\ntumbled the boys out of his lap, and rose to greet the tall girl, like\r\na gentleman as he was. But, somehow, when he shook her hand it looked so\r\nsmall in his big one, and her face reminded him so strongly of his dead\r\nbrother, that he was not satisfied with so cold a welcome, and with\r\na sudden softening of the keen eyes he took her up in his arms,\r\nwhispering, with a rough cheek against her smooth one,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod bless you, child! forgive me if I forgot you for a minute, and be\r\nsure that not one of your kinsfolk is happier to see you here than Uncle\r\nJem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat made it all right; and when he set her down, Rose's face was\r\nso bright it was evident that some spell had been used to banish the\r\nfeeling of neglect that had kept her moping behind the curtain so long.\r\n\r\nThat everyone sat round and heard all about the voyage home how the\r\nCaptain had set his heart on getting there in time to keep Christmas;\r\nhow everything had conspired to thwart his plan; and how, at the very\r\nlast minute, he had managed to do it, and had sent a telegram to Archie,\r\nbidding him keep the secret, and be ready for his father at any moment,\r\nfor the ship got into another port, and he might be late.\r\n\r\nThen Archie told how that telegram had burnt in his pocket all\r\ndinner-time; how he had to take Phebe into his confidence, and how\r\nclever she was to keep the Captain back till the speech was over and he\r\ncould come in with effect.\r\n\r\nThe elders would have sat and talked all the evening, but the young\r\nfolks were bent on having their usual Christmas frolic; so, after an\r\nhour of pleasant chat, they began to get restless, and having consulted\r\ntogether in dumb show, they devised a way to very effectually break up\r\nthe family council.\r\n\r\nSteve vanished, and, sooner than the boys imagined Dandy could get\r\nhimself up, the skirl of the bag-pipe was heard in the hall, and the\r\nbonny piper came to lead Clan Campbell to the revel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDraw it mild, Stevie, my man; ye play unco weel, but ye mak a most\r\ninfernal din,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Uncle Jem, with his hands over his ears, for this\r\naccomplishment was new to him, and \xe2\x80\x9ctook him all aback,\xe2\x80\x9d as he expressed\r\nit.\r\n\r\nSo Steve droned out a Highland reel as softly as he could, and the boys\r\ndanced it to a circle of admiring relations. Captain Jem was a true\r\nsailor, however, and could not stand idle while anything lively was\r\ngoing on; so, when the piper's breath gave out, he cut a splendid\r\npigeon-wing into the middle of the hall, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cWho can dance a Fore\r\nand After?\xe2\x80\x9d and, waiting for no reply, began to whistle the air so\r\ninvitingly that Mrs Jessie \xe2\x80\x9cset\xe2\x80\x9d to him laughing like a girl; Rose and\r\nCharlie took their places behind, and away went the four with a spirit\r\nand skill that inspired all the rest to \xe2\x80\x9ccut in\xe2\x80\x9d as fast as they could.\r\n\r\nThat was a grand beginning, and they had many another dance before\r\nanyone would own they were tired. Even Fun See distinguished himself\r\nwith Aunt Plenty, whom he greatly admired as the stoutest lady in the\r\ncompany; plumpness being considered a beauty in his country. The merry\r\nold soul professed herself immensely flattered by his admiration, and\r\nthe boys declared she \xe2\x80\x9cset her cap at him,\xe2\x80\x9d else he would never have\r\ndared to catch her under the mistletoe, and, rising on the tips of his\r\nown toes, gallantly salute her fat cheek.\r\n\r\nHow they all laughed at her astonishment, and how Fun's little black\r\neyes twinkled over this exploit! Charlie put him up to it, and Charlie\r\nwas so bent on catching Rose, that he laid all sorts of pitfalls for\r\nher, and bribed the other lads to help him. But Rose was wide-awake,\r\nand escaped all his snares, professing great contempt for such foolish\r\ncustoms. Poor Phebe did not fare so well, and Archie was the only one\r\nwho took a base advantage of her as she stood innocently offering tea to\r\nAunt Myra, whom she happened to meet just under the fatal bough. If\r\nhis father's arrival had not rather upset him, I doubt if the dignified\r\nChief would have done it, for he apologized at once in the handsomest\r\nmanner, and caught the tray that nearly dropped from Phebe's hands.\r\n\r\nJamie boldly invited all the ladies to come and salute him; and as for\r\nUncle Jem, he behaved as if the entire room was a grove of mistletoe.\r\nUncle Alec slyly laid a bit of it on Aunt Peace's cap, and then softly\r\nkissed her; which little joke seemed to please her very much, for she\r\nliked to have part in all the home pastimes, and Alec was her favourite\r\nnephew.\r\n\r\nCharlie alone failed to catch his shy bird, and the oftener she escaped\r\nthe more determined he was to ensnare her. When every other wile had\r\nbeen tried in vain, he got Archie to propose a game with forfeits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand that dodge,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Rose, and was on her guard so\r\ncarefully that not one among the pile soon collected belonged to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow let us redeem them and play something else,\xe2\x80\x9d said Will, quite\r\nunconscious of the deeply-laid plots all about him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne more round and then we will,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the Prince, who had now\r\nbaited his trap anew.\r\n\r\nJust as the question came to Rose, Jamie's voice was heard in the hall,\r\ncrying distressfully, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, come quick, quick!\xe2\x80\x9d Rose started up, missed\r\nthe question, and was greeted with a general cry of \xe2\x80\x9cForfeit! forfeit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n in which the little traitor came to join.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I've got her,\xe2\x80\x9d thought the young rascal, exulting in his fun-loving\r\nsoul.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I'm lost,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Rose, as she gave up her pin-cushion with a\r\nsternly defiant look that would have daunted anyone but the reckless\r\nPrince. In fact, it made even him think twice, and resolve to \xe2\x80\x9clet Rose\r\noff easy,'' she had been so clever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere's a very pretty pawn, and what shall be done to redeem it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nSteve, holding the pin-cushion over Charlie's head, for he had insisted\r\non being judge, and kept that for the last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine or superfine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHum, well, she shall take old Mac under the mistletoe, and kiss him\r\nprettily. Won't he be mad, though?\xe2\x80\x9d and this bad boy chuckled over the\r\ndiscomfort he had caused two harmless beings.\r\n\r\nThere was an impressive pause among the young folks in their corner, for\r\nthey all knew that Mac would \xe2\x80\x9cbe mad,\xe2\x80\x9d since he hated nonsense of this\r\nsort, and had gone to talk with the elders when the game began. At\r\nthis moment he was standing before the fire, listening to a discussion\r\nbetween his uncles and his father, looking as wise as a young owl, and\r\nblissfully unconscious of the plots against him.\r\n\r\nCharlie expected that Rose would say, \xe2\x80\x9cI won't!\xe2\x80\x9d therefore he was rather\r\nastonished, not to say gratified, when, after a look at the victim,\r\nshe laughed suddenly, and, going up to the group of gentlemen, drew her\r\nuncle Mac under the mistletoe and surprised him with a hearty kiss.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said the innocent gentleman, looking much pleased\r\nat the unexpected honour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come; that's not fair,\xe2\x80\x9d began Charlie. But Rose cut him short by\r\nsaying, as she made him a fine courtesy,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said 'Old Mac,' and though it was very disrespectful, I did it.\r\nThat was your last chance, sir, and you've lost it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe certainly had, for, as he spoke, Rose pulled down the mistletoe and\r\nthrew it into the fire, while the boys jeered at the crestfallen Prince,\r\nand exalted quick-witted Rose to the skies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's the joke?\xe2\x80\x9d asked young Mac, waked out of a brown study by the\r\nlaughter, in which the elders joined.\r\n\r\nBut there was a regular shout when, the matter having been explained to\r\nhim, Mac took a meditative stare at Rose through his goggles, and said\r\nin a philosophical tone, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don't think I should have minded much\r\nif she had done it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat tickled the lads immensely, and nothing but the appearance of a\r\nslight refection would have induced them to stop chaffing the poor Worm,\r\nwho could not see anything funny in the beautiful resignation he had\r\nshown on this trying occasion.\r\n\r\nSoon after this, the discovery of Jamie curled up in the sofa corner, as\r\nsound asleep as a dormouse, suggested the propriety of going home, and a\r\ngeneral move was made.\r\n\r\nThey were all standing about the hall lingering over the good-nights,\r\nwhen the sound of a voice softly singing \xe2\x80\x9cSweet Home,\xe2\x80\x9d made them pause\r\nand listen. It was Phebe, poor little Phebe, who never had a home, never\r\nknew the love of father or mother, brother or sister; who stood all\r\nalone in the wide world, yet was not sad nor afraid, but took her bits\r\nof happiness gratefully, and sung over her work without a thought of\r\ndiscontent.\r\n\r\nI fancy the happy family standing there together remembered this and\r\nfelt the beauty of it, for when the solitary voice came to the burden of\r\nits song, other voices took it up and finished it so sweetly, that the\r\nold house seemed to echo the word \xe2\x80\x9cHome\xe2\x80\x9d in the ears of both the orphan\r\ngirls, who had just spent their first Christmas under its hospitable\r\nroof.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 21--A Scare\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrother Alec, you surely don't mean to allow that child to go out such\r\na bitter cold day as this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Myra, looking into the study,\r\nwhere the Doctor sat reading his paper, one February morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not? If a delicate invalid like yourself can bear it, surely\r\nmy hearty girl can, especially as she is dressed for cold weather,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n answered Dr. Alec with provoking confidence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have no idea how sharp the wind is. I am chilled to the very\r\nmarrow of my bones,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Aunt Myra, chafing the end of her purple\r\nnose with her sombre glove.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't doubt it, ma'am, if you will wear crape and silk instead of fur\r\nand flannel. Rosy goes out in all weathers, and will be none the worse\r\nfor an hour's brisk skating.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I warn you that you are trifling with the child's health, and\r\ndepending too much on the seeming improvement she has made this year.\r\nShe is a delicate creature for all that, and will drop away suddenly at\r\nthe first serious attack, as her poor mother did,\xe2\x80\x9d croaked Aunt Myra,\r\nwith a despondent wag of the big bonnet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll risk it,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Alec, knitting his brows, as he always did\r\nwhen any allusion was made to that other Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMark my words, you will repent it,\xe2\x80\x9d and with that awful prophecy, Aunt\r\nMyra departed like a black shadow.\r\n\r\nNow it must be confessed that among the Doctor's failings and he had his\r\nshare was a very masculine dislike of advice which was thrust upon him\r\nunasked. He always listened with respect to the great-aunts, and often\r\nconsulted Mrs. Jessie; but the other three ladies tried his patience\r\nsorely, by constant warnings, complaints and counsels. Aunt Myra was an\r\nespecial trial, and he always turned contrary the moment she began\r\nto talk. He could not help it, and often laughed about it with comic\r\nfrankness. Here now was a sample of it, for he had just been thinking\r\nthat Rose had better defer her run till the wind went down and the sun\r\nwas warmer. But Aunt Myra spoke, and he could not resist the temptation\r\nto make light of her advice, and let Rose brave the cold. He had no\r\nfear of its harming her, for she went out every day, and it was a great\r\nsatisfaction to him to see her run down the avenue a minute afterward,\r\nwith her skates on her arm, looking like a rosy-faced Esquimaux in her\r\nseal-skin suit, as she smiled at Aunt Myra stalking along as solemnly as\r\na crow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope the child won't stay out long, for this wind is enough to chill\r\nthe marrow in younger bones than Myra's,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Dr. Alec, half an\r\nhour later, as he drove toward the city to see the few patients he had\r\nconsented to take for old acquaintance' sake.\r\n\r\nThe thought returned several times that morning, for it was truly a\r\nbitter day, and, in spite of his bear-skin coat, the Doctor shivered.\r\nBut he had great faith in Rose's good sense, and it never occurred\r\nto him that she was making a little Casabianca of herself, with the\r\ndifference of freezing instead of burning at her post.\r\n\r\nYou see, Mac had made an appointment to meet her at a certain spot, and\r\nhave a grand skating bout as soon as the few lessons he was allowed were\r\nover. She had promised to wait for him, and did so with a faithfulness\r\nthat cost her dear, because Mac forgot his appointment when the lessons\r\nwere done, and became absorbed in a chemical experiment, till a general\r\ncombustion of gases drove him out of his laboratory. Then he suddenly\r\nremembered Rose, and would gladly have hurried away to her, but his\r\nmother forbade his going out, for the sharp wind would hurt his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will wait and wait, mother, for she always keeps her word, and\r\nI told her to hold on till I came,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Mac, with visions of a\r\nshivering little figure watching on the windy hill-top.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, your uncle won't let her go out such a day as this. If he\r\ndoes, she will have the sense to come here for you, or to go home again\r\nwhen you don't appear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt Jane, returning to her \xe2\x80\x9cWatts on the\r\nMind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish Steve would just cut up and see if she's there, since I can't\r\ngo,\xe2\x80\x9d began Mac, anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteve won't stir a peg, thank you. He's got his own toes to thaw\r\nout, and wants his dinner,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dandy, just in from school, and\r\nwrestling impatiently with his boots.\r\n\r\nSo Mac resigned himself, and Rose waited dutifully till dinner-time\r\nassured her that her waiting was in vain. She had done her best to keep\r\nwarm, had skated till she was tired and hot, then stood watching others\r\ntill she was chilled; tried to get up a glow again by trotting up and\r\ndown the road, but failed to do so, and finally cuddled disconsolately\r\nunder a pine-tree to wait and watch. When she at length started for\r\nhome, she was benumbed with cold, and could hardly make her way against\r\nthe wind that buffeted the frost-bitten rose most unmercifully.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec was basking in the warmth of the study fire, after his drive,\r\nwhen the sound of a stifled sob made him hurry to the door and look\r\nanxiously into the hall. Rose lay in a shivering bunch near the\r\nregister, with her things half off, wringing her hands, and trying\r\nnot to cry with the pain returning warmth brought to her half-frozen\r\nfingers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy darling, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d and Uncle Alec had her in his arms in a\r\nminute.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMac didn't come I can't get warm the fire makes me ache!\xe2\x80\x9d and with a\r\nlong shiver Rose burst out crying, while her teeth chattered, and her\r\npoor little nose was so blue, it made one's heart ache to see it.\r\n\r\nIn less time than it takes to tell it, Dr. Alec had her on the sofa\r\nrolled up in the bear-skin coat, with Phebe rubbing her cold feet while\r\nhe rubbed the aching hands, and Aunt Plenty made a comfortable hot\r\ndrink, and Aunt Peace sent down her own foot-warmer and embroidered\r\nblanket \xe2\x80\x9cfor the dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFull of remorseful tenderness, Uncle Alec worked over his new patient\r\ntill she declared she was all right again. He would not let her get up\r\nto dinner, but fed her himself, and then forgot his own while he sat\r\nwatching her fall into a drowse, for Aunt Plenty's cordial made her\r\nsleepy.\r\n\r\nShe lay so several hours for the drowse deepened into a heavy sleep, and\r\nUncle Alec, still at his post, saw with growing anxiety that a feverish\r\ncolour began to burn in her cheeks, that her breathing was quick and\r\nuneven, and now and then she gave a little moan, as if in pain. Suddenly\r\nshe woke up with a start, and seeing Aunt Plenty bending over her, put\r\nout her arms like a sick child, saying wearily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, could I go to bed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe best place for you, deary. Take her right up, Alec; I've got the\r\nhot water ready, and after a nice bath, she shall have a cup of my sage\r\ntea, and be rolled up in blankets to sleep off her cold,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the\r\nold lady, cheerily, as she bustled away to give orders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you in pain, darling?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Uncle Alec, as he carried her up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy side aches when I breathe, and I feel stiff and queer; but it isn't\r\nbad, so don't be troubled, uncle,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Rose, with a little hot\r\nhand against his cheek.\r\n\r\nBut the poor doctor did look troubled, and had cause to do so, for\r\njust then Rose tried to laugh at Dolly charging into the room with a\r\nwarming-pan, but could not, for the sharp pain took her breath away and\r\nmade her cry out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPleurisy,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Aunt Plenty, from the depths of the bath-tub.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPewmonia!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Dolly, burrowing among the bedclothes with the\r\nlong-handled pan, as if bent on fishing up that treacherous disease.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, is it bad?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Phebe, nearly dropping a pail of hot water in her\r\ndismay, for she knew nothing of sickness, and Dolly's suggestion had a\r\npeculiarly dreadful sound to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x9d ordered the Doctor, in a tone that silenced all further\r\npredictions, and made everyone work with a will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMake her as comfortable as you can, and when she is in her little bed\r\nI'll come and say good-night,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, when the bath was ready and the\r\nblankets browning nicely before the fire.\r\n\r\nThen he went away to talk quite cheerfully to Aunt Peace about its being\r\n\xe2\x80\x9conly a chill\xe2\x80\x9d; after which he tramped up and down the hall, pulling his\r\nbeard and knitting his brows, sure signs of great inward perturbation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought it would be too good luck to get through the year without a\r\ndownfall. Confound my perversity! Why couldn't I take Myra's advice and\r\nkeep Rose at home. It's not fair that the poor child should suffer\r\nfor my sinful over-confidence. She shall not suffer for it! Pneumonia,\r\nindeed! I defy it,\xe2\x80\x9d and he shook his fist in the ugly face of an Indian\r\nidol that happened to be before him, as if that particularly hideous god\r\nhad some spite against his own little goddess.\r\n\r\nIn spite of his defiance his heart sunk when he saw Rose again, for\r\nthe pain was worse, and the bath and blankets, the warming-pan and\r\npiping-hot sage tea, were all in vain. For several hours there was no\r\nrest for the poor child, and all manner of gloomy forebodings haunted\r\nthe minds of those who hovered about her with faces full of the\r\ntenderest anxiety.\r\n\r\nIn the midst of the worst paroxysm Charlie came to leave a message from\r\nhis mother, and was met by Phebe coming despondently downstairs with a\r\nmustard plaster that had brought no relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat the dickens is the matter? You look as dismal as a tombstone,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, as she held up her hand to stop his lively whistling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Rose is dreadful sick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce she is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't swear, Mr. Charlie; she really is, and it's Mr. Mac's fault,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nPhebe told the sad tale in a few sharp words, for she felt at war with\r\nthe entire race of boys at that moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll give it to him, make your mind easy about that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlie,\r\nwith an ominous doubling up of his fist. \xe2\x80\x9cBut Rose isn't dangerously\r\nill, is she?\xe2\x80\x9d he added anxiously, as Aunt Plenty was seen to trot across\r\nthe upper hall, shaking a bottle violently as she went.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but she is though. The Doctor don't say much, but he don't call it\r\na 'chill' any more. It's 'pleurisy' now, and I'm so afraid it will be\r\npewmonia to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Phebe, with a despairing glance at the\r\nplaster.\r\n\r\nCharlie exploded into a stifled laugh at the new pronunciation of\r\npneumonia, to Phebe's great indignation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you have the heart to do it, and she in such horrid pain? Hark\r\nto that, and then laugh if you darst,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a tragic gesture,\r\nand her black eyes full of fire.\r\n\r\nCharlie listened and heard little moans that went to his heart and made\r\nhis face as sober as Phebe's. \xe2\x80\x9cO uncle, please stop the pain, and let me\r\nrest a minute! Don't tell the boys I wasn't brave. I try to bear it, but\r\nit's so sharp I can't help crying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeither could Charlie, when he heard the broken voice say that; but,\r\nboy-like, he wouldn't own it, and said pettishly, as he rubbed his\r\nsleeve across his eyes,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't hold that confounded thing right under my nose; the mustard makes\r\nmy eyes smart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't see how it can, when it hasn't any more strength in it than meal.\r\nThe Doctor said so, and I'm going to get some better,\xe2\x80\x9d began Phebe,\r\nnot a bit ashamed of the great tears that were bedewing the condemned\r\nplaster.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll go!\xe2\x80\x9d and Charlie was off like a shot, glad of an excuse to get out\r\nof sight for a few minutes.\r\n\r\nWhen he came back all inconvenient emotion had been disposed of, and,\r\nhaving delivered a box of the hottest mustard procurable for money, he\r\ndeparted to \xe2\x80\x9cblow up\xe2\x80\x9d Mac, that being his next duty in his opinion. He\r\ndid it so energetically and thoroughly that the poor Worm was cast into\r\nthe depths of remorseful despair, and went to bed that evening feeling\r\nthat he was an outcast from among men, and bore the mark of Cain upon\r\nhis brow.\r\n\r\nThanks to the skill of the Doctor, and the devotion of his helpers, Rose\r\ngrew easier about midnight, and all hoped that the worst was over. Phebe\r\nwas making tea by the study fire, for the Doctor had forgotten to eat\r\nand drink since Rose was ill, and Aunt Plenty insisted on his having\r\na \xe2\x80\x9cgood cordial dish of tea\xe2\x80\x9d after his exertions. A tap on the window\r\nstartled Phebe, and, looking up, she saw a face peering in. She was\r\nnot afraid, for a second look showed her that it was neither ghost nor\r\nburglar, but Mac, looking pale and wild in the wintry moonlight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome and let a fellow in,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a low tone, and when he stood in\r\nthe hall he clutched Phebe's arm, whispering gruffly, \xe2\x80\x9cHow is Rose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks be to goodness, she's better,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Phebe, with a smile that\r\nwas like broad sunshine to the poor lad's anxious heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she will be all right again to-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear no! Dolly says she's sure to have rheumatic fever, if she\r\ndon't have noo-monia!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Phebe, careful to pronounce the word\r\nrightly this time.\r\n\r\nDown went Mac's face, and remorse began to gnaw at him again as he gave\r\na great sigh and said doubtfully,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I couldn't see her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not at this time of night, when we want her to go to sleep!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMac opened his mouth to say something more, when a sneeze came upon him\r\nunawares, and a loud \xe2\x80\x9cAh rash hoo!\xe2\x80\x9d awoke the echoes of the quiet house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn't you stop it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Phebe reproachfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI dare say you've\r\nwaked her up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn't know it was coming. Just my luck!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Mac, turning to go\r\nbefore his unfortunate presence did more harm.\r\n\r\nBut a voice from the stair-head called softly, \xe2\x80\x9cMac, come up; Rose wants\r\nto see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUp he went, and found his uncle waiting for him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat brings you here at this hour, my boy?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Doctor in a\r\nwhisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharlie said it was all my fault, and if she died I'd killed her. I\r\ncouldn't sleep, so I came to see how she was, and no one knows it but\r\nSteve,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with such a troubled face and voice that the Doctor had\r\nnot the heart to blame him.\r\n\r\nBefore he could say anything more a feeble voice called \xe2\x80\x9cMac!\xe2\x80\x9d and with\r\na hasty \xe2\x80\x9cStay a minute just to please her, and then slip away, for I\r\nwant her to sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor led him into the room.\r\n\r\nThe face on the pillow looked very pale and childish, and the smile that\r\nwelcomed Mac was very faint, for Rose was spent with pain, yet could not\r\nrest till she had said a word of comfort to her cousin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew your funny sneeze, and I guessed that you came to see how I did,\r\nthough it is very late. Don't be worried, I'm better now, and it is my\r\nfault I was ill, not yours; for I needn't have been so silly as to wait\r\nin the cold just because I said I would.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMac hastened to explain, to load himself with reproaches, and to beg\r\nher not to die on any account, for Charlie's lecture had made a deep\r\nimpression on the poor boy's mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn't know there was any danger of my dying,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose looked up at\r\nhim with a solemn expression in her great eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I hope not; but people do sometimes go suddenly, you know, and I\r\ncouldn't rest till I'd asked you to forgive me,\xe2\x80\x9d faltered Mac, thinking\r\nthat Rose looked very like an angel already, with the golden hair loose\r\non the pillow, and the meekness of suffering on her little white face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't think I shall die; uncle won't let me; but if I do, remember I\r\nforgave you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him with a tender light in her eyes, and, seeing how\r\npathetic his dumb grief was, she added softly, drawing his head down, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwouldn't kiss you under the mistletoe, but I will now, for I want you to\r\nbe sure I do forgive and love you just the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat quite upset poor Mac; he could only murmur his thanks and get out\r\nof the room as fast as possible, to grope his way to the couch at the\r\nfar end of the hall, and lie there till he fell asleep, worn out with\r\ntrying not to \xe2\x80\x9cmake a baby\xe2\x80\x9d of himself.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 22--Something to do\r\n\r\nWhatever danger there might have been from the effects of that sudden\r\nchill, it was soon over, though, of course, Aunt Myra refused to\r\nbelieve it, and Dr. Alec cherished his girl with redoubled vigilance and\r\ntenderness for months afterward. Rose quite enjoyed being sick, because\r\nas soon as the pain ended the fun began, and for a week or two she led\r\nthe life of a little princess secluded in the Bower, while every one\r\nserved, amused, and watched over her in the most delightful manner. But\r\nthe doctor was called away to see an old friend, who was dangerously\r\nill, and then Rose felt like a young bird deprived of its mother's\r\nsheltering wing; especially on one afternoon when the aunts were taking\r\ntheir naps, and the house was very still within while snow fell softly\r\nwithout.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll go and hunt up Phebe, she is always nice and busy, and likes to\r\nhave me help her. If Dolly is out of the way we can make caramels and\r\nsurprise the boys when they come,\xe2\x80\x9d Rose said to herself, as she threw\r\ndown her book and felt ready for society of some sort.\r\n\r\nShe took the precaution to peep through the slide before she entered the\r\nkitchen, for Dolly allowed no messing when she was round. But the coast\r\nwas clear, and no one but Phebe appeared, sitting at the table with her\r\nhead on her arms apparently asleep. Rose was just about to wake her\r\nwith a \xe2\x80\x9cBoo!\xe2\x80\x9d when she lifted her head, dried her wet eyes with her\r\nblue apron, and fell to work with a resolute face on something she was\r\nevidently much interested in. Rose could not make out what it was,\r\nand her curiosity was greatly excited, for Phebe was writing with a\r\nsputtering pen on some bits of brown paper, apparently copying something\r\nfrom a little book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must know what the dear thing is about, and why she cried, and then\r\nset her lips tight and went to work with all her might,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Rose,\r\nforgetting all about the caramels, and, going round to the door, she\r\nentered the kitchen, saying pleasantly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPhebe, I want something to do. Can't you let me help you about\r\nanything, or shall I be in the way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear no, miss; I always love to have you round when things are\r\ntidy. What would you like to do?\xe2\x80\x9d answered Phebe, opening a drawer as\r\nif about to sweep her own affairs out of sight; but Rose stopped her,\r\nexclaiming, like a curious child,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me see! What is it? I won't tell if you'd rather not have Dolly\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm only trying to study a bit; but I'm so stupid I don't get on much,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n answered the girl reluctantly, permitting her little mistress to examine\r\nthe poor contrivances she was trying to work with.\r\n\r\nA broken slate that had blown off the roof, an inch or two of pencil, an\r\nold almanac for a reader, several bits of brown or yellow paper ironed\r\nsmoothly and sewn together for a copy-book, and the copies sundry\r\nreceipts written in Aunt Plenty's neat hand. These, with a small bottle\r\nof ink and a rusty pen, made up Phebe's outfit, and it was little wonder\r\nthat she did not \xe2\x80\x9cget on\xe2\x80\x9d in spite of the patient persistence that dried\r\nthe desponding tears and drove along the sputtering pen with a will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may laugh if you want to, Miss Rose, I know my things are queer,\r\nand that's why I hide 'em; but I don't mind since you've found me out,\r\nand I ain't a bit ashamed except of being so backward at my age,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nPhebe humbly, though her cheeks grew redder as she washed out some\r\ncrooked capitals with a tear or two not yet dried upon the slate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLaugh at you! I feel more like crying to think what a selfish girl I\r\nam, to have loads of books and things and never remember to give you\r\nsome. Why didn't you come and ask me, and not go struggling along alone\r\nin this way? It was very wrong of you, Phebe, and I'll never forgive you\r\nif you do so again,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, with one hand on Phebe's shoulder,\r\nwhile the other gently turned the leaves of the poor little copy-book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn't like to ask for anything more when you are so good to me all\r\nthe time, miss, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d began Phebe, looking up with grateful eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO you proud thing! just as if it wasn't fun to give away, and I had the\r\nbest of it. Now, see here, I've got a plan and you mustn't say no, or\r\nI shall scold. I want something to do, and I'm going to teach you all\r\nI know; it won't take long,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose laughed as she put her arm around\r\nPhebe's neck, and patted the smooth dark head with the kind little hand\r\nthat so loved to give.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be just heavenly!\xe2\x80\x9d and Phebe's face shone at the mere idea;\r\nbut fell again as she added wistfully, \xe2\x80\x9cOnly I'm afraid I ought not\r\nto let you do it, Miss Rose. It will take time, and maybe the Doctor\r\nwouldn't like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe didn't want me to study much, but he never said a word about\r\nteaching, and I don't believe he will mind a bit. Anyway, we can try it\r\ntill he comes, so pack up your things and go right to my room and we'll\r\nbegin this very day; I'd truly like to do it, and we'll have nice times,\r\nsee if we don't!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose eagerly.\r\n\r\nIt was a pretty sight to see Phebe bundle her humble outfit into her\r\napron, and spring up as if the desire of her heart had suddenly been\r\nmade a happy fact to her; it was a still prettier sight to see Rose run\r\ngaily on before, smiling like a good fairy as she beckoned to the other,\r\nsinging as she went,\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cThe way into my parlour is up a winding stair,\r\n     And many are the curious things I'll show you when you're there.\r\n     Will you, will you walk in, Phebe dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, won't I!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Phebe fervently, adding, as they entered the\r\nBower, \xe2\x80\x9cYou are the dearest spider that ever was, and I'm the happiest\r\nfly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm going to be very strict, so sit down in that chair and don't say\r\na word till school is ready to open,\xe2\x80\x9d ordered Rose, delighted with the\r\nprospect of such a useful and pleasant \xe2\x80\x9csomething to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Phebe sat demurely in her place while her new teacher laid forth\r\nbooks and slates, a pretty inkstand and a little globe; hastily tore a\r\nbit off her big sponge, sharpened pencils with more energy than skill,\r\nand when all was ready gave a prance of satisfaction that set the pupil\r\nlaughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow the school is open, and I shall hear you read, so that I may know\r\nin which class to put you, Miss Moore,\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose with great dignity,\r\nas she laid a book before her scholar, and sat down in the easy chair\r\nwith a long rule in her hand.\r\n\r\nPhebe did pretty well, only tripping now and then over a hard word, and\r\npronouncing identical \xe2\x80\x9cidentickle,\xe2\x80\x9d in a sober way that tickled Rose,\r\nthough never a smile betrayed her. The spelling lesson which followed\r\nwas rather discouraging; Phebe's ideas of geography were very vague, and\r\ngrammar was nowhere, though the pupil protested that she tried so hard\r\nto \xe2\x80\x9ctalk nice like educated folks\xe2\x80\x9d that Dolly called her \xe2\x80\x9ca stuck-up\r\npiece who didn't know her place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDolly's an old goose, so don't you mind her, for she will say 'nater,'\r\n'vittles,' and 'doos' as long as she lives, and insist that they are\r\nright. You do talk very nicely, Phebe, I've observed it, and grammar\r\nwill help you, and show you some things are right and others ain't are\r\nnot, I mean,\xe2\x80\x9d added Rose, correcting herself, and feeling that she\r\nmust mind her own parts of speech if she was to serve as an example for\r\nPhebe.\r\n\r\nWhen the arithmetic came, the little teacher was surprised to find her\r\nscholar quicker in some things than herself, for Phebe had worked away\r\nat the columns in the butcher's and baker's books till she could add so\r\nquickly and correctly that Rose was amazed, and felt that in this branch\r\nthe pupil would soon excel the teacher if she kept on at the same pace.\r\nHer praise cheered Phebe immensely, and they went bravely on, both\r\ngetting so interested that time flew unheeded till Aunt Plenty appeared,\r\nexclaiming, as she stared at the two heads bent over one slate,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless my heart, what is going on now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSchool, aunty. I'm teaching Phebe, and it's great fun!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose,\r\nlooking up with a bright face.\r\n\r\nBut Phebe's was brighter, though she added with a wistful look,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe I ought to have asked leave first; only when Miss Rose proposed\r\nthis, I was so happy I forgot to. Shall I stop, ma'am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not, child; I'm glad to see you fond of your book, and to\r\nfind Rose helping you along. My blessed mother used to sit at work with\r\nher maids about her, teaching them many a useful thing in the good old\r\nfashion that's gone by now. Only don't neglect your work, dear, or let\r\nthe books interfere with the duties.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Aunt Plenty spoke, with her kind old face beaming approvingly upon\r\nthe girls, Phebe glanced at the clock, saw that it pointed to five, knew\r\nthat Dolly would soon be down, expecting to find preparations for supper\r\nunder way, and, hastily dropping her pencil, she jumped up, saying,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, can I go?
