I'll clear up after I've done my chores.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSchool is dismissed,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, and with a grateful \xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\r\nheaps and heaps!\xe2\x80\x9d Phebe ran away singing the multiplication table as she\r\nset the tea ditto.\r\n\r\nThat was the way it began, and for a week the class of one went on\r\nwith great pleasure and profit to all concerned; for the pupil proved\r\na bright one, and came to her lessons as to a feast, while the young\r\nteacher did her best to be worthy the high opinion held of her, for\r\nPhebe firmly believed that Miss Rose knew everything in the way of\r\nlearning.\r\n\r\nOf course the lads found out what was going on, and chaffed the girls\r\nabout the \xe2\x80\x9cSeminary,\xe2\x80\x9d as they called the new enterprise; but they\r\nthought it a good thing on the whole, kindly offered to give lessons in\r\nGreek and Latin gratis, and decided among themselves that \xe2\x80\x9cRose was a\r\nlittle trump to give the Phebe-bird such a capital boost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose herself had some doubts as to how it would strike her uncle, and\r\nconcocted a wheedlesome speech which should at once convince him that\r\nit was the most useful, wholesome, and delightful plan ever devised. But\r\nshe got no chance to deliver her address, for Dr. Alec came upon her so\r\nunexpectedly that it went out of her head entirely. She was sitting on\r\nthe floor in the library, poring over a big book laid open in her lap,\r\nand knew nothing of the long-desired arrival till two large, warm hands\r\nmet under her chin and gently turned her head back, so that someone\r\ncould kiss her heartily on either cheek, while a fatherly voice said,\r\nhalf reproachfully, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy is my girl brooding over a dusty Encyclopedia\r\nwhen she ought to be running to meet the old gentleman who couldn't get\r\non another minute without her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO uncle! I'm so glad! and so sorry! Why didn't you let us know what\r\ntime you'd be here, or call out the minute you came? Haven't I been\r\nhome-sick for you? and now I'm so happy to have you back I could hug\r\nyour dear old curly head off,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, as the Encyclopedia went down\r\nwith a bang, and she up with a spring that carried her into Dr. Alec's\r\narms, to be kept there in the sort of embrace a man gives to the dearest\r\ncreature the world holds for him.\r\n\r\nPresently he was in his easy chair with Rose upon his knee smiling up\r\nin his face and talking as fast as her tongue could go, while he watched\r\nher with an expression of supreme content, as he stroked the smooth\r\nround cheek, or held the little hand in his, rejoicing to see how rosy\r\nwas the one, how plump and strong the other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you had a good time? Did you save the poor lady? Aren't you glad\r\nto be home again with your girl to torment you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, to all those questions. Now tell me what you've been at, little\r\nsinner? Aunty Plen says you want to consult me about some new and\r\nremarkable project which you have dared to start in my absence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe didn't tell you, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a word more expect that you were rather doubtful how I'd take it,\r\nand so wanted to 'fess' yourself and get round me as you always try\r\nto do, though you don't often succeed. Now, then, own up and take the\r\nconsequences.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Rose told about her school in her pretty, earnest way, dwelling\r\non Phebe's hunger for knowledge, and the delight it was to help her,\r\nadding, with a wise nod,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it helps me too, uncle, for she is so quick and eager I have to do\r\nmy best or she will get ahead of me in some things. To-day, now, she had\r\nthe word 'cotton' in a lesson and asked all about it, and I was ashamed\r\nto find I really knew so little that I could only say that it was a\r\nplant that grew down South in a kind of a pod, and was made into cloth.\r\nThat's what I was reading up when you came, and to-morrow I shall tell\r\nher all about it, and indigo too. So you see it teaches me also, and is\r\nas good as a general review of what I've learned, in a pleasanter way\r\nthan going over it alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou artful little baggage! that's the way you expect to get round me,\r\nis it? That's not studying, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, it's teaching; and please, I like it much better than having a\r\ngood time by myself. Besides, you know, I adopted Phebe and promised to\r\nbe a sister to her, so I am bound to keep my word, am I not?\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nRose, looking both anxious and resolute as she waited for her sentence.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec was evidently already won, for Rose had described the old slate\r\nand brown paper copy-book with pathetic effect, and the excellent man\r\nhad not only decided to send Phebe to school long before the story was\r\ndone, but reproached himself for forgetting his duty to one little girl\r\nin his love for another. So when Rose tried to look meek and failed\r\nutterly, he laughed and pinched her cheek, and answered in that genial\r\nway which adds such warmth and grace to any favour,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven't the slightest objection in the world. In fact, I was\r\nbeginning to think I might let you go at your books again, moderately,\r\nsince you are so well; and this is an excellent way to try your powers.\r\nPhebe is a brave, bright lass, and shall have a fair chance in the\r\nworld, if we can give it to her, so that if she ever finds her friends\r\nthey need not be ashamed of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think she has found some already,\xe2\x80\x9d began Rose eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey? what? has anyone turned up since I've been gone?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Dr. Alec\r\nquickly, for it was a firm belief in the family that Phebe would prove\r\nto be \xe2\x80\x9csomebody\xe2\x80\x9d sooner or later.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, her best friend turned up when you came home, uncle,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose\r\nwith an approving pat, adding gratefully, \xe2\x80\x9cI can't half thank you for\r\nbeing so good to my girl, but she will, because I know she is going to\r\nmake a woman to be proud of, she's so strong and true, and loving.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless your dear heart, I haven't begun to do anything yet, more shame\r\nto me! But I'm going at it now, and as soon as she gets on a bit,\r\nshe shall go to school as long as she likes. How will that do for a\r\nbeginning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be 'just heavenly,' as Phebe says, for it is the wish of her\r\nlife to 'get lots of schooling,' and she will be too happy when I tell\r\nher. May I, please? it will be so lovely to see the dear thing open her\r\nbig eyes and clap her hands at the splendid news.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one shall have a finger in this nice little pie; you shall do it all\r\nyourself, only don't go too fast, or make too many castles in the air,\r\nmy dear; for time and patience must go into this pie of ours if it is to\r\nturn out well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, uncle, only when it is opened won't 'the birds begin to sing?\xe2\x80\x9d'\r\nlaughed Rose, taking a turn about the room as a vent for the joyful\r\nemotions that made her eyes shine. All of a sudden she stopped and asked\r\nsoberly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Phebe goes to school who will do her work? I'm willing, if I can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here and I'll tell you a secret. Dolly's 'bones' are getting so\r\ntroublesome, and her dear old temper so bad, that the aunts have decided\r\nto pension her off and let her go and live with her daughter, who has\r\nmarried very well. I saw her this week, and she'd like to have her\r\nmother come, so in the spring we shall have a grand change, and get\r\na new cook and chamber-girl if any can be found to suit our honoured\r\nrelatives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, me! how can I ever get on without Phebe? Couldn't she stay, just so\r\nI could see her? I'd pay her board rather than have her go, I'm so fond\r\nof her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHow Dr. Alec laughed at that proposal, and how satisfied Rose was when\r\nhe explained that Phebe was still to be her maid, with no duties except\r\nsuch as she could easily perform between school-hours.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is a proud creature, for all her humble ways, and even from us\r\nwould not take a favour if she did not earn it somewhere. So this\r\narrangement makes it all square and comfortable, you see, and she will\r\npay for the schooling by curling these goldilocks a dozen times a day if\r\nyou let her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour plans are always so wise and kind! That's why they work so well,\r\nI suppose, and why people let you do what you like with them. I really\r\ndon't see how other girls get along without an Uncle Alec!\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nRose, with a sigh of pity for those who had missed so great a blessing.\r\n\r\nWhen Phebe was told the splendid news, she did not \xe2\x80\x9cstand on her head\r\nwith rapture,\xe2\x80\x9d as Charlie prophesied she would, but took it quietly,\r\nbecause it was such a happy thing she had no words \xe2\x80\x9cbig and beautiful\r\nenough to thank them in,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; but every hour of her day was\r\nbrightened by this granted wish, and dedicated to the service of those\r\nwho gave it.\r\n\r\nHer heart was so full of content that if overflowed in music, and the\r\nsweet voice singing all about the house gave thanks so blithely that\r\nno other words were needed. Her willing feet were never tired of taking\r\nsteps for those who had smoothed her way; her skilful hands were always\r\nbusy in some labour of love for them, and on the face fast growing in\r\ncomeliness there was an almost womanly expression of devotion, which\r\nproved how well Phebe had already learned one of life's great lessons\r\ngratitude.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 23--Peace-Making\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteve, I want you to tell me something,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose to Dandy, who was\r\nmaking faces at himself in the glass, while he waited for an answer to\r\nthe note he brought from his mother to Aunt Plenty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cP'raps I will, and p'raps I won't. What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven't Arch and Charlie quarrelled?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDare say; we fellows are always having little rows, you know. I do\r\nbelieve a sty is coming on my star-board eye,\xe2\x80\x9d and Steve affected to be\r\nabsorbed in a survey of his yellow lashes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that won't do; I want to know all about it; for I'm sure something\r\nmore serious than a 'little row' is the matter. Come, please tell me,\r\nStenie, there's a dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBotheration! you don't want me to turn telltale, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d growled\r\nSteve, pulling his top-knot, as he always did when perplexed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do,\xe2\x80\x9d was Rose's decided answer for she saw from his manner that\r\nshe was right, and determined to have the secret out of him if coaxing\r\nwould do it. \xe2\x80\x9cI don't wish you to tell things to everyone, of course,\r\nbut to me you may, and you must, because I have a right to know. You\r\nboys need somebody to look after you, and I'm going to do it, for girls\r\nare nice peacemakers, and know how to manage people. Uncle said so, and\r\nhe is never wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSteve was about to indulge in a derisive hoot at the idea of her looking\r\nafter them, but a sudden thought restrained him, and suggested a way in\r\nwhich he could satisfy Rose, and better himself at the same time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you give me if I'll tell you every bit about it?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked,\r\nwith a sudden red in his cheeks and an uneasy look in his eyes, for he\r\nwas half ashamed of the proposition.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose looked up rather surprised at his question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'd like to borrow some money. I shouldn't think of asking you, only\r\nMac never has a cent. since he's set up his old chemical shop, where\r\nhe'll blow himself to bits some day, and you and uncle will have the fun\r\nof putting him together again,\xe2\x80\x9d and Steve tried to look as if the idea\r\namused him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll lend it to you with pleasure, so tell away,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, bound to\r\nget at the secret.\r\n\r\nEvidently much relieved by the promise, Steve set his top-knot\r\ncheerfully erect again, and briefly stated the case.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs you say, it's all right to tell you, but don't let the boys know\r\nI blabbed, or Prince will take my head off. You see, Archie don't like\r\nsome of the fellows Charlie goes with, and cuts 'em. That makes Prince\r\nmad, and he holds on just to plague Arch, so they don't speak to one\r\nanother, if they can help it, and that's the row.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre those boys bad?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGuess not, only rather wild. They are older than our fellows, but\r\nthey like Prince, he's such a jolly boy; sings so well, dances jigs and\r\nbreakdowns, you know, and plays any game that's going. He beat Morse at\r\nbilliards, and that's something to brag of, for Morse thinks he knows\r\neverything. I saw the match, and it was great fun!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSteve got quite excited over the prowess of Charlie, whom he admired\r\nimmensely, and tried to imitate. Rose did not know half the danger\r\nof such gifts and tastes as Charlie's, but felt instinctively that\r\nsomething must be wrong if Archie disapproved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Prince likes any billiard-playing boy better than Archie, I don't\r\nthink much of his sense,\xe2\x80\x9d she said severely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course he doesn't; but, you see, Charlie and Arch are both as proud\r\nas they can be, and won't give in. I suppose Arch is right, but I don't\r\nblame Charlie a bit for liking to be with the others sometimes, they are\r\nsuch a jolly set,\xe2\x80\x9d and Steve shook his head morally, even while his eye\r\ntwinkled over the memory of some of the exploits of the \xe2\x80\x9cjolly set.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear me!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Rose, \xe2\x80\x9cI don't see what I can do about it, but\r\nI wish the boys would make up, for Prince can't come to any harm with\r\nArchie, he's so good and sensible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's the trouble; Arch preaches, and Prince won't stand it. He\r\ntold Arch he was a prig and a parson, and Arch told him he wasn't a\r\ngentleman. My boots! weren't they both mad, though! I thought for a\r\nminute they'd pitch into one another and have it out. Wish they had, and\r\nnot gone stalking round stiff and glum ever since. Mac and I settle our\r\nrows with a bat or so over the head, and then we are all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose couldn't help laughing as Steve sparred away at a fat sofa-pillow,\r\nto illustrate his meaning; and, having given it several scientific\r\nwhacks, he pulled down his cuffs and smiled upon her with benign pity\r\nfor her feminine ignorance of this summary way of settling a quarrel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat droll things boys are!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a mixture of admiration and\r\nperplexity in her face, which Steve accepted as a compliment to his sex.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe're a pretty clever invention, miss, and you can't get on without\r\nus,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, with his nose in the air. Then, taking a sudden plunge\r\ninto business, he added, \xe2\x80\x9cHow about that bit of money you were going to\r\nlend me? I've told, now you pay up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I will! How much do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose pulled out her purse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you spare five dollars? I want to pay a little debt of honour\r\nthat is rather pressing,\xe2\x80\x9d and Steve put on a mannish air that was\r\ncomical to see.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren't all debts honourable?\xe2\x80\x9d asked innocent Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, of course; but this is a bet I made, and it ought to be settled up\r\nat once,\xe2\x80\x9d began Steve, finding it awkward to explain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don't bet, it's not right, and I know your father wouldn't like it.\r\nPromise you won't do so again; please promise!\xe2\x80\x9d and Rose held fast the\r\nhand into which she had just put the money.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I won't. It's worried me a good deal, but I was joked into it.\r\nMuch obliged, cousin, I'm all right now,\xe2\x80\x9d and Steve departed hastily.\r\n\r\nHaving decided to be a peace-maker, Rose waited for an opportunity, and\r\nvery soon it came.\r\n\r\nShe was spending the day with Aunt Clara, who had been entertaining some\r\nyoung guests, and invited Rose to meet them, for she thought it high\r\ntime her niece conquered her bashfulness and saw a little of society.\r\nDinner was over, and everyone had gone. Aunt Clara was resting before\r\ngoing out to an evening party, and Rose was waiting for Charlie to come\r\nand take her home.\r\n\r\nShe sat alone in the elegant drawing-room, feeling particularly nice and\r\npretty, for she had her best frock on, a pair of gold bands her aunt had\r\njust given her, and a tea-rose bud in her sash, like the beautiful Miss\r\nVan Tassel, whom everyone admired. She had spread out her little skirts\r\nto the best advantage, and, leaning back in a luxurious chair, sat\r\nadmiring her own feet in new slippers with rosettes almost as big as\r\ndahlias. Presently Charlie came lounging in, looking rather sleepy and\r\nqueer, Rose thought. On seeing her, however, he roused up and said with\r\na smile that ended in a gape,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you were with mother, so I took forty winks after I got those\r\ngirls off. Now, I'm at your service, Rosamunda, whenever you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look as if your head ached. If it does, don't mind me. I'm not\r\nafraid to run home alone, it's so early,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, observing the\r\nflushed cheeks and heavy eyes of her cousin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I see myself letting you do it. Champagne always makes my\r\nheadache, but the air will set me up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you drink it, then?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rose, anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan't help it, when I'm host. Now, don't you begin to lecture; I've had\r\nenough of Archie's old-fashioned notions, and I don't want any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlie's tone was decidedly cross, and his whole manner so unlike his\r\nusual merry good-nature, that Rose felt crushed, and answered meekly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn't going to lecture, only when people like other people, they\r\ncan't bear to see them suffer pain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat brought Charlie round at once, for Rose's lips trembled a little,\r\nthough she tried to hide it by smelling the flower she pulled from her\r\nsash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm a regular bear, and I beg your pardon for being so cross, Rosy,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid in the old frank way that was so winning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you'd beg Archie's too, and be good friends again. You never\r\nwere cross when he was your chum,\xe2\x80\x9d Rose said, looking up at him as he\r\nbent toward her from the low chimney-piece, where he had been leaning\r\nhis elbows.\r\n\r\nIn an instant he stood as stiff and straight as a ramrod, and the heavy\r\neyes kindled with an angry spark as he said, in his high and mighty\r\nmanner,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou'd better not meddle with what you don't understand, cousin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I do understand, and it troubles me very much to see you so cold\r\nand stiff to one another. You always used to be together, and now you\r\nhardly speak. You are so ready to beg my pardon I don't see why you\r\ncan't beg Archie's, if you are in the wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm not!\xe2\x80\x9d this was so short and sharp that Rose started, and Charlie\r\nadded in a calmer but still very haughty tone: \xe2\x80\x9cA gentleman always begs\r\npardon when he has been rude to a lady, but one man doesn't apologize to\r\nanother man who has insulted him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my heart, what a pepperpot!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Rose, and, hoping to make him\r\nlaugh, she added slyly: \xe2\x80\x9cI was not talking about men, but boys, and one\r\nof them a Prince, who ought to set a good example to his subjects.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Charlie would not relent, and tried to turn the subject by saying\r\ngravely, as he unfastened the little gold ring from his watch-guard,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've broken my word, so I want to give this back and free you from the\r\nbargain. I'm sorry, but I think it a foolish promise, and don't intend\r\nto keep it. Choose a pair of ear-rings to suit yourself, as my forfeit.\r\nYou have a right to wear them now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I can only wear one, and that is no use, for Archie will keep his\r\nword I'm sure!\xe2\x80\x9d Rose was so mortified and grieved at this downfall\r\nof her hopes that she spoke sharply, and would not take the ring the\r\ndeserter offered her.\r\n\r\nHe shrugged his shoulders, and threw it into her lap, trying to look\r\ncool and careless, but failing entirely, for he was ashamed of himself,\r\nand out of sorts generally. Rose wanted to cry, but pride would not\r\nlet her, and, being very angry, she relieved herself by talk instead of\r\ntears. Looking pale and excited, she rose out of her chair, cast away\r\nthe ring, and said in a voice that she vainly tried to keep steady,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not at all the boy I thought you were, and I don't respect you\r\none bit. I've tried to help you be good, but you won't let me, and I\r\nshall not try any more. You talk a great deal about being a gentleman,\r\nbut you are not, for you've broken your word, and I can never trust\r\nyou again. I don't wish you to go home with me. I'd rather have Mary.\r\nGood-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd with that last dreadful blow, Rose walked out of the room, leaving\r\nCharlie as much astonished as if one of his pet pigeons had flown in his\r\nface and pecked at him. She was so seldom angry, that when her temper\r\ndid get the better of her it made a deep impression on the lads, for\r\nit was generally a righteous sort of indignation at some injustice or\r\nwrong-doing, not childish passion.\r\n\r\nHer little thunderstorm cleared off in a sob or two as she put on her\r\nthings in the entry-closet, and when she emerged she looked the brighter\r\nfor the shower. A hasty good-night to Aunt Clara now under the hands of\r\nthe hairdresser and then she crept down to find Mary the maid. But\r\nMary was out, so was the man, and Rose slipped away by the back-door,\r\nflattering herself that she had escaped the awkwardness of having\r\nCharlie for escort.\r\n\r\nThere she was mistaken, however, for the gate had hardly closed behind\r\nher when a well-known tramp was heard, and the Prince was beside her,\r\nsaying in a tone of penitent politeness that banished Rose's wrath like\r\nmagic,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou needn't speak to me if you don't choose, but I must see you safely\r\nhome, cousin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned at once, put out her hand, and answered heartily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was the cross one. Please forgive me, and let's be friends again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow that was better than a dozen sermons on the beauty of forgiveness,\r\nand did Charlie more good, for it showed him how sweet humility was, and\r\nproved that Rose practised as she preached.\r\n\r\nHe shook the hand warmly, then drew it through his arm and said, as if\r\nanxious to recover the good opinion with the loss of which he had been\r\nthreatened,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Rosy, I've put the ring back, and I'm going to try again.\r\nBut you don't know how hard it is to stand being laughed at.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do! Ariadne plagues me every time I see her, because I don't\r\nwear ear-rings after all the trouble I had getting ready for them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but her twaddle isn't half as bad as the chaffing I get. It takes\r\na deal of pluck to hold out when you are told you are tied to an apron\r\nstring, and all that sort of thing,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Charlie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you had a 'deal of pluck,' as you call it. The boys all say\r\nyou are the bravest of the seven,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I am about some things, but I cannot bear to be laughed at.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is hard, but if one is right won't that make it easier?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to me; it might to a pious parson like Arch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don't call him names! I guess he has what is called moral\r\ncourage, and you physical courage. Uncle explained the difference to\r\nme, and moral is the best, though often it doesn't look so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose\r\nthoughtfully.\r\n\r\nCharlie didn't like that, and answered quickly, \xe2\x80\x9cI don't believe he'd\r\nstand it any better than I do, if he had those fellows at him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps that's why he keeps out of their way, and wants you to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose had him there, and Charlie felt it, but would not give in just\r\nyet, though he was going fast, for somehow, in the dark he seemed to\r\nsee things clearer than in the light, and found it very easy to be\r\nconfidential when it was \xe2\x80\x9conly Rose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he was my brother, now, he'd have some right to interfere,\xe2\x80\x9d began\r\nCharlie, in an injured tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish he was!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo do I,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Charlie, and then they both laughed at his\r\ninconsistency.\r\n\r\nThe laugh did them good, and when Prince spoke again, it was in a\r\ndifferent tone pensive, not proud nor perverse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, it's hard upon me that I have no brothers and sisters. The\r\nothers are better off and needn't go abroad for chums if they don't\r\nlike. I am all alone, and I'd be thankful even for a little sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose thought that very pathetic, and, overlooking the uncomplimentary\r\nword \xe2\x80\x9ceven\xe2\x80\x9d in that last sentence, she said, with a timid sort of\r\nearnestness that conquered her cousin at once,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlay I was a little sister. I know I'm silly, but perhaps I'm better\r\nthan nothing, and I'd dearly love to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo should I! and we will, for you are not silly, my dear, but a very\r\nsensible girl, we all think, and I'm proud to have you for a sister.\r\nThere, now!\xe2\x80\x9d and Charlie looked down at the curly head bobbing along\r\nbeside him with real affection in his face.\r\n\r\nRose gave a skip of pleasure, and laid one seal-skin mitten over the\r\nother on his arm, as she said happily,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's so nice of you! Now, you needn't be lonely any more, and I'll\r\ntry to fill Archie's place till he comes back, for I know he will, as\r\nsoon as you let him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don't mind telling you that while he was my mate I never missed\r\nbrothers and sisters, or wanted anyone else; but since he cast me off,\r\nI'll be hanged if I don't feel as forlorn as old Crusoe before Friday\r\nturned up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis burst of confidence confirmed Rose in her purpose of winning\r\nCharlie's Mentor back to him, but she said no more, contented to have\r\ndone so well. They parted excellent friends, and Prince went home,\r\nwondering why \xe2\x80\x9ca fellow didn't mind saying things to a girl or woman\r\nwhich they would die before they'd own to another fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose also had some sage reflections upon the subject, and fell asleep\r\nthinking that there were a great many curious things in this world, and\r\nfeeling that she was beginning to find out some of them.\r\n\r\nNext day she trudged up the hill to see Archie, and having told him\r\nas much as she thought best about her talk with Charlie, begged him to\r\nforget and forgive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've been thinking that perhaps I ought to, though I am in the right.\r\nI'm no end fond of Charlie, and he's the best-hearted lad alive; but he\r\ncan't say No, and that will play the mischief with him, if he does not\r\ntake care,\xe2\x80\x9d said Archie in his grave, kind way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhile father was home, I was very busy with him, so Prince got into a\r\nset I don't like. They try to be fast, and think it's manly, and they\r\nflatter him, and lead him on to do all sorts of things play for money,\r\nand bet, and loaf about. I hate to have him do so, and tried to stop it,\r\nbut went to work the wrong way, so we got into a mess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is all ready to make up if you don't say much, for he owned to me\r\nhe was wrong; but I don't think he will own it to you, in words,\xe2\x80\x9d began\r\nRose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't care for that; if he'll just drop those row-dies and come back,\r\nI'll hold my tongue and not preach. I wonder if he owes those fellows\r\nmoney, and so doesn't like to break off till he can pay it. I hope not,\r\nbut don't dare to ask; though, perhaps, Steve knows, he's always after\r\nPrince, more's the pity,\xe2\x80\x9d and Archie looked anxious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think Steve does know, for he talked about debts of honour the day I\r\ngave him--\xe2\x80\x9d There Rose stopped short and turned scarlet.\r\n\r\nBut Archie ordered her to \xe2\x80\x9cfess,\xe2\x80\x9d and had the whole story in five\r\nminutes, for none dared disobey the Chief. He completed her affliction\r\nby putting a five-dollar bill into her pocket by main force, looking\r\nboth indignant and resolute as he said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever do so again; but send Steve to me, if he is afraid to go to his\r\nfather. Charlie had nothing to do with that; he wouldn't borrow a penny\r\nof a girl, don't think it. But that's the harm he does Steve, who adores\r\nhim, and tries to be like him in all things. Don't say a word; I'll make\r\nit all right, and no one shall blame you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh me! I always make trouble by trying to help, and then letting out\r\nthe wrong thing,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Rose, much depressed by her slip of the tongue.\r\n\r\nArchie comforted her with the novel remark that it was always best to\r\ntell the truth, and made her quite cheerful by promising to heal the\r\nbreach with Charlie as soon as possible.\r\n\r\nHe kept his word so well that the very next afternoon, as Rose looked\r\nout of the window, she beheld the joyful spectacle of Archie and Prince\r\ncoming up the avenue, arm-in-arm, as of old, talking away as if to make\r\nup for the unhappy silence of the past weeks.\r\n\r\nRose dropped her work, hurried to the door, and, opening it wide, stood\r\nthere smiling down upon them so happily, that the faces of the lads\r\nbrightened as they ran up the steps eager to show that all was well with\r\nthem.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere's our little peace-maker!\xe2\x80\x9d said Archie, shaking hands with vigour.\r\n\r\nBut Charlie added, with a look that made Rose very proud and happy, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nmy little sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nChapter 24--Which?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle, I have discovered what girls are made for,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose, the day\r\nafter the reconciliation of Archie and the Prince.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my dear, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Dr. Alec, who was \xe2\x80\x9cplanking the\r\ndeck,\xe2\x80\x9d as he called his daily promenade up and down the hall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo take care of boys,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rose, quite beaming with satisfaction\r\nas she spoke. \xe2\x80\x9cPhebe laughed when I told her, and said she thought girls\r\nhad better learn to take care of themselves first. But that's because\r\nshe hasn't got seven boy-cousins as I have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is right, nevertheless, Rosy, and so are you, for the two things go\r\ntogether, and in helping seven lads you are unconsciously doing much\r\nto improve one lass,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Alec, stopping to nod and smile at the\r\nbright-faced figure resting on the old bamboo chair, after a lively\r\ngame of battledore and shuttlecock, in place of a run which a storm\r\nprevented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I? I'm glad of that; but really, uncle, I do feel as if I must take\r\ncare of the boys, for they come to me in all sorts of troubles, and ask\r\nadvice, and I like it so much. Only I don't always know what to do,\r\nand I'm going to consult you privately and then surprise them with my\r\nwisdom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, my dear; what's the first worry? I see you have something on\r\nyour little mind, so come and tell uncle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose put her arm in his, and, pacing to and fro, told him all about\r\nCharlie, asking what she could do to keep him straight, and be a real\r\nsister to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you make up your mind to go and stay with Aunt Clara a month?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n asked the Doctor, when she ended.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir; but I shouldn't like it. Do you really want me to go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe best cure for Charlie is a daily dose of Rose water, or Rose and\r\nwater, or Rose and water; will you go and see that he takes it?\xe2\x80\x9d laughed\r\nDr. Alec.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that if I'm there and try to make it pleasant, he will stay at\r\nhome and keep out of mischief?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut could I make it pleasant? He would want the boys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo danger but he'd have the boys, for they swarm after you like bees\r\nafter their queen. Haven't you found that out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Plen often says they never used to be here half so much before I\r\ncame, but I never thought I made the difference, it seemed so natural to\r\nhave them round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle modesty doesn't know what a magnet she is; but she will find it\r\nout some day,\xe2\x80\x9d and the Doctor softly stroked the cheek that had grown\r\nrosy with pleasure at the thought of being so much loved. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, you see,\r\nif I move the magnet to Aunt Clara's, the lads will go there as sure as\r\niron to steel, and Charlie will be so happy at home he won't care for\r\nthese mischievous mates of his I hope,\xe2\x80\x9d added the Doctor, well knowing\r\nhow hard it was to wean a seventeen-year-old boy from his first taste of\r\nwhat is called \xe2\x80\x9cseeing life,\xe2\x80\x9d which, alas! often ends in seeing death.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll go, uncle, right away! Aunt Clara is always asking me, and will\r\nbe glad to get me. I shall have to dress and dine late, and see lots of\r\ncompany, and be very fashionable, but I'll try not to let it hurt me;\r\nand if I get in a puzzle or worried about anything I can run to you,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n answered Rose, good-will conquering timidity.\r\n\r\nSo it was decided, and without saying much about the real reason for\r\nthis visit, Rose was transplanted to Aunt Clara's, feeling that she had\r\na work to do, and very eager to do it well.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec was right about the bees, for the boys did follow their queen,\r\nand astonished Mrs. Clara by their sudden assiduity in making calls,\r\ndropping in to dinner, and getting up evening frolics. Charlie was a\r\ndevoted host, and tried to show his gratitude by being very kind to his\r\n\xe2\x80\x9clittle sister,\xe2\x80\x9d for he guessed why she came, and his heart was touched\r\nby her artless endeavours to \xe2\x80\x9chelp him be good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose often longed to be back in the old house with the simpler pleasures\r\nand more useful duties of the life there; but, having made up her mind,\r\nin spite of Phebe, that \xe2\x80\x9cgirls were made to take care of boys,\xe2\x80\x9d here\r\nmotherly little soul found much to enjoy in the new task she had\r\nundertaken.\r\n\r\nIt was a pretty sight to see the one earnest, sweet-faced girl among the\r\nflock of tall lads, trying to understand, to help and please them with\r\na patient affection that worked many a small miracle unperceived. Slang,\r\nrough manners, and careless habits were banished or bettered by the\r\npresence of a little gentlewoman; and all the manly virtues cropping up\r\nwere encouraged by the hearty admiration bestowed upon them by one whose\r\ngood opinion all valued more than they confessed; while Rose tried to\r\nimitate the good qualities she praised in them, to put away her girlish\r\nvanities and fears, to be strong and just, and frank and brave, as well\r\nas modest, kind, and beautiful.\r\n\r\nThis trial worked so well that when the month was over, Mac and Steve\r\ndemanded a visit in their turn, and Rose went, feeling that she would\r\nlike to hear grim Aunt Jane say, as Aunt Clara did at parting, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish I\r\ncould keep you all my life, dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter Mac and Steve had had their turn, Archie and Company bore her away\r\nfor some weeks; and with them she was so happy, she felt as if she would\r\nlike to stay for ever, if she could have Uncle Alec also.\r\n\r\nOf course, Aunt Myra could not be neglected, and, with secret despair,\r\nRose went to the \xe2\x80\x9cMausoleum,\xe2\x80\x9d as the boys called her gloomy abode.\r\nFortunately, she was very near home, and Dr. Alec dropped in so often\r\nthat her visit was far less dismal than she expected. Between them, they\r\nactually made Aunt Myra laugh heartily more than once; and Rose did her\r\nso much good by letting in the sunshine, singing about the silent house,\r\ncooking wholesome messes, and amusing the old lady with funny little\r\nlectures on physiology, that she forgot to take her pills and gave up\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMum's Elixir,\xe2\x80\x9d because she slept so well, after the long walks and\r\ndrives she was beguiled into taking, that she needed no narcotic.\r\n\r\nSo the winter flew rapidly away, and it was May before Rose was fairly\r\nsettled again at home. They called her the \xe2\x80\x9cMonthly Rose,\xe2\x80\x9d because\r\nshe had spent a month with each of the aunts, and left such pleasant\r\nmemories of bloom and fragrance behind her, that all wanted the family\r\nflower back again.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec rejoiced greatly over his recovered treasure; but as the time\r\ndrew near when his year of experiment ended, he had many a secret fear\r\nthat Rose might like to make her home for the next twelve month with\r\nAunt Jessie, or even Aunt Clara, for Charlie's sake. He said nothing,\r\nbut waited with much anxiety for the day when the matter should be\r\ndecided; and while he waited he did his best to finish as far as\r\npossible the task he had begun so well.\r\n\r\nRose was very happy now, being out nearly all day enjoying the beautiful\r\nawakening of the world, for spring came bright and early, as if anxious\r\nto do its part. The old horse-chestnuts budded round her windows, green\r\nthings sprung up like magic in the garden under her hands, hardy flowers\r\nbloomed as fast as they could, the birds sang blithely overhead, and\r\nevery day a chorus of pleasant voices cried, \xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, cousin,\r\nisn't it jolly weather?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo one remembered the date of the eventful conversation which resulted\r\nin the Doctor's experiment (no one but himself at least); so when the\r\naunts were invited to tea one Saturday they came quite unsuspiciously,\r\nand were all sitting together having a social chat, when Brother Alec\r\nentered with two photographs in his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember that?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, showing one to Aunt Clara, who happened\r\nto be nearest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed; it is very like her when she came. Quite her sad,\r\nunchildlike expression, and thin little face, with the big dark eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe picture was passed round, and all agreed that \xe2\x80\x9cit was very like\r\nRose a year ago.\xe2\x80\x9d This point being settled, the Doctor showed the second\r\npicture, which was received with great approbation, and pronounced a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ccharming likeness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt certainly was, and a striking contrast to the first one, for it was a\r\nblooming, smiling face, full of girlish spirit and health, with no sign\r\nof melancholy, though the soft eyes were thoughtful, and the lines about\r\nthe lips betrayed a sensitive nature.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec set both photographs on the chimneypiece, and, falling back\r\na step or two, surveyed them with infinite satisfaction for several\r\nminutes, then wheeled round, saying briefly, as he pointed to the two\r\nfaces,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTime is up; how do you think my experiment has succeeded, ladies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless me, so it is!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Aunt Plenty, dropping a stitch in her\r\nsurprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeautifully, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Aunt Peace, smiling entire approval.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe certainly has improved, but appearances are deceitful, and she had\r\nno constitution to build upon,\xe2\x80\x9d croaked Aunt Myra.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am willing to allow that, as far as mere health goes, the experiment\r\nis a success,\xe2\x80\x9d graciously observed Aunt Jane, unable to forget Rose's\r\nkindness to her Mac.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I; and I'll go farther, for I really do believe Alec has done\r\nwonders for the child; she will be a beauty in two or three years,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n added Aunt Clara, feeling that she could say nothing better than that.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always knew he would succeed, and I'm so glad you all allow it, for\r\nhe deserves more credit than you know, and more praise than he will\r\never get,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Aunt Jessie, clapping her hands with an enthusiasm that\r\ncaused Jamie's little red stocking to wave like a triumphal banner in\r\nthe air.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec made them a splendid bow, looking much gratified, and then said\r\nsoberly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you; now the question is, shall I go on? for this is only the\r\nbeginning. None of you know the hindrances I've had, the mistakes I've\r\nmade, the study I've given the case, and the anxiety I've often felt.\r\nSister Myra is right is one thing Rose is a delicate creature, quick to\r\nflourish in the sunshine, and as quick to droop without it. She has no\r\nspecial weakness, but inherits her mother's sensitive nature, and needs\r\nthe wisest, tenderest care, to keep a very ardent little soul from\r\nwearing out a finely organised little body. I think I have found the\r\nright treatment, and; with you to help me, I believe we may build up a\r\nlovely and a noble woman, who will be a pride and comfort to us all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere Dr. Alec stopped to get his breath, for he had spoken very\r\nearnestly, and his voice got a little husky over the last words. A\r\ngentle murmur from the aunts seemed to encourage him, and he went on\r\nwith an engaging smile, for the good man was slyly trying to win all the\r\nladies to vote for him when the time came.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, I don't wish to be selfish or arbitrary, because I am her\r\nguardian, and I shall leave Rose free to choose for herself. We all want\r\nher, and if she likes to make her home with any of you rather than with\r\nme, she shall do so. In fact, I encouraged her visits last winter, that\r\nshe might see what we can all offer her, and judge where she will be\r\nhappiest. Is not that the fairest way? Will you agree to abide by her\r\nchoice, as I do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we will,\xe2\x80\x9d said all the aunts, in quite a flutter of excitement at\r\nthe prospect of having Rose for a whole year.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood! she will be here directly, and then we will settle the question\r\nfor another year. A most important year, mind you, for she has got a\r\ngood start, and will blossom rapidly now if all goes well with her. So\r\nI beg of you don't undo my work, but deal very wisely and gently with\r\nmy little girl, for if any harm come to her, I think it would break my\r\nheart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he spoke, Dr. Alec turned his back abruptly and affected to be\r\nexamining the pictures again; but the aunts understood how dear the\r\nchild was to the solitary man who had loved her mother years ago, and\r\nwho now found his happiness in cherishing the little Rose who was so\r\nlike her. The good ladies nodded and sighed, and telegraphed to one\r\nanother that none of them would complain if not chosen, or ever try to\r\nrob Brother Alec of his \xe2\x80\x9cHeart's Delight,\xe2\x80\x9d as the boys called Rose.\r\n\r\nJust then a pleasant sound of happy voices came up from the garden, and\r\nsmiles broke out on all serious faces. Dr. Alec turned at once, saying,\r\nas he threw back his head, \xe2\x80\x9cThere she is; now for it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe cousins had been a-Maying, and soon came flocking in laden with the\r\nspoils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is our bonny Scotch rose with all her thorns about her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr.\r\nAlec, surveying her with unusual pride and tenderness, as she went to\r\nshow Aunt Peace her basket full of early flowers, fresh leaves, and\r\ncurious lichens.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave your clutter in the hall, boys, and sit quietly down if you\r\nchoose to stop here, for we are busy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt Plenty, shaking her\r\nfinger at the turbulent Clan, who were bubbling over with the jollity\r\nborn of spring sunshine and healthy exercise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, we choose to stay! Wouldn't miss our Saturday high tea for\r\nanything,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Chief, as he restored order among his men with a\r\nnod, a word, and an occasional shake.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is up? a court-martial?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Charlie, looking at the assembled\r\nladies with affected awe and real curiosity, for these faces betrayed\r\nthat some interesting business was afloat.\r\n\r\nDr. Alec explained in a few words, which he made as brief and calm as he\r\ncould; but the effect was exciting, nevertheless, for each of the lads\r\nbegan at once to bribe, entice, and wheedle \xe2\x80\x9cour cousin\xe2\x80\x9d to choose his\r\nhome.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou really ought to come to us for mother's sake, as a relish, you\r\nknow, for she must be perfectly satiated with boys,\xe2\x80\x9d began Archie, using\r\nthe strongest argument he could think of at the moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, do! we'll never slam, or bounce at you or call you 'fraid cat,' if you\r\nonly will,\xe2\x80\x9d besought Geordie and Will, distorting their countenances in the\r\nattempt to smile with overpowering sweetness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I'll always wash my hands 'fore I touch you, and you shall be my dolly,\r\n'cause Pokey's gone away, and I'll love you _hard_,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jamie, clinging\r\nto her with his chubby face full of affection.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrothers and sister ought to live together; especially when the brother\r\nneeds some one to make home pleasant for him,\xe2\x80\x9d added Charlie, with the\r\nwheedlesome tone and look that Rose always found so difficult to resist.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had her longest, and it's our turn now; Mac needs her more than you do,\r\nPrince, for she's 'the light of his eyes,' he says. Come, Rose, choose us,\r\nand I'll never use the musky pomade you hate again as long as I live,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nSteve, with his most killing air, as he offered this noble sacrifice.\r\n\r\nMac peered wistfully over his goggles, saying in an unusually wide-awake and\r\nearnest way, --\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo, cousin, then we can study chemistry together. My experiments don't blow\r\nup very often now, and the gases aren't at all bad when you get used to\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRose meantime had stood quite still, with the flowers dropping from her\r\nhands as her eyes went from one eager face to another, while smiles rippled\r\nover her own at the various enticements offered her. During the laugh that\r\nfollowed Mac's handsome proposition, she looked at her uncle, whose eyes\r\nwere fixed on her with an expression of love and longing that went to her\r\nheart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! yes,\xe2\x80\x9d she thought, \xe2\x80\x9che wants me most! I've often longed to give him\r\nsomething that he wished for very much, and now I can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo, when, at a sudden gesture from Aunt Peace, silence fell, Rose said\r\nslowly, with a pretty colour in her cheeks, and a beseeching look about\r\nthe room, as if asking pardon of the boys,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's very hard to choose when everybody is so fond of me; therefore I\r\nthink I'd better go to the one who seems to need me most.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, dear, the one you love the best and will be happiest with,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDr. Alec quickly, as a doleful sniff from Aunt Myra, and a murmur of \xe2\x80\x9cMy\r\nsainted Caroline,\xe2\x80\x9d made Rose pause and look that way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake time, cousin; don't be in a hurry to make up your mind, and\r\nremember, 'Codlin's your friend,'\xe2\x80\x9d added Charlie, hopeful still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't want any time! I know who I love best, who I'm happiest\r\nwith, and I choose uncle. Will he have me?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose, in a tone that\r\nproduced a sympathetic thrill among the hearers, it was so full of\r\ntender confidence and love.\r\n\r\nIf she really had any doubt, the look in Dr. Alec's face banished it\r\nwithout a word, as he opened wide his arms, and she ran into them,\r\nfeeling that home was there.\r\n\r\nNo one spoke for a minute, but there were signs of emotion among the\r\naunts, which warned the boys to bestir themselves before the water-works\r\nbegan to play. So they took hands and began to prance about uncle and\r\nniece, singing, with sudden inspiration, the nursery rhyme,\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cRing around a Rosy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course that put an end to all sentiment, and Rose emerged laughing\r\nfrom Dr. Alec's bosom, with the mark of a waistcoat button nicely\r\nimprinted on her left cheek. He saw it, and said with a merry kiss that\r\nhalf effaced it, \xe2\x80\x9cThis is my ewe lamb, and I have set my mark on her, so\r\nno one can steal her away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat tickled the boys, and they set up a shout of,\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cUncle had a little lamb!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Rose hushed the noise by slipping into the circle, and making them\r\ndance prettily like lads and lasses round a May-pole; while Phebe,\r\ncoming in with fresh water for the flowers, began to twitter, chirp, and\r\ncoo, as if all the birds of the air had come to join in the spring revel\r\nof the eight cousins.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of the Project Gutenberg EBook of Eight Cousins, by Louisa M.
